Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Usurped Slave Girl's Liability
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee of usurped slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal reasoning behind the intra-Hanafi dispute on usurper's liability when a usurped slave girl dies after return pregnant, and apply the Hanafi principle of causation (sabab) to determine liability.

Core Idea
The cause of loss (pregnancy vs birth) determines whether the usurper's return is valid and whether he remains liable.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The case involves a usurped slave girl who is returned pregnant, gives birth, and then dies. The question is whether the usurper must pay her value. The disagreement turns on identifying the precise cause of the loss: is it the pregnancy (uluq) that occurred in the usurper's hand, or the birth (wilada) that occurred in the owner's hand? This seemingly subtle distinction has significant practical implications and illustrates the Hanafi method of causal analysis in tort law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify what Abu Hanifa holds regarding the usurper's liability and the basis for his ruling.
Abu Hanifa: usurper must pay full value because the cause of loss (pregnancy) occurred in his hand.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi variant (Sahibayn)
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani and their reasoning.
Sahibayn: no liability because the return was valid and death occurred in owner's hand due to birth.
3
Analyze each side's proof
Examine the analogies and principles each side uses to support their view.
Abu Hanifa analogizes to illness; Sahibayn emphasize validity of return.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence of the disagreement in this and similar cases.
If the cause is pregnancy → usurper liable; if birth → owner bears loss.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
Check if the text indicates which view is relied upon for fatwa.
The matn favors Abu Hanifa's view, but the text does not explicitly state a tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو زَنَى بأمة غصبها فردت حاملاً، فولدت، فماتت ضَمِنَ قيمتها واحد، وهو الولادة، ومثل هذا لا يعد نقصاناً.
(فلو زَنَى بأمةٍ غصبها فردت حاملاً ، فولدت فماتت ضَمِنَ قيمتها)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما : لا يضمن لأنَّ الرَّدَّ وقع صحيحاً، وقد ماتت في يد المالك.
[١]قوله : وهو الولادة...الخ ؛ قال في «الكفاية» : أي . عند الصاحبين ه : سبب النقصان الولادة؛ لأنها أوجبت فوات جزء من ماليّة الأصل، وحدوث مالية الولد؛ لأنه وإن كان موجوداً قبل الانفصال لم يكن مالاً حتى لم يجز بيعه وهبته، وإنما صار مالاً مقصوداً بالانفصال. وعند الأعظم له سبب النقصان العلوق، ويظهر ذلك فيما إذا غصب جاريةً فحبلت . عند الغاصب، فردها فماتت بالولادة فعندها لا يضمن؛ لأنّ سبب الهلاكِ الولادة، وكان ذلك في يد المالك، ويضمن عنده؛ لأنَّ سببه العلـوق وكان عند الغاصب.
[٢] قوله : ومثل هذا لا يعد نقصاناً؛ وذلك لأنّ السبب الواحدَ لَمَّا أَثْرَ في الزيادة والنقصان كانت الزيادة خلفاً عن النقصان، كالبيع لَمَّا زَالَ المبيع عن ملك البائع ، يدخل الثمنُ في ملكه، فكان الثمن خلفاً عن مالية المبيع؛ لاتحاد السبب، حتى أنّ الشاهدين إذا شهدا على رجل يبيع شيئاً بمثل قيمته ، فقضى القاضي به، ثم رجعا لم يضمنا شيئاً.
وهذا لأنّ الفوات إلى خلف كلا فوات، وصار كما إذا غصب جارية سمينة فهزلت، ثم سمنت أو سقطت سنها ثم نبتت كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» «الأكملية».
(٣) قوله : لأن الردّ وقع صحيحاً؛ لأنه أوصل الحق إلى المستحق، وصحة الردّ يوجب البراءة عن الضمان ، فإن قيل : لا نُسَلِّمُ أنَّ الرَّدَّ صح؛ لأنها هلكت بسبب كان عند الغاصب، أجاب بقوله : وقد ماتت في يد المالك ... الخ.

بخلاف الحرة ومنافع ما غصب سكنه أو عطله يد المالك بسبب حادث في ملكه، وهو الولادة، وله : أنه لم يصح الرَّد؛ لأنَّ سبب التلف" حصل في يد الغاصب، بخلاف الحرّة؛ لأنها لا تضمن بالغصب
(٤)
ليبقى الضمان بعد فسادِ الرَّد،
[١]قوله : بسبب حادث في ملكه؛ وهو الولادة، فلا يضمن الغاصب كما إذا حميت في يد الغاصب ثم ردَّها محمومة فهلكت . عند المولى فيه، فلا ضمان عليه، وأيضاً كما زنت في يده ثمَّ ردّها فجلدت فهلكت، فلا ضمان عليه، وكمن اشترى جارية قد حبلت في يد البائع ولم يعلم المشتري الحمل فولدت عنده وماتت في نفاسها لا يرجعُ على البائع بالاتفاق. [٢]قوله : لأن سبب التلف؛ يعني العلوق حصل في يد الغاصب، فكأنه لم يردّها فهلكت عنده، وصار كما جنت في يد الغاصب جناية فقتلت بها في يد المالك، أو دفعت بها إلى وليّ الجناية يرجعُ على الغاصب بكل القيمة، كذا هذا.
بخلاف الحرَّةِ لأنها ليست بمال لا تضمن بالغصب؛ ليبقى ضمان الغصب بعد فساد الردّ، وفي فصل الشراء الواجب ابتداء التسليم، وما ذكرنا شرط صحة الردّ، والزنا سبب الجلد مؤلم لا جارح ولا متلف، فلم يوجد السبب في يد الغاصب. كذا في «الهداية». [۳]قوله : ليبقى الضمان؛ أي ضمان الغصب بعد فساد الردّ؛ أي بكونها حبلى.
If he commits zina with a slave girl he usurped and she is returned pregnant, then gives birth, then dies, he guarantees her value. One: it is the birth. And such a thing is not considered a defect. (If he commits zina with a slave girl he usurped and she is returned pregnant, then gives birth, then dies he guarantees her value.) This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], he does not guarantee because the return was valid and she died in the hand of the owner. [1] His saying: 'it is the birth'... Said in al-Kifayah: meaning according to the two companions, the cause of the defect is the birth because it necessitated the loss of part of the property of the original and the occurrence of the property of the child; because even though it existed before separation, it was not property such that its sale or gift was permissible, and it only became intended property through separation. And according to the greatest (Abu Hanifa), the cause of the defect is the pregnancy. This is evident in the case where someone usurps a slave girl and she becomes pregnant in his hand, then returns her and she dies in childbirth: according to the two, he does not guarantee because the cause of death is the birth and that was in the hand of the owner; and according to him, he guarantees because the cause is the pregnancy and it was in the hand of the usurper. [2] His saying: 'and such a thing is not considered a defect' means that because one cause affected both increase and decrease, the increase is a substitute for the decrease, like in a sale: when the sold item leaves the seller's ownership, the price enters his ownership, so the price is a substitute for the value of the sold item due to the unity of the cause; thus when two witnesses testify that a man sold something for its value and the judge rules accordingly, then they retract, they are not liable for anything. Because loss with a substitute is like no loss, just as if someone usurps a fat slave girl and she becomes thin, then becomes fat again, or her tooth falls out then grows back, as mentioned in Hashiyat al-Jalabi al-Akmaliyyah. (3) His saying: 'because the return was valid' because he delivered the right to the entitled party, and the validity of the return requires innocence from liability. If it is objected: we do not accept that the return was valid because she perished due to a cause that existed with the usurper, he responds by saying: 'and she died in the hand of the owner' etc. (etc.) [Page 158] ... Unlike the free woman and the benefits of what was usurped by living in or leaving idle, [the free woman] died in the hand of the owner due to an event in his property, which is the birth, and his [Abu Hanifa's] argument: that the return was not valid because the cause of loss occurred in the hand of the usurper. Unlike the free woman because she is not guaranteed by usurpation. ... [Commentary continues]
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the liability of a usurper when a usurped slave girl is returned pregnant, gives birth, and then dies. The base text (matn) states that the usurper guarantees the value, but the commentary clarifies that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), the usurper is not liable because the return was valid and the death occurred in the owner's possession. The essence of the disagreement lies in identifying the cause of the loss. Abu Hanifa considers the cause (sabab) of the damage to be the pregnancy (uluq) which occurred while the slave was in the usurper's hand, so the return is considered invalid. The Sahibayn consider the cause to be the birth (wilada) which happened after the return, so the return is valid. This difference has practical implications in similar cases. The commentary also compares the free woman, who is not liable to guarantee, and discusses the principle that if a cause produces both increase and decrease, the increase can be a substitute for the decrease, like the price in a sale.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful appropriation and possession of another's property without right.
رض (radd): Return of the usurped property to its owner.
علوق (uluq): Pregnancy of the slave girl; the moment the ovum is fertilized.
ولادة (wilada): Birth of the child; the physical separation from the mother.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), why is the usurper not liable in the case of the slave girl who is returned pregnant and dies in childbirth?
A) Because the pregnancy was not the cause of death
B) Because the return was valid and the death occurred in the owner's hand due to birth
C) Because the usurper did not commit zina with her
D) Because the slave girl was not property

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, the validity of the return and the extinguishment of liability depend on when the cause of loss occurred: if the cause originates in the usurper's hand, the return does not absolve him.

Ruling on Liability for Benefits of Usurped Items
Hanafi ruling on benefit liability in ghasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for benefits of usurped items and identify the exceptions for waqf and orphan property.

Core Idea
The usurper owes no rent for benefits unless the property is waqf or orphan property.

In this passage, the Hanafi school addresses a key issue in ghasb (usurpation): whether the usurper must compensate the owner for the benefits he enjoyed or wasted. The rule is clear: benefits are not guaranteed, meaning no rental payment is owed. However, there are two notable exceptions—waqf and orphan property, where the law protects those vulnerable interests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The text states that benefits of a usurped item are not guaranteed with a wage (rent), whether the usurper actually used the benefits or left the item idle.
2
Understand the scope of 'benefits'
The commentary specifies that benefits include riding, carrying loads, offspring, milk, and fruit. This defines what is not guaranteed.
3
Note the exceptions
The passage explicitly excludes waqf and orphan property, for which benefits are guaranteed (i.e., the usurper must pay rent).
4
Grasp the underlying principle
In Hanafi fiqh, benefits are not considered property (mal), so they are not compensable in ghasb. The exceptions protect vulnerable entities (waqf and orphans).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ عطف على الحرة قوله : (ومنافع ما غصب سكنه أو عطله)، فإنها غير مضمونة بأجر عندنا سواء استوفى المنافع، كما إذا سَكَنَ في الدار المغصوبة، أو عطلها.
[٤]قوله : ومنافع؛ المنافع كركوب الدابة، والحمل عليها، والزوائد للدابة واللبنُ لها، والثمرة للشجر. كذا في «نور الأنوار».
[0]قوله : فإنها غير مضمونة بأجر عندنا؛ إلا أن يكون وقفاً أو مال يتيم، فإنّ منافعهما تضمن. كذا في «الفصولين».
Then he turned to [the phrase] 'and the benefits of what was usurped by living in or leaving idle' in his saying: they are not guaranteed with a rental wage according to us, whether he used the benefits, like if he lives in the usurped house, or leaves it idle. [4] His saying: 'and the benefits': the benefits are like riding an animal, carrying loads on it, the offspring of an animal and its milk, and the fruit of a tree. [0] His saying: 'they are not guaranteed with a wage according to us' except if it is a waqf or orphan's property, then their benefits are guaranteed.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi position regarding liability for the benefits of a usurped item. The matn rules out any rental liability for the usurper, regardless of whether he actually used the benefits or merely wasted the opportunity by leaving the item idle. The commentary clarifies the scope of 'benefits' (manafi'): they include the use of an animal for riding or carrying, its offspring and milk, and the fruit of trees. The only exceptions are when the usurped property is waqf (endowment) or orphan's property, in which case the usurper must compensate for the benefits. This reflects the general Hanafi principle that the usurper is not liable for the benefits of usurped property because benefits are not considered property in themselves, except when they belong to a vulnerable entity like waqf or orphans.

Key Terms
منافع (manafi'): Benefits and usufruct of an item, such as riding, carrying, milk, offspring, fruit.
أجر (ujr): Rental payment or hire for the use of an item.
وقف (waqf): Endowed property for charitable purposes, whose benefits are guaranteed if usurped.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is a usurper liable to pay rent for using a usurped house?
A) Yes, because he used the property without permission.
B) No, except if the property is waqf or orphan's property.
C) Yes, only if he caused damage to the house.
D) No, never under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
The usurper of property is not liable for its benefits (no rent) unless the property is waqf or orphan property, in which case the benefits are guaranteed.

Ruling on Benefits of Usurped Property and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on liability for benefits of usurped property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for benefits of usurped property, explain the inter-madhhab dispute, and apply the Hanafi definition of mal to distinguish guaranteed and non-guaranteed benefits.

Core Idea
Benefits are not property unless contracted — thus, no compensation for mere use of usurped property.

This passage from the chapter on usurpation (ghasb) presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute: if someone takes another's property without right and uses it, must he pay compensation for that use? The Hanafi school, based on its definition of property (mal), holds that benefits (manafi') are not independent property and thus not guaranteed, except for waqf and orphan property. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools disagree. Through this lesson, you will trace the arguments and understand how a fundamental definitional difference yields practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: benefits of usurped property are NOT guaranteed with equivalent hire (ujr al-mithl) in the Hanafi school, except for waqf and orphan's property. Note that this applies whether the usurper used the property or left it idle.
The exception is important – it shows the school does consider benefits valuable in certain contexts.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their views
Two opposing positions are mentioned: Al-Shafi'i holds that benefits are always guaranteed (both if used and if idle). Malik holds that they are guaranteed only if the usurper actually used the property (istifa).
Malik's position draws a distinction between use (destruction) and mere idle possession.
3
Follow the opponents' proofs
Al-Shafi'i argues that benefits are valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) as evidenced by their being the subject of valid contracts like ijara. Malik argues that using benefits is a form of destruction (itlaf), which creates liability.
Each proof hinges on a different conception of what makes something 'property' or 'destruction'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rejects the opponents' premises: benefits are not valuable in themselves; they only become valuable in a contract by necessity (darura). As for Malik's view, Hanafis do not consider use as destruction of property because benefits are not property to begin with. The Hanafi proof is the precedent of Umar and Ali, who did not order compensation for benefits in the case of the deceived man (gharoor).
The silences (sukut) of the Companions is used as evidence – if it were obligatory, they would have mentioned it.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi law, a usurper does not pay rent for living in a house or riding an animal. In Shafi'i law, he must pay the equivalent rent. In Maliki law, he pays only if he actually used the property (e.g., lived in it), not if he left it idle.
This difference matters in real-world cases of property disputes and compensation claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف الحرة ومنافع ما غصب سكنه أو عطله، فإنها غير مضمونة بأجر عندنا سواء استوفى المنافع، كما إذا سَكَنَ في الدار المغصوبة، أو عطلها [١]قوله : بسبب حادث في ملكه ؛ وهو الولادة، فلا يضمن الغاصب كما إذا حميت في يد الغاصب ثم ردَّها محمومة فهلكت . عند المولى فيه، فلا ضمان عليه، وأيضاً كما زنت في يده ثمَّ ردّها فجلدت فهلكت، فلا ضمان عليه، وكمن اشترى جارية قد حبلت في يد البائع ولم يعلم المشتري الحمل فولدت عنده وماتت في نفاسها لا يرجعُ على البائع بالاتفاق. [٢]قوله : لأن سبب التلف ؛ يعني العلوق حصل في يد الغاصب، فكأنه لم يردّها فهلكت عنده، وصار كما جنت في يد الغاصب جناية فقتلت بها في يد المالك ، أو دفعت بها إلى وليّ الجناية يرجعُ على الغاصب بكل القيمة، كذا هذا. بخلاف الحرَّةِ لأنها ليست بمال لا تضمن بالغصب ؛ ليبقى ضمان الغصب بعد فساد الردّ، وفي فصل الشراء الواجب ابتداء التسليم، وما ذكرنا شرط صحة الردّ، والزنا سبب الجلد مؤلم لا جارح ولا متلف ، فلم يوجد السبب في يد الغاصب. كذا في (1) «الهداية». [۳]قوله : ليبقى الضمان ؛ أي ضمان الغصب بعد فساد الردّ؛ أي بكونها حبلى. [٤]قوله : ومنافع ؛ المنافع كركوب الدابة، والحمل عليها، والزوائد للدابة واللبنُ لها، والثمرة للشجر. كذا في «نور الأنوار». [0]قوله : فإنها غير مضمونة بأجر] عندنا ؛ إلا أن يكون وقفاً أو مال يتيم ، فإنّ منافعهما تضمن. كذا في «الفصولين». وعند الشَّافِعِي مضمونة بأجر المثل في الصورتين، وعند مالكِ إن استوفى، لا إن عطلها، وهذا بناءً على عدم تقومها عندنا، وإن لا تقومها ضروري في العقد. لنا : ما روي أن عمر وعليّاً له حكما بوجوب قيمة ولد المغرور، وحريته وردّ الجارية لعقرها على المالك، ولم يحكما بوجوب أجر منافعهما مع علمهما أنّ المستحق يطلب جميع حقه، وأن المغرور كان يستخدمها مع أولادها، ولو كان ذلك واجباً لما سكتا عن بيان ذلك ؛ لوجوبه عليهما. كذا في حاشية الجلبي» ناقلا عن «التبيين» [1] قوله : وعند الشافعي له مضمونة ؛ لأن المنافع أموال متقومة حتى تضمنَ بالعقود : كعقد الإجارة مثلاً، فكذا ينبغي أن يضمن بالغصوب، ونحن لا نسلم أنّ المنافع متقوّمة في ذاتها، بل تتقوّم ضرورة عند ورودِا العقد هاهنا. كذا ولم يوجد في «الهداية»). [٢] قوله : وعند مالك الله مضمونة ؛ أي يجب أجر المثل إن سكنها، ولا شيء عليه إن عطلها بناءً على أن المنافع تضمن بالإتلاف، والسكنى إتلاف، والتعطيلُ ليس كذلك، بل هو غصب. كذا في المعتبرات. [٣]قوله : بناء على عدم تقومها ؛ أي المنافع عندنا ، وذلك لأن صفة المالية للشيء إنما تثبت بالتموّل، والتموّل صيانة الشيء وادّخاره لوقت الحاجة، لا عن الانتفاع بالإتلاف ؛ لأنّ الأكل والشرب لا يسمّيان تموّلاً ؛ لأنّ المال اسمٌ لِمَا هو مخلوق لإقامة مصالحنا به.
Other than the free woman, and benefits of what he usurped of a dwelling or left idle, they are not guaranteed with a hire according to us, whether he used the benefits, like when he lived in the usurped house, or left it idle. [1] His saying: due to an accident in his property; namely childbirth, so the usurper is not liable, like if she became feverish in the usurper's hand then he returned her feverish and she died... [2] His saying: because the cause of destruction; meaning the pregnancy occurred in the usurper's hand, so it is as if he did not return her and she died with him... Other than the free woman because she is not property, she is not guaranteed by usurpation; so that the guarantee of usurpation remains after the invalidity of return... [3] His saying: so that the guarantee remains; meaning the guarantee of usurpation after the invalidity of return; i.e., because she is pregnant. [4] His saying: and benefits; benefits are like riding the animal, carrying loads on it, the increments of the animal, its milk, and the fruit of the tree. As stated in Nur al-Anwar. [0] His saying: they are not guaranteed with a hire according to us; except if it is a waqf or orphan's property, for their benefits are guaranteed. As stated in al-Fusulayn. And according to al-Shafi'i, they are guaranteed with the equivalent hire in both cases. And according to Malik, if he used them, not if he left them idle. And this is built on their non-valuation according to us, and their valuation is necessary in the contract. Our proof: It is narrated that Umar and Ali (may Allah be pleased with them) ruled for the obligation of the value of the child of the deceived man, his freedom, and the return of the slave-girl for her 'aqr to the owner, and they did not rule for the obligation of hire for her benefits, while knowing that the claimant demands all his rights, and that the deceived man had used her with her children. If that had been obligatory, they would not have remained silent about explaining it; because it would be obligatory on them. As stated in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Tabyin. [1] His saying: And according to al-Shafi'i, it is guaranteed; because benefits are valuable properties such that they are guaranteed by contracts: like the contract of hire for example, so similarly they should be guaranteed by usurpations. And we do not admit that benefits are valuable in themselves, but they become valuable necessarily when a contract occurs here. As stated, and it is not found in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: And according to Malik, it is guaranteed; i.e., the equivalent hire is obliged if he inhabited it, and nothing on him if he left it idle, built on the fact that benefits are guaranteed by destruction, and inhabitance is destruction, while leaving idle is not, rather it is usurpation. As stated in al-Mu'tabarat. [3] His saying: built on their non-valuation; i.e., benefits according to us, and that is because the quality of being property is only established by tamawwul, and tamawwul is preserving the thing and storing it for time of need, not for consumption by destruction; because eating and drinking are not called tamawwul; because wealth is a name for what is created to fulfill our needs.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on usurpation (ghasb) presents an inter-madhhab dispute concerning whether the benefits (manafi') of a usurped property are guaranteed by a compensation (ujr al-mithl). The Hanafi position is that benefits are not guaranteed as property because they are not considered mal (wealth) according to the Hanafi definition. The only exceptions are benefits from waqf or orphan's property, which are guaranteed. The Shafi'i school holds that benefits are valuable property and thus guaranteeable, analogizing to ijara contracts. The Maliki school distinguishes: if the usurper used the benefits, he must pay the equivalent hire; if he left the property idle, he owes nothing. The Hanafi proof is drawn from the well-known precedent of Caliphs Umar and Ali (radiyallahu anhuma) in the case of the deceived man (gharoor) who married a slave-girl under false representation of freedom. They ruled for the value of the child and return of the slave-girl, but did not order compensation for the benefits the man had enjoyed from her services. Their silence on that point, despite the necessity of explaining the full obligation, indicates that benefits are not considered property subject to guarantee. The commentary also clarifies that benefits include riding, carrying loads, milk, fruit, etc., and that the fundamental issue is the definition of mal: it must be something stored and preserved for future need, not consumed immediately. This explains why benefits, which are consumed by use, are not considered independent property unless they become the object of a contract (like ijara), where they are valued by necessity.

Key Terms
منافع (manafi'): Benefits or usufruct derived from property, such as riding a horse or living in a house.
مضمون (mamoon): Guaranteed, meaning that compensation must be paid.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property without right.
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Equivalent hire or rent for the use of property.
تقوم (taqawwum): Being considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim).
تموّل (tamawwul): Treating something as property by storing and preserving it for future need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which case are the benefits of a usurped property guaranteed with an equivalent hire?
A) Always.
B) Never.
C) Only for waqf and orphan's property.
D) Only if the usurper used the property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not guarantee benefits of usurped property because benefits are not considered independent property (mal) unless contracted; the only exceptions are waqf and orphan property.

Liability for Destroying Wine and Pig (Itlaf al-Khamr wa al-Khinzir)
Ruling on liability for destroying wine and pig

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for destroying the wine and pig of a Muslim versus a Dhimmi, and explain the underlying principle of property status.

Core Idea
Wine and pig are not property for Muslims, so destroying them incurs no liability; for a Dhimmi, they are property, so liability applies.

This lesson addresses a concise but important ruling from the chapter on usurpation (ghasb). In Hanafi fiqh, not every item that exists is considered property (mal). The ruling distinguishes between a Muslim and a Dhimmi owner because the legal recognition of ownership depends on the permissibility of the item in the owner's religion. Understanding this distinction helps clarify broader principles of property rights and liability in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two scenarios
The act is destruction (itlaf) of two specific items: wine (khamr) and pig (khinzir). The ruling depends on the owner's identity: Muslim or Dhimmi.
The text uses 'muslim' and 'dhimmi' as the contrasting owners.
2
Extract the ruling for a Muslim owner
The text implies no liability (la daman) when the items belong to a Muslim. The commentary confirms that wine and pig are not considered mal for Muslims.
The phrase 'wa in atlafahu' applies only to the Dhimmi case, indicating a contrast.
3
Extract the ruling for a Dhimmi owner
The text explicitly states 'dhamin' (liable) for the Dhimmi case. The destroyer must compensate the Dhimmi for the value of the destroyed items.
Compensation is based on the value of the items as property under Dhimmi law.
4
Understand the principle: property status depends on the owner's religion
The Hanafi school holds that certain items (wine, pig) are not considered mal for Muslims because they are prohibited and have no lawful benefit. However, for a Dhimmi, these same items are recognized as property because they are lawful in their faith.
This principle respects the Dhimmi's covenant (dhimma) and their right to own what is lawful for them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإتلاف خمر المسلم وخنزيره، وإن أتلفهما للذمي ضمن
And the destruction of the wine of a Muslim and his pig, and if he destroys them for a Dhimmi, he is liable.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is a concise ruling from the chapter on usurpation. According to Hanafi fiqh, wine and pigs are not considered property (mal) for Muslims because they are prohibited. Therefore, if someone destroys them, no compensation is due. However, for a Dhimmi (a non-Muslim under Islamic contract), these items are considered their property, so destroying them incurs liability. This difference is based on the principle that the contractual protection given to Dhimmis respects their own legal ownership of items that are prohibited for Muslims.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine or intoxicating liquor.
خنزير (khinzir): Pig.
ذمي (dhimmi): Non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state under a covenant of protection.
إتلاف (itlaf): Destruction of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Full liability because pig is property for the Dhimmi.
C) Half liability because pig is impure.
D) Liability only if the Dhimmi is a resident of an Islamic state.

Key Takeaway
The liability for destroying wine or pig hinges on the owner's religious status: no liability for Muslims, full liability for Dhimmis.

Destroying Wine and Pig: Muslim vs. Dhimmi Compensation
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on wine/pig compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation for destroying a Muslim's and a dhimmi's wine and pig, and explain the key disagreement with Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Core Idea
A Muslim's wine and pig are not valuable property; a dhimmi's are.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a classic dispute: if someone destroys wine or a pig, does compensation (daman) apply? The answer depends on whether the owner is Muslim or dhimmi. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the concept of taqawwum (legal valuation). We'll explore the ruling, the proof from the Companions, and the objections of al-Shafi'i and Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
State the Hanafi position for destroying a Muslim's wine/pig and for a dhimmi's. Note the key factor: taqawwum.
For Muslim: no compensation; for dhimmi: compensation wajib.
2
Understand the basis: taqawwum and tamawwul
Explain why wine/pig are not mal mutaqawwim for Muslims but are for dhimmis. Relate to the concept of tamawwul (storing for future need).
Benefits (manafi’) are also not independently valuable in Hanafi.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the narration of 'Umar and 'Ali in the case of the deceived buyer (al-maghroor). How does it support that benefits are not compensable?
They did not order compensation for the slave's services, implying benefits are not guaranteed in usurpation.
4
Compare with Shafi'i and Maliki views
State the opposing positions: Shafi'i (always liable for hire) and Maliki (liable only if used). Identify their proofs and the Hanafi response.
Shafi'i: benefits are property; Malik: use is destruction.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: in a scenario of usurpation of a house, what does each school require?
Hanafi: no rent; Shafi'i: rent; Maliki: rent only if occupied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإتلاف خمر المسلم وخنزيره، وإن أتلفهما للذمي ضمن. وعند الشَّافِعِي مضمونة بأجر المثل في الصورتين، وعند مالكِ إن استوفى، لا إن عطلها، وهذا بناءً على عدم تقومها عندنا، وإن لا تقومها ضروري في العقد. لنا : ما روي أن عمر وعليّاً له حكما بوجوب قيمة ولد المغرور، وحريته وردّ الجارية لعقرها على المالك، ولم يحكما بوجوب أجر منافعهما مع علمهما أنّ المستحق يطلب جميع حقه، وأن المغرور كان يستخدمها مع أولادها، ولو كان ذلك واجباً لما سكتا عن بيان ذلك ؛ لوجوبه عليهما. كذا في حاشية الجلبي ناقلا عن «التبيين». قوله : وعند الشافعي له مضمونة ؛ لأن المنافع أموال متقومة حتى تضمنَ بالعقود : كعقد الإجارة مثلاً، فكذا ينبغي أن يضمن بالغصوب، ونحن لا نسلم أنّ المنافع متقوّمة في ذاتها، بل تتقوّم ضرورة عند ورود العقد هاهنا. كذا ولم يوجد في «الهداية». قوله : وعند مالك الله مضمونة ؛ أي يجب أجر المثل إن سكنها، ولا شيء عليه إن عطلها بناءً على أن المنافع تضمن بالإتلاف، والسكنى إتلاف، والتعطيلُ ليس كذلك، بل هو غصب. كذا في المعتبرات. قوله : بناء على عدم تقومها ؛ أي المنافع عندنا ، وذلك لأن صفة المالية للشيء إنما تثبت بالتموّل، والتموّل صيانة الشيء وادّخاره لوقت الحاجة، لا عن الانتفاع بالإتلاف ؛ لأنّ الأكل والشرب لا يسمّيان تموّلاً ؛ لأنّ المال اسمٌ لِمَا هو مخلوق لإقامة مصالحنا به. قوله : خلافاً للشافعي له؛ أي في هذه الصورة أيضاً لا يضمن عنده، وله : إنه سقط تقوم الخمر والخنزير في حق المسلم، فكذا في حق الذمي؛ لأنهم أتباع لنا في حق الأحكام، فلا يجب بإتلافهما مال متقوم وهو الضمان. ولنا : إن التقوم باق في حقهم إذ الخمر لهم كالخل لنا، والخنزير لهم كالشاة لنا، ونحن أمرنا بأن نتركهم وما يدينون، والسيف موضوع، فيتعذَّر الإلزام، وإذا بقي التقومُ فقد وجد إتلاف مال مملوك متقوم فيضمنه، بخلاف الميتة والدم. كذا في «الهداية».
And the destruction of a Muslim's wine and his pig — and if he destroys them for a dhimmi, he is liable. And according to al-Shafi'i, they are guaranteed with the hire of the like in both cases, and according to Malik, if he took benefit, not if he left them idle, and this is based on their lack of valuation according to us, and their valuation is necessary in the contract. Our proof: What is narrated that 'Umar and 'Ali ruled the obligation of the value of the child of the deceived one, his freedom, and the return of the slave-girl for her defect to the owner, and they did not rule the obligation of the hire of her benefits even though they knew that the claimant demanded all his rights and that the deceived one had been using her with her children. Had that been obligatory, they would not have remained silent about clarifying that due to its obligation upon them. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Tabyin. His saying: And according to al-Shafi'i, it is guaranteed because benefits are valuable property so they are guaranteed by contracts, like the contract of lease for example, so likewise it should be guaranteed by usurpation. And we do not concede that benefits are valuable in themselves, but they become valuable out of necessity when the contract occurs. Thus it is not found in al-Hidayah. His saying: And according to Malik, it is guaranteed, meaning the hire of the like is obligatory if he occupied it, and nothing upon him if he left it idle, based on the fact that benefits are guaranteed by destruction, and occupancy is destruction, and leaving idle is not like that, rather it is usurpation. Thus in al-Mu'tabarat. His saying: Based on lack of their valuation, meaning benefits according to us, and that is because the quality of being wealth for a thing is only established by tamawwul (treating as property), and tamawwul is preserving the thing and storing it for times of need, not by using it to destruction, because eating and drinking are not called tamawwul; for property is a name for what is created to fulfill our interests. His saying: Contrary to al-Shafi'i, meaning in this image also he does not guarantee according to him. His proof: That the valuation of wine and pig has fallen in the right of a Muslim, so likewise in the right of a dhimmi because they are followers of us in the matter of rulings, so there is no obligation by destroying them of valuable property which is the guarantee. And our proof: That valuation remains in their right because wine for them is like vinegar for us, and pig for them is like sheep for us, and we are commanded to leave them and what they follow, and the sword is laid down, so compulsion is impossible. And when valuation remains, then destruction of owned valuable property has occurred, so he is liable for it, unlike the carcass and blood. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on the destruction of wine and pig owned by a Muslim versus a dhimmi (non-Muslim protected subject). The core ruling states that if someone destroys a Muslim's wine or pig, there is no compensation (ghayr mazmun), because these items are not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) for a Muslim. However, if the same items belong to a dhimmi, the destroyer is liable for compensation, because for a dhimmi, wine and pig are lawful and thus considered valuable property. The text then presents the views of other schools: al-Shafi'i holds that in both cases the usurper is liable for the hire (ujrat al-mithl) for the use of the property, treating benefits as independently valuable. Malik differentiates: if the usurper actually used the property (istifa'), compensation is due; if he merely left it idle, no compensation. The Hanafi justification is that benefits (manafi') are not independently valuable in their essence; they only acquire value through a contract (like lease) due to necessity. The proof adduced is the narration of 'Umar and 'Ali who, in the case of al-maghroor (the deceived buyer of a slave), ordered compensation for the slave's child and return of the slave for defect, but did not order compensation for the benefits (services) the slave provided while in possession, indicating that benefits are not compensable in usurpation. Further, the text argues that the valuation (taqawwum) of wine and pig for a Muslim is nonexistent, so no guarantee. For a dhimmi, their valuation remains because their religious law permits these items, and we leave them to their beliefs. The response to al-Shafi'i's view is that benefits lack the characteristic of tamawwul (storing for future need) because they are transient and cannot be preserved. The practical effect is that in Hanafi law, a usurper who uses a property without destroying it owes no rent; Shafi'i requires rent, Malik requires rent only if actually used. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is the Hanafi view as stated.

Key Terms
المنافع (al-manāfi‘): Benefits/usufruct derived from using a property without consuming its substance.
المتقوم (al-mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable such that its destruction obligates compensation.
التموّل (al-tamawwul): Treating something as property by preserving and storing it for future need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a dhimmi's wine compensable when destroyed?
A) Because wine is valuable to all people.
B) Because the dhimmi's wine is mal mutaqawwim for him.
C) Because the destroyer is always liable.
D) Because the wine is owned by a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, compensation for destroying property depends on whether the property is mal mutaqawwim for its owner; Muslim's wine/pig are not, but a dhimmi's are.

Transforming a Usurper's Worthless Property: Ownership Transfer
Explaining Hanafi ruling and rebutting Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that a usurper owns an item transformed from a worthless original using a valueless agent, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument.

Core Idea
Transforming worthless property with a valueless agent transfers ownership to the usurper because original rights never attached.

This passage addresses a classic Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute: what happens to wine turned into vinegar after being usurped? The ruling hinges on whether the original substance had legal value (taqawwum) and whether consent (rida) is required for property transfer. The Hanafi school's position is grounded in the nature of valuation and the role of consent. Let's walk through the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Case
State the two scenarios: usurping a Muslim's wine and turning it into vinegar with a valueless agent (e.g., moving from shade to sun), or usurping a dead animal hide and tanning it with valueless agent (e.g., earth and sun).
Both scenarios involve a Muslim's property that is not considered valuable (mal mutaqawwim) for the Muslim.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
According to the Hanafi school, the transformed item (vinegar or leather) becomes the property of the usurper; the original owner has no claim.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State the Shafi'i Position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the original owner retains ownership of the transformed item and can demand its return or compensation.
The text says 'khilafan li al-Shafi'i'.
4
Trace the Hanafi Proof
Understand why the original wine and dead hide have no taqawwum for the Muslim: they are not mal mutaqawwim. The transformation with a valueless agent creates new value by the usurper's action, and because the original had no value, no liability arises.
Taqlid: benefits and substances that cannot be stored lack valuation.
5
Examine the Rebuttal to the Analogy
The Shafi'i school may analogize to contracts where benefits are valued. The Hanafi response: valuation (taqawwum) requires the possibility of preservation (tamawwul), which benefits do not have. Moreover, contractual valuation depends on consent, which is absent in usurpation.
The text explicitly says 'فبطلت المقايسة' (the analogy is invalid).
6
Determine the Practical Consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Hanafi, the usurper keeps the transformed item; under Shafi'i, the original owner can reclaim it.
This affects property disputes in daily life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو غصب خمر مسلم فخلَّلَها بما لا قيمة له : كالنَّقل من الظل إلى الشمس، أو جلد ميتة فدبغَه به : أي بما لا قيمة له كالتراب والشَّمس، خلافاً للشَّافِعِي متروك على اعتقاده. ولكن باعتبار صفةِ التمول والادخار لوقت الحاجة، فالمنافع لا تبقى وقتين ؛ لأنها أعراض، كما تخرج من حيز العدمِ إلى الوجودِ تتلاشى فلا يتصوّر فيها التمول. ولئن سلّمنا أن لها حكم المال ليس لها صفة التقوم ؛ لأن التقوم لا يسبق الوجود ؛ لأنّ التقومَ إنّما يكون بعد الإحراز، ألا ترى أن الصيد والحشيش غير متقوم قبل الإحراز، وإن كانا عيناً، والإحرازُ بعد الوجود لا يتحقق فيما لا يبقى وقتين فلا يكون متقوماً. وإنما يثبت حكم التقومُ للمنفعة شرعاً عند ورودِ العقد عليها باعتبار إقامة العين مقام المنفعة للضرورة والحاجة، فبطلت المقايسة ؛ لأنّ للرضاء أثراً في إيجاب الأصول والفصول جميعاً، فالمالُ يجب بالشرط مقابلاً بغير مال، ويجوز بيع عبد قيمته ألف بألوف، وشيء من ذلك لا يثبت بالعدوان، وكل قياس لا يقوم إلا بوصف به يقعُ الفرق بين الأصل والفرع فهو باطل. كذا في «الكفاية».
And if he usurps a Muslim's wine and turns it into vinegar with something having no value: like moving from shade to sun, or a hide of a dead animal and tans it with something having no value like soil and sun, contrary to al-Shafi'i — he is left upon his belief. But by considering the quality of tamawwul (treating as property) and storing for times of need, benefits do not last two moments because they are accidents; as they emerge from non-existence into existence they dissipate, so tamawwul is inconceivable in them. And even if we concede that they have the rule of property, they do not have the quality of valuation because valuation does not precede existence; for valuation is only after acquisition. Do you not see that game and grass are not valuable before acquisition, even though they are physical things? And acquisition after existence is not realized in what does not last two moments, so it cannot be valuable. And the rule of valuation for a benefit is established by law only when a contract occurs upon it, by considering the substitution of the property for the benefit out of necessity and need. Thus the analogy fails because consent has an effect in establishing both principles and branches. Wealth is obligatory by condition in exchange for non-wealth, and it is permissible to sell a slave worth a thousand for thousands, and none of that is established by aggression. And every analogy that is not based on an attribute that distinguishes the original from the branch is invalid. Thus in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi ruling on a specific case of usurpation: if someone usurps a Muslim's wine and transforms it into vinegar using a valueless method (like exposing it to the sun, moving it from shade to sun), or usurps the hide of a dead (non-slaughtered) animal and tans it using a valueless agent (like earth and air), then according to the Hanafi school, the transformed item (vinegar or leather) becomes the property of the usurper, and the original owner loses all claim. This is contrary to al-Shafi'i, who holds that the original owner retains ownership and can claim the transformed item or its value. The text explains the reasoning: benefits (and here, the original substance) are not valuable in themselves for the Muslim because wine and dead animal hides are not mal mutaqawwim. The transformation with a valueless agent does not create a liability; rather, the usurper's action creates new value (vinegar from wine, leather from hide). The commentary delves into the nature of valuation (taqawwum): true valuation requires the possibility of preservation and storage (tamawwul), but benefits are transient and cannot be stored, so they lack taqawwum. Even if benefits were considered property, they only become valuable when a contract is made (like a lease), and that contractual valuation does not apply to usurpation because consent is absent. The argument concludes that analogizing usurpation to contracts is invalid because consent (rida) is what establishes the valuation in contracts, whereas usurpation is based on aggression. The mufta bihi position is that the usurper owns the transformed product, and the original owner has no claim.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine; an intoxicating beverage forbidden in Islam, not considered property (mal) for Muslims.
الميتة (al-maytah): A dead animal not slaughtered according to Islamic law; its hide is not purified without tanning and is not mal mutaqawwim for Muslims.
الدبغ (al-dabgh): Tanning; a process that purifies the hide of a dead animal using astringent substances, rendering it usable.
التقوم (al-taqawwum): Legal valuation; the quality of being considered valuable property, which requires the possibility of preservation (tamawwul) and consent.
التمول (al-tamawwul): The quality of being treated as property; ability to be stored and saved for future need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary reason the usurper becomes owner of the transformed item?
A) The usurper had intent to own.
B) The original substance was not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) for the Muslim.
C) The transformation was done with a valuable agent.
D) The original owner consented.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, transforming a usurped item that had no legal value using a valueless agent transfers ownership to the usurper.

8 min
Ruling on Usurper's Transformation with Valueless Agent and the Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling on transformation with valueless agent and Shafi'i dispute over dhimmi property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a usurper transforms wine into vinegar using a valueless substance and explain the Hanafi-Shafi'i disagreement regarding the value of dhimmi property.

Core Idea
Transformation with a valueless agent does not transfer ownership; the owner takes the product for free.

This passage from `Umdat al-Ri`aya addresses a key scenario in the chapter on usurpation (ghasb): when a usurper changes the form of the usurped property using something valueless, such as turning wine into vinegar by exposure to sun or tanning a dead skin with dust. The text establishes that the original owner retains ownership of the transformed item without paying the usurper. It then engages with the Shafi'i school's view that wine and pig have no value for Muslims and therefore also not for dhimmis, offering a detailed Hanafi rebuttal based on respect for dhimmi beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the Hanafi ruling for usurper transformation with a valueless agent. Note who the original owner is and that the agent is valueless.
2
Understand the basis for the ruling
The author explains that benefits (manafi') do not persist and thus cannot be considered wealth with monetary value (taqawwum) unless contracted. This justifies why the usurper's action does not create new ownership.
Benefits are accidents that dissipate quickly.
3
Analyze the analogy (qiyas) rejected
The author argues that any analogy between usurpation and valid contracts fails because consent (rida) is essential in contracts but absent in transgression.
The text says: 'فبطلت المقايسة لأن للرضاء أثراً'.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i holds that wine and pig lose value for Muslims and by extension for dhimmis. The Hanafi response: dhimmis are left to their own practices, so their property retains value.
Hadith 'السيف موضوع' is cited.
5
Extract the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference: in Hanafi, the usurper of a dhimmi's wine must pay its value if destroyed; in Shafi'i, no compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو غصب خمر مسلم فخلَّلَها بما لا قيمة له أو جلد ميتة فديغه به خلافاً للشافعي " متروك على اعتقاده. ه، فإنَّ الدّمي تبعُ المسلم، فلا تقوم في حقه، ولنا : أنه (ولو غصب خمر مسلم فخلَّلَها بما لا قيمة له) : كالنَّقل من الظل إلى الشمس"، (أو جلد ميتة فدبغَه به : أي بما لا قيمة له كالتراب والشَّمس ولكن باعتبار صفةِ التمول والادخار لوقت الحاجة، فالمنافع لا تبقى وقتين ؛ لأنها أعراض، كما تخرج من حيز العدمِ إلى الوجودِ تتلاشى فلا يتصوّر فيها التمول. ولئن سلّمنا أن لها حكم المال ليس لها صفة التقوم ؛ لأن التقوم لا يسبق الوجود ؛ لأنّ التقومَ إنّما يكون بعد الإحراز ، ألا ترى أن الصيد والحشيش غير متقوم قبل الإحراز، وإن كانا عيناً، والإحرازُ بعد الوجود لا يتحقق فيما لا يبقى وقتين فلا يكون متقوماً. وإنما يثبت حكم التقومُ للمنفعة شرعاً عند ورودِ العقد عليها باعتبار إقامة العين مقام المنفعة للضرورة والحاجة، فبطلت المقايسة ؛ لأنّ للرضاء أثراً في إيجاب الأصول والفصول جميعاً، فالمالُ يجب بالشرط مقابلاً بغير مال، ويجوز بيع عبد قيمته ألف بألوف، وشيء من ذلك لا يثبت بالعدوان، وكل قياس لا يقوم إلا بوصف به يقعُ الفرق بين الأصل والفرع فهو باطل. كذا في «الكفاية»). [١] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي له ؛ أي في هذه الصورة أيضاً لا يضمن عنده، وله : إنه سقط تقوم الخمر والخنزير في حق المسلم، فكذا في حق الذمي؛ لأنهم أتباع لنا في حق الأحكام، فلا يجب بإتلافهما مال متقوم وهو الضمان. ولنا : إن التقوم باق في حقهم إذ الخمر لهم كالخل لنا، والخنزير لهم كالشاة لنا ، ونحن أمرنا بأن نتركهم وما يدينون، والسيف موضوع، فيتعذَّر الإلزام، وإذا بقي التقومُ فقد وجد إتلاف مال مملوك متقوم فيضمنه ، بخلاف الميتة والدم. كذا في «الهداية». [٢]قوله : كالنقل من الظل إلى الشمس ؛ وبالعكس : أي النقل من الشمس إلى الظل. كما صرح به صاحب الهداية. [١]قوله : أخذها المالك ؛ بلا شيء : أي لا يلزم للمالك أن يدفع شيئاً إلى الغاصب ؛ لأن التخليل تطهير للخمر، فلا يضاف إليه المالية، والتقومُ والدباغة إظهار للمالية والتقوم، فصار كغسل الثوب النجس ، فلَمَّا أنّ غسل الثوب المغصوب النجس لا يزيل ملك المالك، فكذا هذا. [٢) قوله : ولو أتلفهما ؛ أي لو أتلف الغاصب الخـل الـذي صـيـره مـن الخـمـر المغصوب والجلد المدبوغ الذي دبغَه بعد الغصب، ضَمِنَ مثل الخل ؛ لأنه أتلف مالاً متقوماً خالصاً للمالك مثليّاً، وقيمة الجلد ظاهراً غير مدبوغ في رواية ؛ لأنه المحصل لوصف الدباغة، فلا يلزم عليه ضمان أثره. وأكثر الفقهاء على أنه يضمن قيمته مدبوغاً ؛ لأنَّ صفة الدباغة تابعة للمدبوغ ، فإذا كان الأصل مضموناً فلا بد أن يستتبع وصفه. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي) [٣] قوله : وعندهما أخذها ؛ أي الخلّ المالك، وأعطى ما زاد الملح فيه بمنزلة دبغ الجلد. قال صاحب «الهداية»: ومعناه هاهنا أن يعطي مثل وزن الملح من الخل، هذا إذا لم يوجد الملح بعينه.
And if he usurps the wine of a Muslim and turns it into vinegar with something of no value, or [usurps] the skin of a dead animal and tans it with it, contrary to al-Shafi'i: "[It is] left upon his belief." So indeed the dhimmi is a follower of the Muslim, so it is not valuable in his right. And for us: that (And if he usurps the wine of a Muslim and turns it into vinegar with something of no value): like moving from shade to sun, (or the skin of a dead animal and tans it with it: i.e., with something of no value like dust and sun, but considering the attribute of wealth and storing for time of need. So benefits do not last two moments because they are accidents; as they emerge from non-existence into existence they dissipate, so wealth cannot be imagined in them. And even if we concede that they have the ruling of wealth, they do not have the attribute of monetary value because monetary value does not precede existence; indeed monetary value comes only after acquisition. Do you not see that game and grass are not valuable before acquisition, even though they are physical objects? And acquisition after existence is not realized in what does not last two moments, so it is not valuable. And the ruling of monetary value for benefit is only established legally when a contract is made upon it, based on substituting the object for the benefit due to necessity and need. So the analogy is invalid because consent has an effect in establishing principles and particulars all together. So wealth becomes obligatory by condition in exchange for other than wealth, and it is permissible to sell a slave worth a thousand for thousands, and nothing of that is established by transgression. And every analogy that does not stand except by a description through which a difference occurs between the original and the branch is invalid. Thus in al-Kifayah. [1] His saying: "contrary to al-Shafi'i — may Allah have mercy on him — i.e., in this scenario also he does not hold liability. And for him: that the value of wine and pig has fallen in the right of the Muslim, so similarly in the right of the dhimmi, because they are followers of us in the matter of rulings. So by destroying them, no valuable property — which is compensation — is obligatory. And for us: that value remains in their right, for wine is to them like vinegar to us, and pig is to them like sheep to us. And we are commanded to leave them and what they practice, and the sword is laid down, so compulsion is impossible. And when value remains, destruction of owned valuable property has occurred, so he must compensate, unlike the dead animal and blood. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: "like moving from shade to sun" and vice versa: i.e., moving from sun to shade, as al-Hidayah's author clarified. [1] His saying: "the owner takes it for free": i.e., the owner is not required to pay anything to the usurper, because turning into vinegar is a purification of wine, so no financial value is attached to it, and tanning is a manifestation of financial value and value. So it is like washing a filthy garment. Since washing a usurped filthy garment does not remove the owner's ownership, so too this. [2] His saying: "and if he destroys them": i.e., if the usurper destroys the vinegar he made from the usurped wine and the tanned leather he tanned after usurpation, he is liable for the like of the vinegar because he destroyed valuable property purely owned by the owner, being fungible, and the apparent value of the leather undressed in one narration because it is what achieves the attribute of tanning, so he is not liable for the compensation of its effect. And most jurists hold that he is liable for its value tanned, because the attribute of tanning follows the tanned object, and if the original is liable, its attribute must necessarily be included. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] His saying: "and according to them, he takes it": i.e., the owner takes the vinegar, and gives the increase of salt in it, as an analogy to tanning the skin. The author of al-Hidayah said: Its meaning here is that he gives the equivalent weight of salt from the vinegar, this is if the salt itself is not found.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling from Al-Wiqaya regarding a usurper (ghasib) who transforms usurped property using something of no value. The author first states the ruling: if someone usurps a Muslim's wine and turns it into vinegar using a valueless agent (like exposure to sun), or usurps the skin of a dead animal and tans it using something valueless (like dust or sun), then the original owner takes the transformed item without paying anything. This is contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that wine and pig become valueless for a Muslim, so by extension they are also valueless for a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject). The Hanafi response: value is relative to the dhimmi's belief; wine and pig are lawful property for them, so destruction of their property requires compensation. The commentary then provides detailed reasoning: benefits (manafi) are accidents that do not last, so they cannot be considered wealth with monetary value (taqawwum) until they are actualized and acquired. Benefits only gain legal value when a contract is made upon them. Therefore, using a valueless agent does not create value; it merely purifies (takhlih) or manifests (dibagh) the existing substance. Analogies to sale of a slave at a price are invalid because consent (rida) is present in contracts but absent in usurpation (udwan). The passage then explains the intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Amir al-Mu'minin Abu Hanifa, if the usurper uses something of value (like salt or alum), the transformed product belongs to the usurper and he owes nothing, because the increase is attached to his own property. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the owner may take the vinegar and pay the value of the salt added. The commentary also discusses liability if the usurper later destroys the transformed item: he must pay the like of the vinegar (since it is a fungible), and for the tanned leather, there are two views: either the value in its untanned state (since the usurper's act of tanning is not compensated) or its value tanned (since the attribute of tanning follows the object). The majority hold the latter. The text then concludes with the meaning of 'giving the increase of salt' in al-Hidayah: the usurper gives the equivalent weight of salt from the vinegar.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Taking another's property wrongfully and forcefully.
التخليل (al-takhlil): The process of turning wine into vinegar by contact with air or adding a substance.
الدبغ (al-dabgh): Tanning or curing a hide to make it usable, often using astringent substances.
التقوم (al-taqawwum): The attribute of being legally recognized as valuable and subject to monetary compensation.
خلافاً للشافعي (khilafan li al-Shafi'i): Contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i's position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a usurper turns a Muslim's wine into vinegar by moving it from shade to sun (valueless), what must the usurper give to the owner to obtain the vinegar?
A) Nothing, because the owner takes the vinegar for free.
B) The value of the vinegar.
C) Compensation for the labor.
D) The vinegar must be destroyed.

Key Takeaway
A usurper who transforms property using a valueless agent does not gain ownership; the owner takes the transformed item for free.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Usurper Using Valuable Substance and Liability for Destruction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on valuable substance and liability for destruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi disagreement when a usurper uses a valuable substance to transform property and determine the liability if the transformed item is later destroyed.

Core Idea
Using a valuable substance creates a dispute: Abu Hanifa gives the product to the usurper; the Sahibayn give the owner the option and require payment for the increase.

This passage continues the commentary on usurpation by addressing the more complex case where the usurper adds something of value (such as salt or alum) to transform the property. The text presents the disagreement between the founding imams: Abu Hanifa holds that the usurper owns the transformed product because the valuable substance is his property, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the owner may claim the product but must pay for the increase. The passage also discusses what happens if the usurper later destroys the vinegar or tanned leather, ruling that compensation must be paid to the owner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
The usurper uses a valuable substance (e.g., salt) to turn wine to vinegar or tan skin. Abu Hanifa: usurper owns product, owes nothing. Sahibayn: owner can take product and pay increase.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each position
Abu Hanifa sees the increase as attached to the usurper's property. Sahibayn emphasize that the product still belongs to the owner, but the owner must compensate for the added value.
3
Analyze the ruling if the transformed item is destroyed
If the usurper destroys the vinegar, he must pay its like (misl) because it's a fungible. For tanned leather, two views: pay value undressed or tanned; majority hold tanned value.
The majority view is that the attribute of tanning follows the object.
4
Apply the principle from al-Hidayah on giving the increase
The owner gives the equivalent weight of salt from the vinegar if the salt is not returned.
5
Extract the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference affects who owns the product and who bears the risk.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling from Al-Wiqaya regarding a usurper (ghasib) who transforms usurped property using something of no value. The author first states the ruling: if someone usurps a Muslim's wine and turns it into vinegar using a valueless agent (like exposure to sun), or usurps the skin of a dead animal and tans it using something valueless (like dust or sun), then the original owner takes the transformed item without paying anything. The Hanafi response: value is relative to the dhimmi's belief; wine and pig are lawful property for them, so destruction of their property requires compensation. The commentary then provides detailed reasoning: benefits (manafi) are accidents that do not last, so they cannot be considered wealth with monetary value (taqawwum) until they are actualized and acquired. Benefits only gain legal value when a contract is made upon them. Therefore, using a valueless agent does not create value; it merely purifies (takhlih) or manifests (dibagh) the existing substance. Analogies to sale of a slave at a price are invalid because consent (rida) is present in contracts but absent in usurpation (udwan). The passage then explains the intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Amir al-Mu'minin Abu Hanifa, if the usurper uses something of value (like salt or alum), the transformed product belongs to the usurper and he owes nothing, because the increase is attached to his own property. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the owner may take the vinegar and pay the value of the salt added. The commentary also discusses liability if the usurper later destroys the transformed item: he must pay the like of the vinegar (since it is a fungible), and for the tanned leather, there are two views: either the value in its untanned state (since the usurper's act of tanning is not compensated) or its value tanned (since the attribute of tanning follows the object). The majority hold the latter. The text then concludes with the meaning of 'giving the increase of salt' in al-Hidayah: the usurper gives the equivalent weight of salt from the vinegar.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Taking another's property wrongfully and forcefully.
التخليل (al-takhlil): The process of turning wine into vinegar by contact with air or adding a substance.
الدبغ (al-dabgh): Tanning or curing a hide to make it usable, often using astringent substances.
التقوم (al-taqawwum): The attribute of being legally recognized as valuable and subject to monetary compensation.
خلافاً للشافعي (khilafan li al-Shafi'i): Contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i's position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if a usurper uses salt to turn wine into vinegar, what are the owner's rights to the vinegar?
A) The owner must leave it to the usurper.
B) The owner may take the vinegar and must pay the value of the salt.
C) The owner may take the vinegar for free.
D) The owner and usurper share the vinegar equally.

Key Takeaway
When a usurper uses a valuable substance to transform property, Abu Hanifa gives ownership to the usurper, while the Sahibayn give the owner the right to reclaim it with payment for the increase.

Transforming Usurped Property: Vinegar and Tanned Skin
Commentary on the matn regarding ownership and liability when usurped property is transformed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) on ownership and liability when usurped wine is made into vinegar or a usurped skin is tanned with a valuable substance, and apply the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
Transformation with valuable property can transfer ownership to the usurper, but the skin remains the owner's.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah tackles a subtle fiqh issue: what happens when a person wrongfully takes someone else's wine or skin and then processes it? Does the owner get back the transformed item? Does the usurper gain ownership? The Hanafi masters differ on these questions, and the ruling depends on whether the transforming agent is valuable. We will unpack Abu Hanifa's position and the alternative view of the Sahibayn, then see which is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What are the two acts discussed? Making vinegar from wine and tanning skin. For each, the matn gives a ruling based on whether the substance used is valuable or valueless.
The acts themselves are permissible; the issue is property rights.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position for valuable substance
Abu Hanifa rules that when a valuable substance (e.g., salt) is used to make vinegar, the usurper owns the vinegar and owes nothing. For tanning with a valuable substance, the owner takes the tanned skin but returns the increase in value; if destroyed, no liability.
Why the difference? Wine is completely transformed; skin remains.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that in both cases, the owner takes the transformed object and compensates the usurper for the increase due to the valuable substance. If destroyed, the usurper is liable for the tanned value.
They emphasize compensating the usurper's property.
4
Analyze the reasoning (qiyas)
The Hanafi argument uses qiyas to washing a usurped najis garment: washing does not transfer ownership. Similarly, making vinegar with valueless substance does not transfer. But with valuable substance, the usurper's property preponderates.
The Sahibayn reject this analogy when valuable property is mixed.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah prefers Abu Hanifa's view. The mufta bihi is that the usurper owns the vinegar made with valuables but must return the increase for tanning, with a right of detention.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text

أخذهما المالك بلا شيء، ولو أتلفهُما ضَمِنَ، ولو خللها بذي قيمةٍ ملكه ولا شيء عليه فلو دبغ به الجلد

الظل. كما صرح به صاحب الهداية.

[٢) قوله : ولو أتلفهما؛ أي لو أتلف الغاصب الخـل الـذي صـيـره مـن الخـمـر المغصوب والجلد المدبوغ الذي دبغَه بعد الغصب، ضَمِنَ مثل الخل؛ لأنه أتلف مالاً متقوماً خالصاً للمالك مثليّاً، وقيمة الجلد ظاهراً غير مدبوغ في رواية؛ لأنه المحصل لوصف الدباغة، فلا يلزم عليه ضمان أثره. وأكثر الفقهاء على أنه يضمن قيمته مدبوغاً؛ لأنَّ صفة الدباغة تابعة للمدبوغ، فإذا كان الأصل مضموناً فلا بد أن يستتبع وصفه. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي) [٣] قوله : وعندهما أخذها؛ أي الخلّ المالك، وأعطى ما زاد الملح فيه بمنزلة دبغ الجلد.
قال صاحب «الهداية»: ومعناه هاهنا أن يعطي مثل وزن الملح من الخل، هذا إذا أخذَهُ المالك وردّ ما زادَ الدِّبغ فيه، ولو أتلفه لا يضمن (أخذه المالك وردّ ما زادَ الدِّبغ فيه، ولو أتلفه لا يضمن)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يضمن الجلد مدبوغاً، ويعطيه المالك ما زاد الدباغ فيه. فالحاصل أنه إذا خلل أو دبغ بما لا قيمة له أخذهما المالك؛ لأن الأصل حقه، وليس من الغاصب سوى العمل، ولا قيمة له، أما إذا خلَّلَ أو دبغ بذي قيمة يصير ملكاً للغاصب ترجيحاً للمال المتقوم على غير المتقوّم، والفرق لأبي حنيفة بين الخل والجلد : إنَّ المالك يأخذ الجلد، ولا يأخذ الخل؛ لأنَّ الجلد باق لكن أزال عنه النجاسات والخمر غير باق، بل صارت حقيقة أخرى، وإنما لا يضمن الجلد عند أبي حنيفة له إذا أتلفه؛ لأنَّه غَصَبَ جلداً غير مدبوغ خللها بإلقاء الملح، أما لو خللها بإلقاء الخلّ فيها فعن محمد ه أنه إن صار خلا من ساعة يصير ملكاً للغاصب، ولا شيء عليه؛ لأنه استهلالاً له، وهو غير متقوم، وإن لم تصر خلاً إلا بعد ضمان بأن كان الملقى فيه خلاً قليلاً فهو بينهما على قدر كيلهما؛ لأنه خلط الخلّ بالخل في التقدير، وهو على أصله ليس باستهلاك. وعند الأعظم له هو للغاصب في الوجهين، ولا شيء عليه؛ لأنَّ نفس الخلط استهلاك عنده، ولا ضمانَ في الاستهلاك؛ لأنه أتلف ملك نفسه، وزيادة التوضيح في «الهداية».
[١] قوله : ورد ما زاد الدبغ؛ وبيانه أن ينظر إلى قيمته ذكياً غير مدبوغ، وإلى قيمته مدبوغاً، فيضمن فضل ما بينهما، وللغاصب أن يحبسه حتى يستوفي حقه لحق الحبس في المبيع. كذا في «الهداية».
[٢] قوله : لأنه غصب جلداً...الخ؛ تفصيل دليله على ما قاله أخي جلبي إن ماليته وتقومه حصل بفعل الغاصب، وفعله متقوم لاستعماله إلا متقوماً فيه؛ ولهذا كان له أن يحبسه حتى يستوفي ما زادَ الدباغ فيه، فكان حقاً له، والجلد تبعاً لصنعته في
Book of Usurpation / Its Definition and Rulings. The owner takes them for nothing, and if he destroys them he is liable, and if he makes them vinegar with something of value, he owns it and nothing is upon him, then if he tans the skin with it. (The owner takes them for nothing, and if he destroys them he is liable, and if he makes them vinegar with something of value): such as salt and vinegar, (he owns it and nothing is upon him, this is according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two, the owner takes it and gives the increase of the salt. Then if he tans the skin with it): i.e., with something of value like qaraz and 'afas. With clarification as stated by the author of Al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'The owner takes them for nothing' means it is not necessary for the owner to pay anything to the usurper, because making vinegar is a purification of wine, so no monetary value is attached to it, and tanning is a manifestation of value and worth, so it becomes like washing a najis garment. Since washing a usurped najis garment does not remove the owner's ownership, likewise this. [2] His statement: 'And if he destroys them' means if the usurper destroys the vinegar he made from the usurped wine and the tanned skin he tanned after usurpation, he is liable for the like of the vinegar because he destroyed pure valuable property belonging to the owner which is fungible, and the value of the skin is apparently undressed in one narration because it is the one that attained the quality of tanning, so he is not liable for the effect of his action. Most jurists hold that he is liable for its value tanned because the quality of tanning is subsidiary to the tanned object, so if the principal is guaranteed, its quality must follow. As stated in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] His statement: 'And according to the two, the owner takes it' i.e., the vinegar, and gives the increase due to the salt, resembling the tanning of the skin. The author of Al-Hidayah said: Its meaning here is that he gives the like of the weight of salt from the vinegar. This is when the owner takes it and returns the increase due to the tanning, and if he destroys it, he is not liable. This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two, he is liable for the skin tanned, and its owner gives him the increase due to tanning. The conclusion is that if he makes vinegar or tans with something of no value, the owner takes both, because the original is his right, and from the usurper there is only action, which has no value. But if he makes vinegar or tans with something of value, it becomes the property of the usurper, giving preference to valuable property over non-valuable. The difference according to Abu Hanifa between vinegar and skin is that the owner takes the skin and does not take the vinegar, because the skin remains but he removed impurities, while the wine does not remain but has become another substance. And he does not guarantee the skin according to Abu Hanifa if he destroys it, because he usurped undressed skin and made it vinegar by throwing salt. But if he made it vinegar by throwing vinegar into it, according to Muhammad if it becomes vinegar instantly, it becomes the property of the usurper and nothing is upon him, because it is destruction of it, and it is not valuable. And if it does not become vinegar except after guarantee, such that what was thrown into it was a little vinegar, then it is between them according to their measure, because it is mixing vinegar with vinegar in estimation, and according to his principle it is not destruction. According to the Greatest (Abu Hanifa), it is for the usurper in both cases, and nothing upon him, because the mixing itself is destruction according to him, and there is no liability for destruction, because he destroyed his own property. Further clarification is in Al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'And returns the increase due to tanning'; its explanation is that one looks at its value as undressed dead animal and its value as tanned, and he guarantees the difference between them, and the usurper may detain it until he receives his right, due to the right of detention in a sale. As stated in Al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'Because he usurped skin...' etc.; detailed proof according to what Akhi Jalabi said: Its value and worth came about by the action of the usurper, and his action is valuable except that it is used for something valuable; hence he has the right to detain it until he receives the increase due to tanning, so it became his right, and the skin is subsidiary to his craftsmanship.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the rulings on usurped property that has been transformed through processing, specifically making vinegar from wine and tanning skin. The passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute, as it presents the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The matn states that if someone usurps wine and makes it vinegar with a valueless substance (like water or something without market value), the owner takes the vinegar for free. If the usurper destroys the vinegar, he is liable for its like. If he makes it vinegar with a valuable substance (like salt or vinegar), he owns the vinegar and owes nothing. Similarly for tanning: if he tans the skin with a valueless substance, the owner takes it; if with a valuable substance, the usurper owns it. The commentary explains the reasoning: making vinegar is a purification that destroys the original wine, so no value is added to the usurper's action; tanning shows the value of the skin. The Sahibayn differ: they hold that when a valuable substance is used, the owner takes the transformed object but must compensate the usurper for the increase in value due to that substance. For tanning, according to Abu Hanifa, the owner takes the tanned skin and returns the increase in value from tanning, and if he destroys it he is not liable. The Sahibayn say he is liable for the tanned value but the owner gives the increase. The commentary then summarizes: when the processing is with a valueless substance, the owner takes; with a valuable one, the usurper acquires ownership due to the preponderance of valuable property. The difference in Abu Hanifa's view between vinegar and skin is that the skin remains as a substance while the wine is completely transformed. Further details discuss the case of mixing wine with a little vinegar to make it vinegar, where there is a dispute between Muhammad and Abu Hanifa. The passage provides a thorough exploration of how Hanafi fiqh balances property rights and value.

Key Terms
التخليل (al-takhleel): The process of turning wine into vinegar by adding a substance or allowing it to sour naturally.
الدباغ (al-dibagh): Tanning of animal skin using substances like qaraz or 'afas to make it suitable for use.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Wrongful taking of another's property without permission; the usurper is liable for damages.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Property that has legal value and is permissible to benefit from in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position (Abu Hanifa), if someone usurps wine and makes it vinegar by adding salt, who owns the vinegar?
A) The original owner, because the wine was his property.
B) The usurper, because he owns the vinegar and owes nothing.
C) Both, in shares according to the value of the wine and salt.
D) The state, because it is a transformed najis substance.

Key Takeaway
When usurped property is transformed using a valuable substance, Abu Hanifa distinguishes between wine (completely transformed, so usurper owns) and skin (remains, so owner takes but must compensate for increase).

Tanning Leather by a Usurper: Ownership and Guarantee in Hanafi Fiqh
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tanning leather

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding ownership and guarantee when a usurper tans leather, and identify the key factor (value of tanning material) that determines the ruling according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
If a usurper tans leather with valueless materials, the owner retains it; if with valuable materials, the usurper owns it.

Imagine someone takes another's leather hide without permission, then tans it using salt or other materials. Who owns the tanned leather? And if the usurper later destroys it, must he compensate? The Hanafi school has a nuanced answer depending on the value of the tanning agent. In this lesson, you will explore the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two leading students on this issue, and learn the reasoning behind the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal question
The act is usurpation (ghasb) of an untanned leather hide followed by tanning (dibgh). The legal question: who owns the tanned leather and does the usurper guarantee it if destroyed?
2
Recognize the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that the owner takes the leather and returns the increase; no guarantee if destroyed. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold the usurper guarantees the tanned leather and the owner pays the increase.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Analyze the underlying principle: value of added material
Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on whether the tanning material has monetary value (taqawwum). If valueless (e.g., salt), only labor remains, which is not compensable, so owner retains. If valuable (e.g., alum or minerals), the valuable substance gives ownership priority to the usurper.
The key is the shariah value of substances used.
4
Determine the consequence of destruction
Under Abu Hanifa, if the usurper destroys the tanned leather that was tanned with valueless material, he does not guarantee because his act (tanning) is not compensable and the original leather remains the owner's property. Under Sahibayn, he guarantees the tanned value.
Practical difference: guarantee vs. no guarantee.
5
Apply the ruling to concrete scenarios
Scenario A: Usurper tans leather with salt (valueless) → owner takes leather, usurper gets nothing if destroyed. Scenario B: Usurper tans with valuable alum → usurper owns leather, must compensate owner for the original hide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
