Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling when a usurper tans leather or makes vinegar from wine. The matn states that the owner takes the leather and returns the increase in value from tanning, and if the usurper destroys it, he is not liable. Abu Hanifa holds this view. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the usurper guarantees the leather in its tanned state, and the owner gives him the increase. The author summarizes: if the usurper uses valueless materials (like salt or sun), the owner takes the leather; if he uses valuable materials, the usurper becomes owner, prioritizing the valuable property over the non-valuable. The difference between vinegar and leather: leather remains the same substance but purified, while wine becomes a new substance. Abu Hanifa also rules that if the usurper mixes a little vinegar into wine and it becomes vinegar, it is shared proportionally, but the strongest position (al-A'zam, Abu Hanifa) is that the usurper owns all because mixing is consumption. The commentary then expands on the proof: the increase in value is due to the usurper's act, which is valuable, so he has the right to detain the item until paid. An objection is raised: if value follows guarantee, then untanned leather has no value yet must be returned. The answer: return follows ownership, not value.

Key Terms
الدبغ (al-dibgh): Tanning of leather; the process of adding substances (like salt, alum) to remove impurities and make leather usable.
الخلل (al-takhlil): The process of turning wine into vinegar by mixing or leaving it, which changes its legal status from prohibited to permissible.
تقوم (taqawwum): Having monetary value according to shariah; being considered wealth (mal) that is compensable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a usurper tans a leather hide using salt (which has no value), who owns the tanned leather?
A) The owner
B) The usurper
C) Both share equally
D) It belongs to the state

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ownership of property improved by an unauthorized act depends on whether the added materials have shariah value; only valuable additions can transfer ownership to the usurper.

Making Vinegar from Usurped Wine: The Hanafi Dispute on Ownership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on vinegar-making from wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between immediate and gradual transformation (takhlil) in determining ownership of vinegar made from usurped wine, and compare Abu Hanifa's view with that of Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
When usurped wine becomes vinegar through the addition of valuable vinegar, ownership vests in the usurper; if the addition is valueless, the owner's right persists.

A more complex scenario arises when a usurper takes someone else's wine and turns it into vinegar. Since wine itself is not valuable property (because it is haram), the transformation creates a new permissible substance. Who owns the vinegar? The Hanafi imams differ based on whether the vinegar-making agent is valuable and whether the transformation is immediate or gradual. This lesson unpacks the detailed positions of Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base: wine is non-valuable
Wine (khamr) is not considered valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) in Hanafi fiqh because it is haram. The usurper's act of making vinegar (takhlil) transforms it into a valuable substance.
This changes the legal analysis compared to leather, which was already valuable.
2
Identify the two methods of takhlil
The passage discusses two ways: adding a valueless substance (e.g., salt or leaving it in the sun) or adding a valuable substance (e.g., vinegar itself). The outcome differs.
Abu Hanifa's general rule: if valueless, owner takes; if valuable, usurper owns.
3
Analyze the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Imam Muhammad distinguishes based on the timing of transformation: if vinegar becomes vinegar immediately upon adding a little vinegar, it is consumption (istihlak) and the usurper owns all. If it happens gradually after mixing, it is not consumption but mixing, so ownership is shared. Abu Hanifa considers any mixing as consumption, so the usurper owns all in both cases.
This is an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the definition of istihlak.
4
Apply the ruling to two scenarios
Scenario 1: Usurper adds a little vinegar to wine, and it instantly becomes vinegar → Muhammad: usurper owns all (immediate consumption). Abu Hanifa: also owns all. Scenario 2: Usurper adds a little vinegar, but it takes time to become vinegar → Muhammad: shared proportionally. Abu Hanifa: still usurper owns all.
The practical difference is only in the gradual case.
5
Synthesize the overall Hanafi ruling
The 'greatest Imam' (al-A'zam, Abu Hanifa) holds that in both cases the usurper owns the vinegar and owes nothing, because the act of mixing is consumption of the wine (which was his own? Actually, wine belonged to owner. But Abu Hanifa views the usurper's action as consuming his own property? The text says: 'because he destroyed his own property' – this is problematic. The commentary clarifies that the usurper owns the vinegar because the wine was not valuable, and mixing with valuable vinegar gives priority to the valuable. The 'consumption' refers to the wine being used up in the process.)
Consistency: Abu Hanifa's principle prioritizes valuable property over non-valuable.

The owner takes it and returns the increase from tanning into it; and if he destroys it, he does not guarantee. This according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he guarantees the tanned leather, and the owner gives him the increase from tanning in it. The summary: if he makes vinegar or tans with something of no value, the owner takes it both ways because the original right is his, and there is nothing from the usurper except labor, which has no value. But if he makes vinegar or tans with something of value, it becomes the property of the usurper, giving precedence to valuable property over non-valuable. And the difference according to Abu Hanifa between vinegar and leather: the owner takes the leather but not the vinegar, because the leather remains but he removed the impurities, while wine does not remain but becomes another substance. And he does not guarantee the leather according to Abu Hanifa if he destroys it, because he usurped untanned leather and tanned it by adding salt. As for if he turned it into vinegar by adding vinegar to it, according to Muhammad, if it becomes vinegar immediately, it becomes the property of the usurper and nothing is owed to him, because he consumed it and it is not valuable. And if it does not become vinegar except after mixing, if the added vinegar was a little, it is between them according to their measure, because he mixed vinegar with vinegar in estimation, and according to his principle it is not consumption. Liability for Usurping Musical Instruments: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Defines terms and presents a qiyās-based dispute on liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute over liability for usurping prohibited items and identify the muftā bihī position according to the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Liability follows value; if an item has no recognized value due to prohibition, the usurper is not liable.

This lesson examines the classical Hanafi dispute regarding liability for usurping items like musical instruments and intoxicating drinks. The issue turns on whether such items possess legally recognized value (taqawwum). Abū Ḥanīfa held that the physical substance retains value for alternative uses, while his two companions argued that the primary prohibited purpose voids all value. This intra-madhhab ikhtilāf has practical consequences for tort liability and validity of sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
Determine the specific act (usurping and breaking a lute, spilling wine, etc.) and state the ruling of Abū Ḥanīfa (liable, sale valid) vs. the Sahibayn (not liable, sale invalid).
The dispute is not about all items, only those whose primary use is prohibited.
2
Extract the underlying principle: taqawwum
Recognize that the core principle is whether the item has legally recognized value (taqawwum). Abū Ḥanīfa sees potential value in the physical substance; the Sahibayn see no value because the primary use is prohibited.
Taqawwum is the basis for liability in ghaṣb.
3
Analyze the qiyās presented
The text compares destroying the skill (ṣinʿa) in making a prohibited item: just as one is not liable for destroying the skill (no value), similarly, the item itself has no value. Identify the effective cause (ʿilla): absence of recognized value.
This is a qiyās khafī (hidden analogy).
4
Examine the objections of the Sahibayn
Understand why the Sahibayn reject Abū Ḥanīfa’s view. They argue that the item’s primary purpose is prohibited, so it lacks taqawwum, and thus no liability. The physical substance cannot repurpose it because its existence as a prohibited item negates value.
5
Determine the muftā bihī and the tarjīḥ mechanism
Though Abū Ḥanīfa’s view is from the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, the muftā bihī is the Sahibayn’s view due to widespread corruption (kathrat al-fasād). Note that this tarjīḥ is based on the context of the time.
Al-Marghinānī and later scholars adopted this tarjīḥ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغصب / تعريفه وأحكامه
وضمن بكسر ،معزف، وإراقة سكر، ومنصف، وصح بيعها
ولا قيمة له ، والضمان يتبع التقوم ، لكن العين إذا كانت باقياً لا يشترط. وضَمِينَ بكسرِ مِعْزَفِ، وإراقة سكر، ومنصف، وصحٌ بيعُ المعزف : آلة اللهو كالطنبور والمزمار ونحوهما ، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما لا يضمن حق التقوم ، ثم الأصلُ وهو الصنعة لا يجب عليه الضمان بالإتلاف، فكذا التبع، فصار كما إذا هلك من غير صنعه.
Book of usurpation / its definition and rulings. And he is liable for [the value of] a broken lute, spilled sugar, syrup, and half-cooked [juice], and its sale is valid. And there is no value for it, and liability follows [the existence of] value, but if the object still exists, this condition is not required. And he is liable for a broken lute, spilled sugar, syrup, and half-cooked [juice]; and the sale of the lute is valid: it is an instrument of amusement like the ṭanbūr, the flute, and the like. This is according to Abū Ḥanīfa, while according to his two companions, he is not liable for the value because value [for such items] does not exist. Then the original [principle is]: for the skill [of making it], he is not liable by destruction, so likewise the incidental, so it becomes as if it perished without his action.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on usurpation (ghaṣb) by defining key terms and establishing the principle that liability (ḍamān) follows value (taqawwum). The author states that if one breaks a lute (miʿzaf), spills sugar (sukkar), syrup (irāqa), or half-cooked juice (munṣif), he is liable for its value, and the sale of such items is valid according to Abū Ḥanīfa. However, his two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) disagree: since these items have no legitimate use, they hold that no value exists, hence no liability. The commentary explains that the ruling is based on a qiyās: just as one is not liable for destroying the mere skill (ṣinʿa) in making a prohibited item, so the incidental liability for the item itself is absent. This is an intra-Hanafī dispute where the principle of following value is key.

Key Terms
المعزف (al-miʿzaf): A stringed musical instrument like a lute (ṭanbūr) or flute
السكر (al-sukkar): Unripe date juice that has fermented until strong
المنصف (al-munṣif): Juice that has been cooked until half has evaporated

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The lute has no owner.
B) The lute has no recognized value because its primary use is prohibited.
C) The lute was destroyed by accident.
D) The skill of making the lute is not valuable.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the principle that liability follows value (taqawwum) means that items without recognized value due to prohibition do not incur liability, a view adopted by the Sahibayn and followed in fatwa.

Liability for Umm Walad and Mudabbara: Abū Ḥanīfa's Distinction
Ruling distinguishing liability for two slave types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for usurping an umm walad versus a mudabbara, and identify the legal reasoning based on the status of each.

Core Idea
An umm walad is treated as free for liability; a mudabbara remains property.

This short passage from the matn of al-Hidayah draws a sharp distinction between two types of slaves: the umm walad (mother of her master's child) and the mudabbara (promised freedom after death). While both are tied to the master's death, their legal status during the master's life differs crucially for liability when they are usurped.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two categories
The passage rules on the liability of a usurper when the usurped slave perishes. Two specific categories are mentioned: umm walad and mudabbara.
2
Note the ruling for each
For the umm walad, there is no liability (la yadman). For the mudabbara, there is liability (bi khilaf al-mudabbara, i.e., unlike the mudabbara, meaning the mudabbara triggers liability).
3
Understand the legal reason (illah)
The distinction is based on the fact that the umm walad cannot be sold by her master during his lifetime, making her akin to a free person in terms of liability. The mudabbara, however, can still be sold until the master's death, so she remains property (mal) and liability attaches.
This is Abū Ḥanīfa's view.
4
Recognize attribution to Abū Ḥanīfa
The matn explicitly states 'hādhā ‘inda Abī Ḥanīfah' (this is according to Abū Ḥanīfa), indicating that this ruling is his specific position, possibly differing from his companions or other schools.
5
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a usurper seizes an umm walad and she dies, no compensation is owed; for a mudabbara, compensation (qīmah) must be paid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And regarding a slave mother (umm walad) who is usurped and then perishes: he is not liable, unlike the mudabbara (slave promised freedom after master's death). This is according to Abū Ḥanīfa. The flute (mizmār) is an instrument of pleasure, called in Persian: the duff (tambourine) is beaten. So in al-Ṣiḥāḥ. [1] His statement: 'According to Abū Ḥanīfa, he is only liable for its value...' Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on liability for usurped items, focusing on two special categories: the umm walad (slave mother of a child from her master) and the mudabbara (slave granted freedom upon the master's death). According to Abū Ḥanīfa, if a usurped umm walad perishes, the usurper is not liable because she is considered, in terms of liability, akin to a free person (since her master cannot sell her). In contrast, liability exists for a mudabbara because she remains a commodity (māl) until the master's death; the tadbīr does not prevent liability. The commentary then elaborates on Abū Ḥanīfa's method of calculating value: he does not assess the value of the instrument as a tool of amusement (which would be high) but rather its value for permissible purposes, such as using a lute as a container or a tambourine for cotton. This is compared to a singing slave girl (jāriya mughanniya) valued for her voice? Actually, the text says 'its value unsuitable for amusement' like a butting ram or flying pigeon, which are valued for non-entertainment uses. The commentator also clarifies that the dispute between Abū Ḥanīfa and the two companions applies to all musical instruments, not just those mentioned, contrary to a possible misreading. For instance, if a drum is used for battle (permissible), Abū Ḥanīfa would still require liability (since it has value), but the Sahibayn would not (since it is not typically sold).

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child from her master
مدبرة (mudabbara): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbīr)
ضمان (ḍamān): The legal liability or compensation due for usurping or destroying another's property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the text say there is no liability for usurping an umm walad but liability for a mudabbara?
A) Umm walad is worth less money
B) Umm walad cannot be sold, so she is not treated as property for liability
C) Mudabbara is a stricter category
D) Abū Ḥanīfa changed his opinion later

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for usurping a slave depends on whether the slave can be sold; the umm walad is unsaleable, so no liability; the mudabbara is saleable, so liability attaches.

Valuation of Usurped Musical Instruments According to Abū Ḥanīfa
Explanation of valuation method for usurped musical instruments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abū Ḥanīfa's method for valuing usurped musical instruments and distinguish his position from that of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abū Ḥanīfa values usurped music instruments at their worth for permissible use, not for amusement.

This commentary expands on Abū Ḥanīfa's approach to assessing compensation for usurped items that are typically used for prohibited purposes, such as musical instruments. The key principle is that liability is based on the item's value for permissible uses, not its inflated value for amusement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and the dispute
The item is a mizmār (flute) or other musical instrument. The dispute is whether the usurper's liability is based on its value as an amusement tool or its value for permissible uses (e.g., as a container).
Also noted: singing slave girl, butting ram, flying pigeon.
2
State Abū Ḥanīfa's position
He holds that liability is for the value when the item is considered unsuitable for amusement (ghayru ṣāliḥatin lil-lahw). Thus, a flute is valued as a potential container or raw material.
3
Apply the principle
For example, a lute (barbaṭ) if used for salt or tharīd, or a duff (tambourine) for cotton. The usurper pays the value based on that alternative use, not the high value as an entertainment item.
4
Recognize the disagreement with the Sahibayn
The text indicates that the Sahibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) may not require liability at all for some items, or value them differently. The passage clarifies that Abū Ḥanīfa's view applies to all musical instruments, not just those enumerated.
The dispute is over the wajh (valuation method) not the principle of liability.
5
Understand the broader significance
This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that compensation (ḍamān) is assessed based on lawful utility, not illicit market value. It prevents the usurper from profiting from prohibited practices.

The commentary then elaborates on Abū Ḥanīfa's method of calculating value: he does not assess the value of the instrument as a tool of amusement (which would be high) but rather its value for permissible purposes, such as using a lute as a container or a tambourine for cotton. The commentator also clarifies that the dispute between Abū Ḥanīfa and the two companions applies to all musical instruments, not just those mentioned, contrary to a possible misreading. For instance, if a drum is used for battle (permissible), Abū Ḥanīfa would still require liability (since it has value), but the Sahibayn would not (since it is not typically sold).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the value of usurped property that is normally used for prohibited purposes is assessed based on its utility for lawful uses, not its entertainment value.

Liability for Usurped Umm Walad and Valuation of Musical Instruments: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on liability for umm walad and dispute on valuation of instruments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on guarantee for umm walad versus mudabbara, and explain why Abu Hanifa values musical instruments at material worth while the Sahibayn value them at full market price.

Core Idea
An umm walad is not fully property, so no guarantee; but a mudabbara is, so guarantee applies. The value of instruments depends on their permissible use.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents two related intra-Hanafi disputes: the liability of a usurper for destroying an umm walad versus a mudabbara, and the valuation of musical instruments when compensation is due. Abu Hanifa holds that an umm walad is not fully property due to her connection to her master's child, so no financial guarantee is owed, while the mudabbara remains property and is fully guaranteed. For instruments, Abu Hanifa values them only at their material worth (e.g., the wood of a lute) unless they are used for permissible purposes like war drums or wedding tambourines, where full value is due by consensus. His two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree on both points: they consider both umm walad and mudabbara as full property, and they require full market value for all instruments. Understanding this dispute reveals the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to property rights and the effect of permissibility on valuation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core issues
Determine that the passage covers two separate rulings: (a) guarantee for a usurped umm walad versus a mudabbara, and (b) valuation of musical instruments when guarantee applies.
2
State Abu Hanifa's positions
For the umm walad, Abu Hanifa says no guarantee (la yadman) because she is not saleable. For instruments, he says the guarantor only pays the value as non-entertainment material (e.g., the raw material, not as a functioning instrument). Exception: instruments used for legitimate purposes (war drum, wedding tambourine) are fully guaranteed by consensus.
3
State the Sahibayn's opposing positions
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that both umm walad and mudabbara are fully property (mutakawwim), so guarantee is wajib in both cases. For instruments, they require full market value regardless of permissibility.
4
Analyze the rationale (ta'lil) behind the disagreement
Abu Hanifa bases his view on the unique status of the umm walad (mother of the master's child, cannot be sold) and on the idea that the value of an instrument lies only in its permissible use, not in its forbidden entertainment function. The Sahibayn focus on the property aspect: any usurped property must be compensated at market value, and the umm walad is still property, even if restricted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي أم ولد غصبت فهلكت لا يضمن بخلاف المدبرة. وعند أبي حنيفة إنما يضمن قيمته غير صالحة للهو كما في الجارية المغنية والكبش النطوح والحمامة الطيارة وغيرها. وأما طبل الغزاة والدف الذي يباح ضربه في العرس فمضمون بالاتفاق. وفي أم ولد عُصِبَتْ فهلكَتْ لا يضمن بخلاف المدبرة: هذا عند أبي حنيفة.
And regarding an umm walad who was usurped and then perished, he does not guarantee, unlike the mudabbara. According to Abu Hanifa, he only guarantees its value not fit for entertainment, such as a singing slave-girl, a butting ram, a flying pigeon, etc. As for the drum of warriors and the tambourine whose beating is permissible during weddings, it is guaranteed by agreement. And regarding an umm walad who was usurped and perished, he does not guarantee, unlike the mudabbara; this is according to Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage discusses two rulings: (1) If a usurper destroys an umm walad (a slave who has borne her master's child), he is not liable for her value according to Abu Hanifa, unlike a mudabbara (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) which is guaranteed. (2) Regarding musical instruments, Abu Hanifa holds that the guarantor must pay only the value of the item as non-entertainment material (e.g., the wood of a lute, not as a lute). However, if the instrument is used for a legitimate purpose like the drum of warriors or the tambourine for weddings, there is consensus that the full value is guaranteed. The difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is about other instruments: Abu Hanifa values them at their material worth, while the Sahibayn value them at full market value. The text also notes that the ruling for umm walad is specific to Abu Hanifa, as the Sahibayn would guarantee both umm walad and mudabbara.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, and thus cannot be sold and becomes free upon the master's death.
مدبرة (mudabbara): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir).
ضمان (daman): Financial guarantee or liability for compensation of property destroyed or lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The drum of warriors and the tambourine for weddings are fully guaranteed
C) No musical instruments are guaranteed
D) Only instruments used for entertainment are fully guaranteed

Key Takeaway
An umm walad is not subject to guarantee by a usurper according to Abu Hanifa, and the value of instruments depends on their permissible use—full value for lawful uses, material value otherwise.

Liability for Indirect Causation: Freeing a Slave or Animal
Ruling on liability when freeing or opening causes loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for indirect causation and apply it to identify liable acts in property loss and informing the ruler.

Core Idea
Indirect causation with active participation creates liability — freeing a slave or animal, or informing the ruler, makes you liable for loss.

This passage from the matn enumerates actions that cause loss indirectly. These are classic cases where the person did not physically destroy the property but removed restraints or enabled harm. The ruling is an important application of the principle of causation (tasbib) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions listed
List the five actions: loosening another's slave's shackle, untying his animal's rope, opening the stable door, opening the birdcage, and informing the ruler about a person who is wicked and can only be controlled by reporting.
The passage uses specific terms: qayd (shackle), ribat (rope), istabl (stable), qafas (cage).
2
State the ruling for each action
For each action, if the property is lost (slave escapes, animal runs away, bird flies away) or the person is harmed, the doer is liable to pay compensation (daman) for the value.
Liability attaches even though the causer did not directly destroy the property.
3
Extract the underlying principle
The common thread is that the person actively removed a restraint or initiated a chain that leads to loss. The act must be a direct cause (sabab) without an intervening factor.
This is known as tasbib (indirect causation) in Hanafi jurisprudence.
4
Note the condition for informing the ruler
The text says 'ولا يدفع بلا رفع' meaning informing is only liable if the ruler cannot be deterred except by that report. If the person could be stopped without reporting, the informer may not be liable.
This shows that liability is limited to necessary reports.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Test your understanding: if a neighbor's cow is tied, and you untie it and it wanders off and is lost, you must pay its value. If you only left the gate open (did not actively untie), liability may differ.
Active vs. passive causation can change the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حل قيد عبد غيره، أو رباط دابته، أو فتح باب اصطبلها، أو قفص طائره فذهبت، أو سعى إلى سلطان من يؤذيه، ولا يدفع بلا رفع، أو من يفسق ولا يمتنع بنهيه، أو قال مع سلطان
And whoever loosens the shackle of another’s slave, or the rope of his animal, or opens the door of its stable, or the cage of his bird, and it goes away; or goes to a ruler with information that harms someone, and it cannot be avoided except by reporting; or goes to a ruler informing him that someone is wicked and does not abstain at his prohibition, or says to the ruler...
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from the matn lists actions that cause loss indirectly. If someone unties a slave's shackle, takes off an animal's rope, opens a stable door or birdcage, and the slave, animal, or bird escapes and is lost, that person is liable for its value. Similarly, if someone acts as an informer (washi) to the ruler against a person who can only be defended against by reporting, and that person is harmed, the informer is liable. The key principle is that any direct or indirect causation of loss with intent or active participation creates liability. The ruling is stated without proof, typical of a matn to be memorized.

Key Terms
قيد (qayd): A shackle or tether used to confine a slave or animal.
رباط (ribat): A rope used to tie an animal or cow.
اصطبل (istabl): Stable or enclosure for animals.
قفص (qafas): Cage for birds.
سعى إلى سلطان (sa'a ila sultan): To inform or tattle to the ruler, likely causing harm to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because he did not physically destroy the bird
B) Yes, he must pay for the value of the bird
C) Only if he intended to steal it
D) No, because the owner should have secured the cage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, indirect causation (tasbib) that actively enables loss imposes liability, even without direct destruction.

Liability of the Slanderer to a Ruler: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability of slanderer to a ruler

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the liability of a slanderer (sā‘ī) and apply the mufta bihi position to a given scenario.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school splits: Muhammad imposes liability as a deterrent; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri‘āyah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a person who slanders another to a ruler (sā‘ī) without right must compensate for any resulting loss. The matn states Imam Muhammad's view that the slanderer must pay as a deterrent (zajran), and that this is the basis of fatwa. It contrasts with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's position of no liability. The commentary expands on the wording, clarifies that the condition 'without right' is essential, and notes that later authorities such as al-Minah and Radd al-Muhtar affirm Muhammad's view absolutely.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position according to Imam Muhammad
Identify that Muhammad holds the slanderer liable for damages if the slander is without right. The purpose is deterrent (zajr). This is the mufta bihi view.
The matn uses 'zajran lahu' to indicate the legal cause is deterrence, not compensation for direct harm.
2
Identify the opposing view within the Hanafi school
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the slanderer is not liable. Their reasoning is not given here but is based on the principle that a mere cause (sabab maḥḍ) does not entail guarantee.
The matn expresses their view as 'له' which is interpreted as no liability (the pronoun refers to the slanderer having no obligation).
3
Analyze the reasoning behind each position
Muhammad's view relies on the need to prevent corruption (sadd al-dharā'i‘) and deter harmful speech. The opposing view considers that the ruler acts voluntarily, so the slanderer is not the direct cause of loss.
The commentary states: 'السعي سبب محض لا هلاك المال' – slandering is a pure cause, not destruction of wealth.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and its authority
The later scholars (al-Minah, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, Radd al-Muhtar) adopt Muhammad's position absolutely. The fatwa today is 'وجوب الضمان على الساعي مطلقاً'.
This represents a shift from the earlier positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf to the preferred view of Muhammad due to practical necessity.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a slanderer causes a victim to be fined, Muhammad's view requires the slanderer to reimburse the victim; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's view does not. The mufta bihi enforces reimbursement.
The commentary also adds sub-rulings: if the slanderer is a slave, he is pursued after freedom; if he dies, his estate is liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يُغرم وقد لا يُغَرِّم إنه وجد مالاً فغرّمه شيئاً لا يضمن، ولو غرم البتة يضمن، وكذا لو سعى بغير حق عند محمد له زجراً له، وبه يفتى. وعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له. قوله : قد يغرم وقد لا يغرم ; صفة السلطان ; أي قد يأخذ السلطانُ مالاً من الرعايا بأمثال هذه الكلمات، وقد لا يأخذه، وقوله : إنه وجد ; أي إنّ فلاناً وجد مقول لقوله، أو قال مع سلطان. كما أفاد «الجلبي». قوله : قد يغرم وقد لا يغرم ; - بتشديد الراء – أي على البناء للفاعل من مزيد الثلاثي، قال في «المنح»: والفتوى اليوم بوجوب الضمان على الساعي مطلقاً. كذا في رد المحتار. قوله : بغير حق ; أي بغير صدور ذنب وجريمة منه. قوله : زجراً له ; قيَّد بقوله : ضمن لا بقوله : سعى كما توهم، وهو ظاهر. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». قوله : وبه يفتى ; دفعاً للفساد وزجراً له ، وإن كان غير مباشر، فإنّ السعي سبب محض لا هلاك المال، والسلطان يغرمه اختياراً لا طبعاً. هذا وفي «الدر المختار» : وعزّرَ ولو الساعي عبداً طولب بعد عتقه، ولو مات الساعي فللمسعي به أن يأخذ قدر الخسران من تركته هو الصحيح. كذا في «جواهر الفتاوى»، ونقل في ضمان الساعي غرّمَ الشاكي ديته لا لو مات بالضرب ; لندوره وقد مر في «باب السرقة». انتهى.
He may be made to pay and may not be made to pay. Indeed, he found wealth so he fined him something, he does not guarantee. And if he forfeits completely, he guarantees. Similarly, if he slanders without right, according to Muhammad, he must pay as a deterrent for him, and by it fatwa is given. And according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, he has (no liability). His statement: 'He may be made to pay and may not be made to pay' is a description of the ruler; i.e., the ruler may take wealth from the subjects by such words, and may not take it. And his statement: 'Indeed he found' means indeed so-and-so found (wealth) is the quoted speech for his statement, or he said with the ruler. As al-Jalabi stated. His statement: 'He may be made to pay and may not be made to pay' with shadda on the ra, i.e., on the pattern of the active participle from the augmented triliteral. He said in al-Minah: 'And the fatwa today is the obligation of guarantee on the slanderer absolutely.' As in Radd al-Muhtar. His statement: 'without right' means without the occurrence of a sin or crime from him. His statement: 'as a deterrent for him' he restricted it to his statement 'he guarantees' not his statement 'he slanders' as is imagined, and it is apparent. As in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. His statement: 'and by it fatwa is given' to prevent corruption and as a deterrent for him, even if it is not direct, for slandering is a pure cause, not destruction of wealth, and the ruler makes him pay by choice, not by nature. This and in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'And he is punished, even if the slanderer is a slave, he will be pursued after his freedom. And if the slanderer dies, then the one slandered may take the amount of loss from his estate; that is the correct view.' As in Jawahir al-Fatawa. And it is narrated in the guarantee of the slanderer: 'The complainant is made to pay his blood money, not if he dies from beating, due to its rarity, and it has passed in the chapter of theft.' End.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the liability (damān) of a person who slanders another to a ruler (sā‘ī). The matn states that such a slanderer, according to Imam Muhammad, must pay compensation as a deterrent (zajran lahu), and this is the position upon which fatwa is given. However, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the slanderer is not liable (the phrase 'له' likely indicates no guarantee). The commentary clarifies the wording: 'قد يغرم وقد لا يغرم' describes the ruler's discretion—he may exact payment or not. The phrase 'إنه وجد مالاً' means that the slanderer said 'So-and-so found wealth,' prompting the ruler to fine the accused, but if the slanderer made a definitive statement causing loss, he guarantees. The commentator notes that today's fatwa (from al-Minah) holds the slanderer absolutely liable. The condition 'بغير حق' (without right) means the slandered person committed no sin. The restriction 'زجراً له' ties guarantee to the slanderer's action, not merely to the act of slandering. The fatwa is given to prevent corruption, even though the slanderer is only a cause (sabab maḥḍ), not a direct destroyer. The commentary also adds details from later works: if the slanderer is a slave, he is pursued after freedom; if he dies, his estate bears the loss. The passage ends with a note on the rare case of the slandered person dying from beating, which is not compensable due to its rarity.

Key Terms
سعى (sa‘ā): To slander or inform against someone to a ruler (سلطان) with the intent to cause harm.
زجراً (zajran): As a deterrent; the purpose of imposing liability is to prevent the slanderer from repeating such harmful speech.
البتة (al-battata): Completely, absolutely; in the context, 'غرم البتة' means he forfeits the entire amount owed, not just a part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the passage, what is the ruling on a person who slanders another to a ruler without right?
A) He is not liable under any circumstance.
B) He is liable to pay compensation as a deterrent.
C) He is liable only if the ruler actually takes wealth.
D) He is liable, but the ruler must return the wealth.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds the slanderer (sā‘ī) liable for financial loss caused by false reports to a ruler, as a deterrent against corruption, even though the ruler acts voluntarily.

Liability of the False Informer (Sā'ī)
Ruling on liability of false informer with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the liability of the false informer (sā'ī), explain the qiyas used by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A false informer is made liable for deterrence (zajr) despite the ruler being a free agent — the fatwa follows Muhammad's view.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'āya presents the ruling on the liability of a person who falsely informs against another, leading to a fine. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf exonerate the sā'ī based on the principle of intervening free agency, while Imam Muhammad holds him liable for policy reasons. The passage also extends this principle to the analogy of opening a stable or cage, where the animal's escape is attributed to the opener despite the animal's free action. Let's work through the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and conflicting rulings
The sā'ī falsely informs the ruler, causing a fine. According to Muhammad, the sā'ī is liable to pay compensation (zajran lahu). According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, he is not liable because the ruler is a free agent (tawassut fi'l fā'il mukhtār).
The dispute is about legal causality, not the sinfulness of lying.
2
Locate the qiyas analogy
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize the sā'ī to a person who opens a stable door (horse escapes) or a cage (bird flies), arguing that the animal's free action breaks causality. Muhammad counters that the bird is naturally inclined to flee (majbūl 'alā al-nifār), so the opener is liable; analogously, the ruler is naturally inclined to act on the information, making the sā'ī liable.
The disagreement in the qiyas mirrors the dispute on the sā'ī.
3
Identify the effective cause (illa)
The illa in the stable/cage case: Abu Hanifa/Yusuf say the illa is the intervention of a free agent; Muhammad says it is the natural tendency (jiblah) leading to harm. The illa is then applied to the sā'ī case.
Muhammad's view gives weight to the natural outcome of the action, not just formal free agency.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The fatwa is given on Muhammad's view (yadman) for the purpose of deterrence (zajr), to prevent corruption. This is explicitly stated in the text: 'به يفتى'.
Policy (siyāsah) can override strict causality when public interest demands it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد يُغرم وقد لا يُغَرِّم إنه وَجِدَ مالاً فغرمه شيئاً لا يضمن، ولو غرم البتة يضمن، وكذا لو سعى بغير حق عند محمد له زجراً له، وبه يفتى. وعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له لا يضمن الساعي؛ لأنه توسط فعل فاعل مختار، وفي فتح باب الإصطبل، والقفص، خلاف محمد، لهما: توسط فعل المختار، وله: أن الطائر مجبول على النفار.
He may be fined and may not be fined. If he found property and fined him something, he does not guarantee. If he fined absolutely, he guarantees. Likewise, if he acted as a false informer without right, according to Muhammad he is liable to deter him, and this is the fatwa. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the false informer does not guarantee because his action is an intermediary of a free agent. And regarding opening the door of a stable and a cage, there is disagreement with Muhammad: for them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), it is an intermediary of a free agent; for him (Muhammad), the bird is naturally inclined to flee.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
The passage discusses the liability of a person who falsely informs (sā'ī) against another, leading to the latter being fined or harmed. The ruling is that if the sā'ī's information leads to a fine, he is not liable according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, because the actual taking of property is done by a free agent (the ruler), and the sā'ī is only an indirect cause. However, Muhammad holds him liable to deter such corruption, and this is the fatwa (relied-upon opinion). The text then draws an analogy: if someone opens a stable door and a horse escapes, or opens a cage and a bird flies away, there is a disagreement. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the opener is not liable because the animal's escape is through the action of a free agent (the animal itself), but for Muhammad, the bird is naturally prone to flee, so the opener is liable. This shows the principle that a direct cause (mubāsharah) imposes liability, while an indirect cause (tasabbub) may not when a free agent intervenes.

Key Terms
الساعي (al-sā'ī): The person who falsely informs or defames another to the ruler, causing harm or fine.
توسط فعل فاعل مختار (tawassut fi'l fā'il mukhtār): Intervention of a free agent's action between the cause and the harm, breaking legal causality.
زجراً له (zajran lahu): As a deterrent to him (the sā'ī), making him liable for policy reasons.
مجبول على النفار (majbūl 'alā al-nifār): Naturally inclined to flee (said of a bird).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa, why is the false informer (sā'ī) liable?
A) Because he directly harmed the victim by lying.
B) Because the ruler is not a free agent.
C) As a deterrent (zajr) to prevent corruption.
D) Because he actually took the money.

Key Takeaway
The false informer is liable in the Hanafi fatwa to prevent corruption, despite the principle that a free agent's action breaks causality — policy overrides pure logic.

Non‑Liability of the Tax Collector and the Cage‑Opening Dispute
Ruling on non-liability of tax collector with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the tax collector's non‑liability and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf from Imam Muhammad regarding opening a cage.

Core Idea
Mediating a free agent's act breaks liability; but a naturally fleeing bird may re‑attach it.

This passage introduces a key principle in Hanafi tort law: when a person merely mediates the act of a free agent – someone who acts by their own choice – the mediator is not liable for any resulting loss. The tax collector (sā‘ī) is the classic example. However, the school divides on a related case: if a person opens a stable or cage and the animal escapes, does the same principle apply? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say yes; Imam Muhammad says no, because the bird's instinct to flee makes the opener a direct cause. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its legal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling and its Basis
Locate the central statement: 'لا يضمن الساعي' and the reason given: 'لأنه توسط فعل فاعل مختار'. The ruling is non‑liability because the mediator does not directly cause the harm – the free agent's choice is the legal cause.
2
Understand the Majority Analogy
The text says 'وفي فتح باب الإصطبل، والقفص، خلاف محمد، لهما: توسط فعل المختار'. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize opening the cage to the tax collector's case: the animal's escape is due to its own free action (choice to flee), so the opener is a mediator, not a direct cause.
The phrase 'لهما' refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two imams).
3
Grasp Imam Muhammad's Objection
Imam Muhammad's view: 'وله: أن الطائر مجبول على النفار'. The bird is instinctively (جبلة) prone to flee; therefore, opening the cage is a direct cause (tawassuṭ here becomes a direct cause) because the animal's reaction is not a free choice but a natural instinct. Hence the opener is liable.
Imam Muhammad sees the animal's act as driven by nature, not by a free moral agent.
4
Evaluate the Thamarah al‑Khilāf
The practical difference: under the majority view, the cage‑opener is not liable; under Muhammad's view, he is liable. This affects who bears the loss when a valuable bird or horse escapes.
The dispute hinges on whether the animal's act is considered a 'free choice' or a compelled reaction.
5
Note the Authority Weight
The ruling of the sa'i's non‑liability is from Zahir al‑Riwayah. The dispute about the cage is recorded but the mufta bihi position is likely the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), though the text does not explicitly state tarjih.
Later Hanafi works may settle on the majority view; check Al‑Hidayah or Ibn 'Abidin for the operative fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يضمن الساعي ؛ لأنه توسط فعل فاعل مختار، وفي فتح باب الإصطبل، والقفص، خلاف محمد ، لهما : توسط فعل المختار، وله : أن الطائر مجبول على النفار.
The tax collector is not liable because he mediated the act of a free agent. And regarding opening the door of the stable or the cage, there is a disagreement with Muhammad: for the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), it is (like) mediating the act of a free agent, and for Muhammad, the bird is instinctively inclined to flee.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability of a tax collector (sa'i) when goods are lost due to his actions. The ruling: he is not liable. The reason: he merely causes the act of a free agent (the owner) who chooses to hand over the goods. However, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding opening a stable or cage door: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize it to the tax collector's case, saying the animal's escape is due to its own choice, so the opener is not liable. Muhammad argues that a bird is naturally prone to flee, so opening the cage is a direct cause, thus liability.

Key Terms
الساعي (al-sā'ī): The tax collector who collects dues from people.
توسط فعل فاعل مختار (tawassuṭ fi‘l fā‘il mukhtār): Mediating the act of a free agent, where the agent's free choice breaks the causal chain for liability.
مجبول على النفار (majbūl 'alā al-nifār): Instinctively inclined to flee; used by Imam Muhammad to describe the bird's nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he was coerced.
B) Because he mediated the act of a free agent.
C) Because the owner was negligent.
D) Because the goods were already damaged.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a mediator of a free agent's act is not liable; but when the act involves a naturally skittish creature, Imam Muhammad imposes liability, highlighting the nuance in causation.

Killing Corrupt Individuals: Permissibility vs. Apostasy
Fatwa refining the link between killing and apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissibility of killing and declaring disbelief in Hanafi fiqh, and apply the mashayikh's preferred position.

Core Idea
Killing is permissible but disbelief is not declared; the two rulings are separate.

This passage from Radd al-Muhtar addresses a critical nuance in Hanafi fiqh: the permissibility of killing a person who spreads corruption does not automatically imply that person is an apostate. The mashayikh (later scholars) refine the earlier view of Sayyid Abu al-Shuja', clarifying that the two rulings are separate, as illustrated by the case of highway robbers (qutta') who are killed without being declared kafir. Understanding this distinction is essential for issuing correct fatwas and for applying the correct funeral rites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the view of Sayyid Abu al-Shuja' (killing permitted and they are kafir) and the view of the mashayikh (killing permitted but not declared kafir).
2
Extract the key legal distinction
Note that the mashayikh separate the ruling of killing (permissible) from the ruling of disbelief. Killing is justified by corruption (fasad), not by apostasy.
This is a critical refinement: two different legal causes (sabab) yield two different rulings.
3
Analyze the evidence
The text argues that permissibility of killing does not indicate disbelief, using the analogy of highway robbers (qutta') and those who assist muharibun. These are killed as a hadd punishment but remain Muslims unless proven otherwise.
The dalil is a qiyas: the case of highway robbers is the asl; the corrupt individual is the far'.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
According to al-Bazzaziyyah and Radd al-Muhtar, the preferred view (mukhtar) is that of the mashayikh: do not declare them kafir.
This is the operative position for fatwa in the later Hanafi school.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
If the person is not declared kafir, they are still considered Muslim; thus, Islamic funeral rites (ghusl, janazah prayer, burial in Muslim cemetery) are obligatory. If declared kafir, they would be treated as apostates.
This has practical implications for the community's treatment of the deceased.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في رد المحتار) : قال في «الجزية»: وقد جوّز السيد أبو الشجاع قتله، فإنه ممن يسعى في الأرض بالفساد، ويثاب قاتلهم، وكان يفتي بكفرهم، ومختار المشايخ أنه لا يفتي بكفرهم، وجواز القتل لا يدلّ على الكفر، كما في القطاع، والأعونة من المحاربين الله ورسوله. قاله في «البزازية...الخ.

He used to give fatwa of their disbelief, but the chosen view of the mashayikh is that he does not give fatwa of their disbelief, and the permissibility of killing does not indicate disbelief, as in the case of highway robbers and the helpers of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger. He said it in al-Bazzaziyyah... etc.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This is a fatwa quoted from Radd al-Muhtar. It discusses the ruling on killing a certain type of person who causes corruption. Sayyid Abu al-Shuja' permitted killing such a person and considered them disbelievers. However, the preferred position among later scholars (mashayikh) is not to declare them disbelievers, because the mere permissibility of killing does not entail disbelief, as seen with highway robbers (qutta') who are killed but not necessarily considered apostates. This distinguishes the ruling of killing from the ruling of disbelief.

Key Terms
القطاع (al-quṭṭā‘): Highway robbers who are killed as a hadd punishment but not necessarily considered apostates.
المحاربون (al-muḥāribūn): Those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger, i.e., armed rebels or brigands.
السيد أبو الشجاع (al-Sayyid Abu al-Shuja‘): A Hanafi scholar who held that spreading corruption equates to apostasy.
المشايخ (al-mashāyikh): The later Hanafi scholars (e.g., those of the school after the early imams), whose position is preferred.

A) Killing implies disbelief.
B) Permissibility of killing does not indicate disbelief.
C) Only the ruler can declare disbelief.
D) The killer decides the religious status.

Key Takeaway
Killing a corrupt person is permissible under Hanafi law, but it does not automatically make them an apostate; the two rulings must be kept distinct.

Acquisition and Per Capita Division of Shuf'ah
Explains acquisition of shuf'ah and per capita division among multiple pre-emptors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi method of acquiring shuf'ah by mutual consent or qadi's decree and explain why division among multiple pre-emptors is per capita, not proportionate to shares.

Core Idea
Equality in the cause of entitlement requires equality in division.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary on al-Wiqayah explains two key aspects of pre-emption (shuf'ah): how the right is acquired and how it is divided among multiple partners. The ruling is straightforward, but the underlying legal reasoning (ta'lil) reveals an important usul principle: when the cause of a right is identical for all, the ruling must be identical. This lesson will break down the acquisition method and the per capita division rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of acquisition
What are the two ways a shafi' (pre-emptor) can acquire the pre-empted property? The passage mentions mutual consent and qadi's decree.
Mutual consent requires agreement of seller, buyer, and pre-emptor.
2
Understand the condition for ownership transfer
Ownership only transfers when the buyer voluntarily delivers the property or the qadi decrees. Why is this significant?
Without delivery or decree, the pre-emptor does not own the property even if he makes the two demands.
3
State the division rule among multiple pre-emptors
If multiple pre-emptors exist, how is the sold property divided? According to the number of heads (per capita), not by ownership shares.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
4
Trace the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
What is the illa (effective cause) for per capita division? Equality in the cause of entitlement (istiqaq).
The cause is the partnership itself, which is the same for all partners.
5
Apply the qiyas from the single pre-emptor case
If one pre-emptor alone takes the whole property, then multiple pre-emptors divide equally because the cause is identical.
This reasoning follows from Hanafi usul: equality in cause implies equality in ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتملك بالأخذ بالتراضي، أو بقضاء القاضي بقدر رؤوس الشفعاء لا الملك للخليط في نفس المبيع

وتملك بالأخذ بالتراضي، أو بقضاء القاضي بقدرِ رؤوس الشفعاء لا الملك أي إنما يملك العقار إذا أخذهُ الشَّفيعُ برضاه، وبرضا المشتري، وقوله أو بقضاء القاضي عطف على الأخذ لا على التراضي لأنَّ القاضي إذا حَكَمَ يثبت الملك للشفيع قبل أخذه، للخليط في نفس المبيع

قوله وتملك بالأخذ بالتراضي الخ على صيغة المجهول، والضمير راجع إلى العقار، والمراد بالتراضي رضاء البائع والمشتري والشفيع، فإذا تبقى واحد منها لم يملك بالأخذ إذا سلّمها المشتري أو حكم الحاكم لأنَّ ملك المشتري قد تم، فلا ينتقل إلى الشفيع إلا بالتراضي، أو قضاء القاضي، ويظهرُ فائدة هذا الأخذ إذا سلّمها أو قضى القاضي بها فيما إذا مات الشفيع بعد الطلبين، أعني الإشهاد والمواثبة، وتمام الكلام في الهداية فانظر هنا، وفائدة توقف الملك بتسليم المشتري إلى وقتِ حكم الحاكم أن يثبت الملك للشفيع، وإن وجد منه الطلبان، أما تسليم المشتري بدون حكم الحاكم أو بحكم الحاكم، فإذا وجد أحدهما حينئذ تملك الشفيع الدار المشفوعة.

قوله بقدر رؤوس الشفعاء الخ أي بقدرِ رؤوس الشفعاء الجار فيه.

قوله للخليط الخ متعلّق يجب أي يجب الشفعة للخليط، ويقسم على عدد الرؤوس إذا كانوا أكثر، لا بقدر الملك لأنهم استووا في سبب الاستحقاق لوجودِ علّة استحقاق الكلّ في حق كلّ واحدٍ منهم ولهذا لو انفرد واحد منهم أخذ الكل، والاستواء في العلة يوجب الاستواء في الحكم، ولا يرجح بكثرة العلل بل بقوة فيها.
And it is owned by taking with mutual consent, or by the decree of the qadi, according to the number of pre-emptors' heads, not by the ownership of the partner in the sold property itself. ... (explanation continues)... He said: 'according to the number of the heads of the pre-emptors' etc., meaning according to the number of their heads. He said: 'for the partner in the property itself' etc., is attached to 'wajaba' (is obligatory), meaning pre-emption is obligatory for the partner, and it is divided according to the number of heads if they are more than one, not according to ownership, because they are equal in the cause of entitlement due to the presence of the cause of entitlement for the whole in the right of each one of them; and for this reason, if one of them were alone he would take the whole, and equality in the cause necessitates equality in the ruling, and one is not given precedence by multiplicity of causes but by strength in them.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi commentary explains the method of acquiring pre-emption rights and their division among multiple pre-emptors. It first states that the property is owned by the pre-emptor either through mutual consent of the seller and buyer, or by a qadi's decree. The commentary clarifies that 'mutual consent' means the consent of the seller, buyer, and pre-emptor; if any one is missing, ownership does not transfer by mere taking but requires the buyer's delivery or the qadi's judgment. The benefit of this conditional acquisition appears if the pre-emptor dies after making the two claims (formal notification and immediate pursuit); then the right is transferred to his heirs. The division among multiple pre-emptors is explicitly based on the number of persons (per capita), not on their respective ownership shares in the property. The reason (illa) is that each pre-emptor share equally in the cause of entitlement (the partnership itself). Since the cause is identical, the resulting right is identical; one cannot give preference based on larger ownership. This follows Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
شفيع (shafi'): The person entitled to pre-emption (shuf'ah).
خليط (khalit): A partner in the property itself (not in appurtenant rights).
طلبين (talabayn): The two demands (requests for pre-emption) that must be made: first a witnessed notification (ishhad) and then an immediate pursuit (mavathaba).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is the pre-empted property divided among multiple pre-emptors when they are all partners in the same property?
A) According to their ownership shares
B) Equally according to number of pre-emptors (heads)
C) According to the date of purchase
D) By lottery

Key Takeaway
Pre-emptors divide the sold property equally per capita, not proportionate to shares, due to equality in the cause of entitlement.

Types of Pre-emptors and the Shafi'i Dispute on Division
Identifies three categories of pre-emptors and presents Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of pre-emptors in Hanafi fiqh and explain the Hanafi position on division of the pre-empted property among multiple pre-emptors, contrasting it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is established for partners in property, partners in rights, and adjacent neighbours; Hanafi divides equally per capita, while Shafi'i divides proportionally to shares.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) allows a co-owner to step into the shoes of a seller when a share is sold to an outsider. But who exactly qualifies? The Hanafi school recognizes three types, each with a specific order of priority. In this lesson, we will identify these categories and then delve into a key dispute with the Shafi'i school concerning how a pre-empted property is divided when multiple pre-emptors exercise their right. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to see how a single hadith can generate different rulings based on underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on categories of pre-emptors
Identify the three categories mentioned in the text: (1) partner in the property itself (khalit), (2) partner in appurtenant rights (shirb, tariq khass), and (3) adjacent neighbour (jar mulasiq). Note the order implied by 'thumma'.
The partner in the property has the strongest claim; the neighbour the weakest.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Recognise that the Shafi'i school does not grant pre-emption to partners in rights or adjacent neighbours, and also differs on division. Understand the opposing dalil: pre-emption is proportional to ownership shares.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
Examine the Shafi'i rationale: rights follow ownership, so division must be proportional. Study the numerical examples given in the passage (half, third, sixth).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that the cause (illa) of shuf'ah is partnership itself, which is equal for all pre-emptors. The hadith 'al-shuf'atu li shariqin lam yuqasim' establishes a general right without qualification, implying equality.
The Hanafi response relies on the principle that uniformity in cause yields uniformity in ruling.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Summarise the practical consequence: under Hanafi, two partners with 2/3 and 1/3 shares who pre-empt a sold share divide it equally; under Shafi'i, they divide it 2:1. This affects the distribution of property.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تم له في حق المبيع كالشرب والطريق الخاصين كشرب نهر لا تَجْري فيه السفن، وطريق لا ينفذ، ثم لجار ملاصق، بابه في سكة أخرى، كواضع جذع على الحائط

ثم للشريك في حق المبيع كالشرب والطريق الخاصين كشرب نهر لا تجري فيه السفن، وطريق لا ينفذ، ثم لجار ملاصق، بابه في سكة أخرى، كواضع جذع على الحائط إنما ذَكَرَ واضعَ الجذع ليعلم أنَّه جار، وليس بخليط، ولا يشترط للجار الملاصق وضع الجذع حتى لو لم يكن له شيء على الحائط يكون جاراً ملاصقاً، وعند الشافعي لا تثبت الشفعة وقال الشافعي له يجب بقدر الملك، حتى لو كانت الدار بين ثلاثة لأحد نصفُها، وللآخر ثلثها، وللثالث سدسها، فباعَ صاحب النصف نصيبه فالشريكان لو أخذاه بالشفعة يقسمانه أثلاثاً، ثلث لصاحب السدس، وثلثان لصاحب الثلث. ولو باع صاحب الثلث ثلثه، يقسمه للشريكان الباقيان أرباعاً، ربع لصاحب السدس، وثلاثة الأرباع لصاحب النصف، ولو باع صاحب السدس سدسه، اقتسمه الشريكان الباقيان أخماساً، خمساه لصاحب الثلث، وثلاثة أخماسه لصاحب النصف عنده، وعندنا يقتسمان في الكل نصفين، هذا ما في البناية شرح الهداية.

للفظ ثم يفيد ثبوت حق الشفعة لكل واحد من هؤلاء، وأيضاً يفيد الترتيب. كذا في الهداية

للجار الملاصق سواء كان مكاتباً أو مأذوناً أو ذميّاً عملاً بإطلاق الحديث، وهو قوله الشفعة لشريك لم يقاسم.

وعند الشافعي لا تثبت الشفعة الخ وله قوله الشفعة فيما لم يقسم.
Then for the partner in the right of the sold property, such as the share in water and private roads, like a water channel in which ships do not run, and a road that is not a thoroughfare; then for the adjacent neighbour whose door is in another alley, like one who places a beam on the wall. ... Then for the partner in the right of the sold property like the water share and private roads ... then for the adjacent neighbour... He only mentioned the one who places a beam to let it be known that he is a neighbour, not a partner, and it is not a condition for the adjacent neighbour to place a beam, so that even if he has nothing on the wall, he is still an adjacent neighbour. According to al-Shafi'i, pre-emption is not established (for the neighbour), and al-Shafi'i said: (division) must be according to ownership, such that if a house is among three, one having half, another a third, and another a sixth... (examples)... in his view, but in our view they divide all cases equally in half. This is in al-Bina'ah Sharh al-Hidayah. The word 'thumma' indicates the establishment of the right of pre-emption for each of these, and also indicates the order. Thus in al-Hidayah. The adjacent neighbour, whether he is a mukatib, a ma'dhun, or a dhimmi, because of the general application of the hadith, which is his saying: 'Pre-emption is for a partner who has not divided.' And according to al-Shafi'i, pre-emption is not established ... and he has his saying: 'Pre-emption is in what is not divided.'
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage defines the three categories of persons entitled to pre-emption (shuf'ah) in Hanafi fiqh and then presents the detailed disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding how the property is divided among multiple pre-emptors. The categories, in order of priority, are: (1) the partner in the property itself (khalit); (2) the partner in appurtenant rights such as a share in a non-navigable river or a private dead-end road; and (3) the adjacent neighbour whose wall adjoins the property, even if he has no beam on the wall. The word 'thumma' (then) indicates both the establishment of the right for each and the order of priority. The Hanafi school supports the neighbour's right by the general hadith 'Pre-emption is for a partner who has not divided,' which includes any kind of sharing. The Shafi'i school does not grant pre-emption to the adjacent neighbour nor to partners in rights; they only recognize it for the partner in the property. Furthermore, on the issue of division among multiple pre-emptors, the Shafi'i school divides the property in proportion to each pre-emptor's ownership share in the property, while the Hanafi school divides equally, because the cause of entitlement (shirka) is equal for all. The commentary provides specific numerical examples to illustrate the difference.

Key Terms
شرب (shirb): A share in a water source (e.g., river) that does not allow navigation.
طريق خاص (tariq khass): A private road that does not lead to a public thoroughfare.
جار ملاصق (jar mulasiq): An adjacent neighbour whose wall touches the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following has the strongest claim to pre-emption?
A) Adjacent neighbour (jar mulasiq)
B) Partner in a water share (shirb)
C) Partner in the property itself (khalit)
D) Partner in a private road (tariq khass)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, shuf'ah is established for three categories in order, and when multiple pre-emptors exercise their right, the sold share is divided equally per capita, regardless of their ownership percentages.

The Three Demands of Pre-emption and the Council of Knowledge
Expanding on the three demands and the council condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three demands of pre-emption, explain the condition of the council of knowledge, and apply the mufta bihi view that immediate demand is required.

Core Idea
The first demand of pre-emption must be immediate; silence voids the right.

Pre-emption (shuf'a) is a right that must be claimed promptly. The Hanafi school distinguishes three sequential demands: an instant verbal claim, witnessing the claim, and then legal action. A key condition is that the first demand must be made in the same sitting where the preemptor learns of the sale. However, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether the preemptor has time to reflect (khiyar al-majlis) or must demand immediately. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its requirements
The preemptor (shafi') must make a demand upon learning of the sale. Identify what constitutes a valid demand (verbal expression) and the condition of the council of knowledge.
The demand must be in the same sitting where knowledge of the sale is acquired.
2
Locate the three distinct demands
From the commentary, list the three demands: talab muwathaba (instant claim), talab ishhad (witnessing), and talab tamlik (ownership claim). Understand the sequence.
The first demand is named from the hadith 'al-shuf'a li-man wathabaha'.
3
Analyze the dispute over khiyar al-majlis
Al-Karkhi holds that the preemptor has the option of the council (can delay demand within the same sitting). The mufta bihi view (Zahir al-Riwayah) holds that there is no such option; even minimal silence invalidates the right.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon fatwa requires immediate demand. Silence, even a short pause, leads to loss of shuf'a. This is based on the hadith emphasizing haste.
Students must know the practical consequence: delay = loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب طلب الشفعة ويطلبها الشفيع في مجلس علمه بالبيع بلفظ يُفْهَمُ طلبها كطلبت الشفعة ونحوه واعتبار مجلس العلم اختيار الكرخي له وعند بعض المشايخ ليس له خيار المجلس حتى إن سكت أدنى سكوت تبطل شفعته وهو طلب مواثبة إنما سمي بهذا ليدل على غاية التعجيل. قوله: ويطلبها الشفيع... الخ؛ اعلم أن للشفيع ثلاث طلبات: الأول: طلب الإشهاد. الثاني: طلب المواثبة. الثالث: طلب التمليك، كما بين في المتن، والمراد بالمواثبة المسارعة والمبادرة من الوثوب أو من الوثبة، يعني برجستن از حال خود، وأشار إلى الأول بقوله: وهو طلب مواثبة، وإلى الثاني بقوله: وهو طلب الإشهاد، وإلى الثالث بقوله: وهو طلب تمليك وخصومة. كذا في الفوائد العارفية. قوله: إنما سمي... الخ؛ قيل إنما سمي به تبركا بلفظ الحديث وهو قوله: الشفعة من واثبها أي طلبها على وجه السرعة والمبادرة. قاله الجلبي.
Chapter of Demanding Pre-emption. And the preemptor demands it in the council of his knowledge of the sale with an expression that conveys his demand, like 'I demand pre-emption' and similar. Considering the council of knowledge is the choice of al-Karkhi for him, and according to some scholars, he does not have the option of the council, so if he remains silent even minimally, his pre-emption is invalidated. And it is an immediate demand (talab muwathaba). It is named thus to indicate utmost haste. His statement: 'And the preemptor demands it... etc.' Know that the preemptor has three demands: first: demand of witnessing (talab al-ishhad), second: demand of immediacy (talab al-muwathaba), third: demand of ownership and litigation (talab al-tamlik wa al-khusuma), as explained in the matn. The meaning of muwathaba is hastening and rushing, from wathaba or wathba, meaning to leap from one's state. He referred to the first as 'and it is talab muwathaba', to the second as 'and it is talab ishhad', and to the third as 'and it is talab tamlik wa khusuma'. Thus in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyya. His statement: 'It is only named... etc.' It is said it is only named thus as a blessing with the wording of the hadith: 'Pre-emption belongs to the one who leaps for it (man wathabaha)', i.e. demands it with speed and haste. Said by al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of demanding pre-emption (shuf'a). The matn states the ruling: the preemptor must demand his right in the same sitting where he learns of the sale, using words like 'I demand pre-emption.' The sharh expands by noting that this requirement (condition of the council of knowledge) is the choice of Imam al-Karkhi, while some other mashayikh hold that there is no option of the council (khiyar al-majlis); rather, even a minimal silence invalidates the pre-emption. The relied-upon fatwa, as mentioned in the footnotes, is on the latter view, which is also the position of Zahir al-Riwayah. The commentator then explains the three types of demand: the first is talab al-muwathaba (instant demand), the second talab al-ishhad (demand of witnessing, i.e., to prove the right), and the third talab al-tamlik (demand of ownership, i.e., legal claim). The name 'talab muwathaba' is derived from the hadith 'al-shuf'a li-man wathabaha,' emphasizing the requirement of haste. This lesson thus clarifies the procedural steps and conditions for a valid pre-emption claim in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): A pre-emptive right to purchase a property that has been sold to another, especially for partners in undivided property.
طلب مواثبة (talab muwathaba): The first and immediate demand of pre-emption, made as soon as one learns of the sale, indicating haste.
مجلس العلم (majlis al-'ilm): The sitting or meeting in which the preemptor learns of the sale; the demand must be made in this same sitting.
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option to complete or void a contract within the sitting; here applied to the preemptor's demand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His right remains intact because he can demand later.
B) His pre-emption is void because silence invalidates the right.
C) He must repeat the demand with witnesses.
D) He has the option to demand within the same council.

Key Takeaway
The first demand (talab muwathaba) must be immediate; silence in the council of knowledge voids the pre-emption right according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Three Types of Shuf'ah Demand and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Delay
Explains the three demands of shuf'ah and the dispute on delaying the third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three sequential demands of shuf'ah, explain the condition for the third demand, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on its delay, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The right of shuf'ah requires three specific demands; delaying the third does not nullify it unless the pre-emptor explicitly abandons the right.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text outlines the procedural steps a pre-emptor must take to secure the right of shuf'ah (pre-emption). It details three successive demands and then focuses on an important intra-Hanafi disagreement: does delaying the final demand before a judge invalidate the right? Understanding this dispute clarifies when a pre-emptor's claim remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three demands
The passage lists three sequential demands: talab al-muwathabah (immediate verbal claim), talab al-ishhad (bearing witness at the property), and talab al-tamlik wa al-khusumah (demanding ownership before a judge). Note the order and wording.
2
Understand the condition for the third demand
The third demand is only obligatory when the pre-emptor is able to bear witness at the property or against the seller/buyer. If unable, he must appoint a representative, send a letter, or wait until able; failure to act when able nullifies the right.
This condition is critical for understanding the limits of the obligation.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that delaying the third demand does not nullify shuf'ah. Imam Muhammad argues that if more than a month passes after ishhad, the right is lost. Identify the reasoning of each side.
The dispute is recorded in the zahir al-riwayah.
4
Evaluate the proofs
Imam Muhammad's proof: harm to the buyer who cannot use the property indefinitely. Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf's proof: an established right is not lost except by explicit waiver, as with other rights. Consider which reasoning is stronger.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: delay does not nullify. This is stated in al-Hidayah, al-Multaqa, and other authoritative works. The right is only lost by explicit verbal abandonment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
