كأن الشفيع يثب"، ويطلب الشفعة
(ثم يشهد عند العقار، أو على من معه من بائع أو مشتر، فيقول : اشترى فلان هذه الدار، وأنا شفيعها، وقد كنتُ طلبتُ الشفعة، وأطلبها الآن، فاشهدوا عليه، وهو طلب إشهاد).
[١] قوله : كأن الشفيع يثب... الخ ؛ من الوثبة، بالثاء المثلثة، والباء الموحدة، وهي: الطفرة، قد فسر في «الصحاح» كلُّ واحدٍ منهما بالأخرى ، قاله الجلبي [٢]قوله : أو على من معه... الخ ؛ الضمير المتصل بلفظ : مـع ؛ يرجع إلى موصول، ومن بائع أو مشتريان، الموصول يعني يشهد على شخص يكون معه العقار من البائع إن لم يُسلم المبيع، أو مشتر إن سَلّم، هذا ملخص ما في بعض حواشي «الكتاب».
(۳) قوله : على من معه ؛ أي يشهد على شخص يكون معه العقار من بائع إن لم يسلّم المبيع، أو مشتر إن سلمه ، وقد عبر عنه الشارحُ له بقوله : أو عند صاحب اليد. قال شيخ الإسلام : الشفيع إنما يحتاج إلى طلب الإشهاد بعد طلب المواثبة إذا سمع الشراء حال عينه من المشتري والبائع والدار، أما إذا سمع الشراء بحضرة أحد هؤلاء فطلب طلب المواثبة، وأشهد على ذلك فهو يكفيه، ويقوم مقام الطلبين، قاله الجلبي
ثم يطلب عند قاض فيقول : اشترى فلان داراً كذا، وأنا شفيعها بدار كذا لي، فَمُرْهُ يُسَلَّم إلى، وهو طلب تمليك وخصومة، وبتأخيره لا تبطل الشفعة اعلم أنَّ هذا الطلب إنما يجب عند التَّمكن من الإشهاد عند الدار وعند صاحب اليد حتى لو تمكن، ولم يشهد بطلت شفعته، وفي «الذخيرة» : إذا كانَ الشفيع في طريق مكة فطلب طلب المواثبة ، وعجز عن طلب الإشهاد عند الدار، أو عند صاحب اليد يوكل وكيلاً إن وجد، وإن لم يجد يرسل رسولاً ، أو كتاباً ، فإن لم يجد فهو على شفعته ، فإذا حضر طلب، وإن وَجَدَ ولم يفعل بطلت شفعته. ثم يطلب عند قاض فيقول : اشترى فلان داراً كذا، وأنا شفيعها بدار كذا لي، فَمُرْهُ يُسَلّم إلى، وهو طلب تمليك وخصومة، وبتأخيره لا تبطل الشفعة [١] قوله : إنما يجب عند التمكن ... الخ ؛ كما إذا كان الشفيع حاضراً وقت البيع ، أو لم يغب عن بلده مدة السفر : يعني لم يكن غائباً عن البلد. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية». [٢] قوله : ثم يطلب عند قاض فيقول ... الخ ؛ لفظ : لي؛ جار ومجرور مع ياء المتكلم، صفة الدار ، وحرف الباء للسببية : أي بسبب دار معهود هي حق لي، وهي بيته، ولفظ : فمره، صيغة أمر مع ضمير المفعول، هذا هو الطلب الثالث ، ولا تسقط الشفعة بتأخير طلب الخصومة عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ، وقال محمد : إن ترك شهراً بعد الإشهاد وبطلت شفعته.
وجه قول محمد : إنه لو لم يسقط بتأخر الخصومة أبداً يتضرر به المشتري ، فلا يمكنه التصرف حذار نقض البيع من جهة الشفيع، فقدرنا الضرر بالشهر؛ لأنه أجل، وما دونه عاجل ووجه قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف : إن الحق متى ثبت أو استقر لا يسقط إلا بإسقاط ، وهو التصريح بلسانه، كما في سائر الحقوق. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية».
[٣] قوله : وبتأخيره لا تبطل ... الخ ؛ يعني لا تسقط الشفعة بتأخر هذا الطلب، (1) وهو ظاهر الرواية، وفي الهداية (٤) (۲۸)، و«الملتقى» (ص (۱۷۸)، و«الدر المختار» (٥ : ١٤٤)، و«الغرر (۲) : (۲۱۰)، وتنوير الأبصار» (ص۲۰۳): وعليه الفتوى.
Then he bears witness at the property, or against the one with him from the seller or buyer, saying: So-and-so bought this house, and I am its pre-emptor, and I had demanded pre-emption, and I demand it now, so bear witness against him. This is the demand of bearing witness. It is as if the pre-emptor jumps and demands pre-emption. (Then he bears witness at the property, or against the one with him from the seller or buyer, saying: So-and-so bought this house, and I am its pre-emptor, and I had demanded pre-emption, and I demand it now, so bear witness against him. This is the demand of bearing witness.) [1] His saying: 'It is as if the pre-emptor jumps...' from al-wathbah (jump) with the triple-dot thā and single-dot bā, meaning leap. It has been explained in as-Sihāh that each of the two is explained by the other, as said by al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: 'or against the one with him...' the attached pronoun in the word 'ma' refers to the relative noun, and 'man' (who) is the seller or buyer; i.e., he bears witness against a person who is with the property, either the seller if he has not delivered the sold item, or the buyer if he has delivered. This is a summary from some glosses of the book. (3) His saying: 'against the one with him' i.e., he bears witness against a person who is with the property, either the seller if he did not deliver the sold item, or the buyer if he delivered it. The commentator expressed it by saying 'or at the one in possession.' Shaykh al-Islam said: The pre-emptor needs the demand of bearing witness after the immediate demand only if he heard of the sale at the very moment from the buyer, seller, and the house. But if he heard of the sale in the presence of one of these, then he makes the immediate demand and bears witness on that, it suffices him and stands in place of both demands, as said by al-Jalabi. Then he demands before a judge, saying: So-and-so bought such-and-such a house, and I am its pre-emptor by such-and-such a house of mine, so order him to surrender it to me. This is the demand of ownership and litigation, and by delaying it pre-emption is not invalidated. Know that this demand is only obligatory when one is able to bear witness at the house and at the one in possession, such that if he is able and does not bear witness, his pre-emption is nullified. And in adh-Dhakhira: If the pre-emptor is on the road to Mecca and makes the immediate demand, and is unable to make the demand of bearing witness at the house or at the one in possession, he appoints a representative if he finds one; if he does not find, he sends a messenger or a letter; if he does not find (any means), he remains on his pre-emption, and when he arrives he demands; and if he finds (a means) and does not act, his pre-emption is nullified. Then he demands before a judge, saying: So-and-so bought such-and-such a house, and I am its pre-emptor by such-and-such a house of mine, so order him to surrender it to me. This is the demand of ownership and litigation, and by delaying it pre-emption is not invalidated. [1] His saying: 'It is only obligatory when able...' as when the pre-emptor is present at the time of sale, or has not been absent from his town for the duration of travel; meaning he is not absent from the town. So in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyya. [2] His saying: 'Then he demands before a judge...' the word 'li' (for me) is a genitive construction with the first-person pronoun, an adjective of the house, and the letter ba' is for causation: i.e., because of an acknowledged house that is my right, his house. The word 'famurhu' is a command form with object pronoun. This is the third demand, and pre-emption is not lost by delaying the demand of litigation according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: If he leaves it for a month after bearing witness, his pre-emption is nullified. The reasoning of Muhammad: If it were not lost by delaying litigation forever, the buyer would be harmed, for he cannot dispose (of the property) fearing revocation of the sale by the pre-emptor; so we estimated the harm by a month, because it is a term, and less than that is immediate. The reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: When a right is established and confirmed, it is not lost except by abandonment, which is explicit verbal statement, as in other rights. So in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyya. [3] His saying: 'And by delaying it it is not invalidated...' means pre-emption is not lost by delaying this demand. (1) This is the apparent narration, and in al-Hidaya (4:28), al-Multaqa (p. 178), ad-Durr al-Mukhtar (5:144), al-Ghurar (2:210), and Tanwir al-Absar (p.203): upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage deals with the procedures for claiming the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) in Hanafi law. The author explains three sequential demands the pre-emptor must make to secure his right. The first is talab al-muwathabah (the immediate demand): upon first hearing of the sale, the pre-emptor must immediately express his desire to take the property. The second is talab al-ishhad (the demand of bearing witness): he must then go to the property or to the seller/buyer and formally declare his claim before witnesses, saying 'I am the pre-emptor, I demanded pre-emption, and I now demand it again.' The third is talab al-tamlik wa al-khusumah (the demand of ownership and litigation): he must go before a judge and request that the judge order the property transferred to him. The passage then focuses on an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of delaying this third demand. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, delaying the judicial demand does not nullify the right of pre-emption, because a right once established is not lost except by explicit waiver. Imam Muhammad holds that if the pre-emptor delays taking the matter to court for more than one month after the second demand (ishhad), his right lapses; this is to protect the buyer from indefinite uncertainty that would prevent him from freely using the property. The adopted opinion for fatwa, however, is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as stated in several authoritative Hanafi works.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption: the right of a co-owner to purchase the share of a new buyer in the property under certain conditions.
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathabah): The immediate demand: the pre-emptor must verbally express his intention to claim pre-emption as soon as he learns of the sale.
طلب الإشهاد (talab al-ishhad): The demand of bearing witness: the pre-emptor goes to the property or the parties and declares his claim before witnesses.
طلب التمليك والخصومة (talab al-tamlik wa al-khusumah): The demand of ownership and litigation: the pre-emptor formally requests a judge to order transfer of the property to him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school, what happens if the pre-emptor delays the third demand (talab al-tamlik wa al-khusumah) indefinitely?
A) The right of pre-emption is nullified immediately.
B) The right is lost after one month.
C) The right is not lost; it can be claimed at any time.
D) The right is lost only if the pre-emptor dies.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires three sequential demands for shuf'ah, but delaying the final judicial demand does not nullify the right; only explicit abandonment does.

Liability in Preemption: Seller vs Buyer
Ruling on hearing testimony and liability before delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on liability in preemption and explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, identifying the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In preemption, liability shifts from seller to buyer upon delivery; before delivery, the seller remains liable.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a critical procedural question in preemption: when the preemptor exercises his right, who bears liability for defects or claims? The answer depends on whether delivery has occurred. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the period before and after delivery, while the Shafi'i school places liability on the buyer in all cases. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly advising clients in property disputes.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi rule: before delivery of the sold property to the buyer, liability (al-uhdah) is on the seller; after delivery, it shifts to the buyer. Also, testimony is not heard until the buyer is present if the property is still with the seller.
The text explicitly says 'al-uhdah 'ala al-ba'i' (liability on the seller) before delivery.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school holds that liability is on the buyer in all circumstances, whether the property is taken from the seller or the buyer. Their reasoning is that rights of the slave (i.e., the buyer) revert to the owner.
The text mentions al-Shafi'i's view: 'al-uhdah 'ala al-mushtari bi-kulli hal' (liability on the buyer absolutely).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof is based on the principle that the buyer's rights follow him; he is the ultimate owner once the sale is concluded, so any defect claim should be against him.
The source does not elaborate, but the reference to 'rights of the slave' indicates a strong buyer-centric view.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that before delivery, the contract is not complete with respect to the preemptor. The preemptor takes the property as if buying from the seller, so the seller remains liable until delivery is made. After delivery, the seller becomes a stranger and liability shifts.
The text analogises to loss before delivery: if the property perishes before delivery, the contract is rescinded, implying the seller bears risk.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a defect appears before delivery, the preemptor can sue the seller in Hanafi, but must sue the buyer in Shafi'i. This affects who is liable for compensation.
This difference is crucial for litigation strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسمع البيِّنة عليه حتى يحضر المشتري فيفسح بحضوره ويقضى للشفيع بالشفعة، والعهدة على البائع [1] (ولا يسمع البيِّنة عليه حتى يحضر المشتري فيفسح بحضوره)، إنَّما يشترط حضور البائع والمشتري ; لأنَّ الملك له ، واليد للبائع ، فإذا سَلَّمَ إلى المشتري لا يشترط حضور البائع ; لأنَّهُ صارَ أجنبياً ، ويقضى للشفيع بالشفعة، والعهدة على البائع" (۱) ) ، حتى يجب تسليمُ الدَّارِ على البائع ، وعند الاستحقاق يكون عهدة الثّمن على البائع ، فيطلب منه. [1] قوله : حضور البائع والمشتري ... الخ ؛ فلا بُدّ من اجتماعهما ; لأنَّ القضاء على الغائب لا يجوز; لأنَّ أخذه من يد البائع يوجب فوات المبيع قبل القبض، وفواتُه قبل القبض يوجب الفسخ ; لكونه قبل تمامه. كما إذا هلك قبل القبض ، ولا يجوزُ الفسخ عليهما إلا بحضرتهما، بخلاف ما بعد القبض، حيث لا يشترط حضور البائع ; لأنَّ العقد قد انتهى بالتسليم وصار البائع أجنبياً عنهما. كذا في «البحر الرائق ) (۲) [٢]قوله : والعهدة على البائع ... الخ ؛ يعني قبل تسليم المبيع إلى المشتري، وأما بعده فلا ريب في أنّ العهدة على المشتري. كما صرّح به في «الهداية»(۳)، فعبارة المصنف لا تخلو من نوع إخلال، فليتأمل. وقال الشافعي له : العهدة على المشتري بكل حال سواء يأخذها من يد البائع أو من يد المشتري ; لأن عنده حقوق العبد ترجع الى المالك. كذا في «الكفاية» (٤) ، قاله جلبي
And testimony shall not be heard against him until the buyer is present, then the court is adjourned with his presence, and the preemptor is decreed the preemption, and the liability is on the seller. [1] (And testimony shall not be heard against him until the buyer is present, then the court is adjourned with his presence), indeed the presence of the seller and buyer is required because ownership is for the seller and possession is with the seller. When he delivers to the buyer, the seller's presence is not required because he becomes a stranger. And the preemptor is decreed the preemption, and the liability is on the seller. So the delivery of the house is incumbent upon the seller, and upon claim the liability of the price is on the seller, so it is demanded from him. [1] His statement: presence of the seller and buyer... etc.; so their meeting is necessary because judgment against an absent person is not permissible; because taking from the seller's hand causes loss of the sold item before delivery, and its loss before delivery necessitates rescission because it is before completion, just as if it perished before delivery. And rescission is not permissible against them except in their presence, unlike after delivery, where the seller's presence is not required because the contract has ended with delivery and the seller becomes a stranger to them. Thus in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. [2] His statement: and the liability is on the seller... etc.; means before delivery of the sold item to the buyer; as for after, there is no doubt that the liability is on the buyer. As stated in al-Hidayah, so the author's expression is not free from a kind of deficiency, so let it be considered. And al-Shafi'i said: The liability is on the buyer absolutely, whether he takes it from the seller's hand or from the buyer's hand, because in his view the rights of the slave revert to the owner. Thus in al-Kifayah, said by Jalabi.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the liability (al-uhdah) in preemption. The text begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: testimony is not heard against the preemptor until the buyer is present. This is because judgment cannot be passed on an absent person. The presence of both seller and buyer is initially required because ownership is with the seller and possession is with him. However, once the seller delivers the property to the buyer, the seller becomes a stranger to the transaction, so his presence is no longer necessary. The preemptor is then decreed the preemption. The liability (for defects, etc.) is on the seller before delivery; after delivery, it shifts to the buyer. The commentator notes that the author's phrasing may be slightly deficient, but al-Hidayah clarifies that the liability on the seller applies only before delivery. Then the Shafi'i position is presented: al-Shafi'i holds that liability is on the buyer in all cases, whether the preemptor takes the property from the seller's hand or the buyer's hand, because in his school, rights of a slave (i.e., the buyer) revert to the owner. The Hanafi response is implicit: the liability is on the seller before delivery because the contract is not yet complete with respect to the preemptor.

Key Terms
العهدة (al-uhdah): Liability or responsibility for defects or claims in a sale; obligation to indemnify if the property is taken away.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Preemption: the right of a co-owner to purchase a sold share from a third party.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): Judgment against an absent person; generally not permissible in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who bears the liability (al-uhdah) when the preemptor takes the property from the seller before delivery?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) Both equally
D) The preemptor

Key Takeaway
In preemption, liability rests with the seller until delivery; after delivery, it shifts to the buyer.

Preemptor's Options and Price Disputes in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on the preemptor's options and price disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the preemptor's options of sight and defect, and evaluate the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on conflicting price testimonies.

Core Idea
A preemptor's rights are independent of the buyer's stipulations, and conflicting price testimonies are reconciled by presuming separate transactions.

Preemption (shuf'ah) gives a co-owner or neighbor the right to take a sold property by paying the price. This lesson examines two critical aspects: first, the preemptor retains the options of sight and defect even if the buyer waived them; second, when the preemptor and buyer disagree on the price and both present witnesses, the Hanafi school internally disagrees on how to resolve the conflict. The source passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the reasoning behind the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the matn
The base text states: (1) the preemptor has the option of sight and defect even if the buyer stipulated immunity; (2) in a price dispute, the buyer is believed, but if both have testimonies, the preemptor is more entitled.
These are the two key legal questions covered.
2
Understand the analogy to purchase
The commentary explains that taking by preemption is like a purchase (mubadala mal bi mal), so the same options exist as in a regular sale. The preemptor is not a deputy of the buyer, so the buyer cannot drop these options for him.
This analogy (qiyas) justifies the preemptor's options.
3
Analyze the conflicting testimonies scenario
When both the preemptor and buyer present conflicting testimonies about the price, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the preemptor may take at the lower price because both testimonies can be true if two separate contracts occurred. Abu Yusuf prefers the buyer's testimony as more probative.
The key is whether the two testimonies can be reconciled.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the positions: Abu Hanifa & Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf. Note the dalil for each: possibility of two contracts vs. greater probative force. The Hanafi response: the preemptor's right is strong, so both testimonies are considered valid for him.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is the majority view and supported by the possibility of reconciling the testimonies. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Contrast with the opposite scenario of seller-buyer dispute where only one contract is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة تعريفها وأنواعها وأحكامها
وللشفيع خيار الرؤيةِ والعيب ، وإن شرط المشتري البراءة عنه، وإن اختلف الشفيع والمشتري في الثَّمن صُدِّقَ المشتري ولو برهنا، فالشفيع أحق
(وللشفيع خيار الرؤية والعيب ، وإن شرط المشتري البراءة عنه، وإن اختلف الشفيع والمشتري في الثَّمن صُدِّقَ المشتري ) : أي مع الحلف، لأنَّ الشفيع يدعي استحقاق الدَّارِ عند نقدِ الأقل والمشتري ينكره.
(ولو برهنا، فالشفيع أحق) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ومحمد ، وحجتهما له، ما ذكرنا، وأيضاً : يمكن صدق البينتين بجريان العقدِ مرَّتين، فيأخذ الشفيع بالأقل) ، وعند أبي يوسف له بينةُ المشتري أحقُّ لأنها أكثر إثباتاً.
١ ] قوله : وللشفيع ... الخ ؛ لأن الأخذ بالشفعة بمنزلة الشراء، ألا يرى أنه مبادلة المال بالمال، فيثبت فيه الخياران كما في الشراء، ولا يسقط بشرط البراءة من المشتري ، ولا برؤيته ؛ لأنه ليس بنائب عنه، فلا يملك إسقاطه. كذا في «الهداية»(۳). [٢]قوله : صدق المشتري ؛ أي صدّق المشتري مع الحلف كما قال الشارح ولا يتحالفان ; لأنَّ الشفيع إن كان يدعي عليه استحقاق الدار، فالمشتري لا يدعي عليه شبهة لتخيّره من الترك والأخذ ، ولا نص هاهنا فلا يتحالفان. كذا في «الهداية». (۳) قوله : وأيضاً ; وهذا دليل ثان لهما، وتلخيصه ; لأنه لا تنافي بين البينتين في حق الشفيع الجواز تحقق البيع مرّة بألف وأخرى بألفين على ما شهد عليه البينتان، وفسخ أحدهما بالآخر لا يظهر في حق الشفيع، لتأكد حقه، فيجمع بينهما، فجاز أن يجعله موجودين في حقه وله أن يأخذ بأيهما شاء. (۳) وهذا بخلاف البائع والمشتري ; لأنهما لا يتوالى بينهما عقدان إلا بانفساخ الأوّل فالجمع بينهما غير ممكن، فيصار إلى أكثرهما إثباتاً ; لأنّ المصير إلى الترجيح عند : التوفيق تعذر
The Book of Preemption: its definition, types, and rulings. And the preemptor has the option of sight and defect, even if the buyer stipulated immunity from it. And if the preemptor and the buyer disagree over the price, the buyer is believed, even if there are two testimonies, then the preemptor is more entitled. (And the preemptor has the option of sight and defect, even if the buyer stipulated immunity from it. And if the preemptor and the buyer disagree over the price, the buyer is believed): i.e., with oath, because the preemptor claims entitlement to the house upon payment of the lesser amount and the buyer denies it. (And even if there are two testimonies, the preemptor is more entitled): This is according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) and Muhammad, and their proof is what we mentioned, and also: it is possible that both testimonies are true by the occurrence of the contract twice, so the preemptor takes at the lesser. And according to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him), the buyer's testimony is more entitled because it is more probative. [1] His statement: And for the preemptor... etc.; because taking by preemption is like purchase. Do you not see that it is an exchange of property for property, so both options are established as in purchase, and they are not dropped by the condition of immunity from the buyer, nor by his sight; because he is not his deputy, so he cannot drop them. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: believed the buyer; i.e., the buyer is believed with an oath as the commentator said, and they do not take mutual oaths, because the preemptor claims entitlement to the house, and the buyer does not claim anything against him due to his option to leave or take, and there is no text here, so they do not take mutual oaths. Thus in al-Hidayah. (3) His statement: and also; and this is a second proof for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and its summary: because there is no conflict between the two testimonies with respect to the preemptor, as it is possible that the contract occurred once for a thousand and another for two thousand as witnessed by the two testimonies, and the rescission of one by the other does not appear with respect to the preemptor, due to the strength of his right, so both are combined, thus it is permissible to consider both as existing in his right, and he may take whichever he wishes. And this is unlike the seller and buyer, because two contracts cannot occur between them except by the rescission of the first, so combining them is not possible, so resort is made to the more probative one, because resort to preference is when reconciliation is impossible.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the preemptor's options and the treatment of conflicting testimonies. The matn states that the preemptor has the option of sight and defect, even if the buyer stipulated immunity. This is because preemption is analogous to purchase: it is an exchange of property for property, so the same options exist. The preemptor is not a deputy of the buyer, so the buyer cannot drop these options on his behalf. On the dispute over price, the buyer's statement is taken with an oath, because the preemptor claims a lower price than what the buyer asserts. The preemptor does not take a mutual oath because there is no reciprocal claim. Then the text addresses the scenario where two conflicting testimonies are presented: one showing a lower price, another a higher price. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the preemptor is more entitled and may take at the lower price, because both testimonies could be true if two separate contracts occurred. The preemptor's right is strong, so both are considered existing in his favor. However, Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer's testimony (the higher price) should be preferred because it is more probative (more in number? or more confirming? Actually, it says because it is more probative, likely meaning the buyer's proof is stronger). This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The passage concludes by contrasting this with a dispute between buyer and seller themselves, where only one contract is possible, so preference is given to the stronger proof.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option of sight: the right to cancel a sale after seeing the item if it was bought unseen.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option of defect: the right to return an item if a defect is discovered.
البراءة (al-bara'ah): Immunity or disclaimer: the buyer stipulating that the seller is not liable for defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Pay the higher price as determined by the buyer's witnesses
B) Take the property at the lower price
C) Reject both testimonies and settle at market value
D) Wait for the judge to decide

Key Takeaway
The preemptor's options of sight and defect are independent of the buyer's stipulations, and in price disputes with conflicting testimonies, the preemptor may take at the lower price per the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).

Preemption When Dhimmi Buys with Prohibited Items: Payment Method for Muslim and Dhimmi Shafi'i
Ruling on preemption compensation type based on shafi'i's religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on how a dhimmi and a Muslim shafi'i must compensate a dhimmi buyer who paid with wine and pig, and explain the legal reason (ta'lil) behind the difference.

Core Idea
Wine is fungible (mithli) for dhimmis but valued (qimi) for Muslims — the shafi'i's status determines the compensation.

Preemption (shuf'ah) allows a co-owner or neighbour to take a sold property by paying its price. But what if the buyer paid with prohibited items like wine or a pig? The ruling depends on whether the shafi'i is a Muslim or a dhimmi, because of their differing legal capacity to own and deliver such items. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the two distinct compensation methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The act is a property sale where a dhimmi buyer pays with wine and a pig. Two potential shafi'is exist: a dhimmi or a Muslim. Determine the ruling for each.
The buyer's dhimmi status is fixed; the shafi'i's religion changes the outcome.
2
Classify the items as mithli or qimi
For a dhimmi, wine is mithli (fungible) because they can legally possess and deliver it. For a Muslim, wine is qimi (non-fungible) because possession and delivery are impossible (haram). A pig is qimi for everyone in this context.
The classification of wine depends on the party's legal capacity.
3
Apply the compensation rule
A shafi'i must give the same type of consideration the buyer gave. For the dhimmi shafi'i: give like wine (mithl al-khamr) and the value of the pig. For the Muslim shafi'i: give the value (qimah) of both wine and pig, because he cannot deliver wine or pig.
When the shafi'i is Muslim, wine is treated as qimi, so its value is given.
4
Exclude the apostate
An apostate (murtadd) has no preemption rights at all, regardless of the sale conditions, because his property is not transmissible.
This is an exception mentioned in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي شراء ذمي بخمر أو خِنزير، والشفيع ذمّي بمثل الخمر وقيمة الخنزير، والشفيع المسلم بقيمة كلّ. قوله : والشفيع ذمي؛ قال في «العناية»: هذا احتراز عما إذا كان مرتداً فإنّه لا شفعة له، سواء قتل على الردّة أو مات أو لحق بدار الحرب، ولأ ورثة له؛ لأنها لا تورث. قاله جلبي. قوله : بقيمة كل..الخ؛ أما الخنزير فظاهر، فإنّه من ذواتِ القيم، وكذا الخمر لامتناع التسليم والتسلّم في حق المسلم، فالتحق بغير المثلي، وإن كان شفيعاً مسلماً أو ذميّاً أخذ المسلم نصفها بنصف قيمة الخمر، والذمي نصفها بنصف مثل الخمر اعتباراً للبعض بالكل.
And when a dhimmi buys with wine or pig, and the shafi'i is a dhimmi, he takes like the wine and the value of the pig, and the Muslim shafi'i takes the value of each. His statement: 'And the shafi'i is a dhimmi'; he said in al-'Inayah: this is a precaution for when he is an apostate, for he has no preemption, whether he is killed for apostasy or died or joined the abode of war, and he has no heirs because it is not inherited. Jilabi said. His statement: 'the value of each'... as for the pig, it is clear, for it is among things measured by value, and similarly wine due to the impossibility of delivery and taking in the right of a Muslim, so it is attached to non-mithli, and if the shafi'i is Muslim or dhimmi, the Muslim takes half its value of wine, and the dhimmi takes half like the wine, considering the part for the whole.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding preemption (shuf'ah) when a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of Islamic state) purchases property in exchange for wine or a pig, which are prohibited for Muslims. The commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the details: If the shafi'i (pre-emptor) is also a dhimmi, he takes the property by giving the purchaser like amount of wine (mithl al-khamr) and the value of the pig. If the shafi'i is a Muslim, he gives the value (qimah) of both wine and pig, because he cannot possess or deliver wine or pig. The commentary further excludes an apostate from preemption rights entirely, whether he dies as apostate or becomes a murtadd. The value reasoning: wine is treated as a non-fungible (qimi) for Muslims due to prohibition, so it is valued. For a dhimmi, wine is fungible (mithli) because he can possess and deliver it. When the shafi'i is Muslim and takes half the property, he pays half the value of the wine; if dhimmi, half the like amount.

Key Terms
شفيع (shafi'i): The person entitled to preemption (shuf'ah) in a property sale.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state.
مثل الخمر (mithl al-khamr): The like quantity and quality of wine, i.e., fungible replacement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dhimmi buys a house with wine and a pig, and the shafi'i is a Muslim, how does the shafi'i take the property?
A) By giving like wine and value of pig
B) By giving value of both wine and pig
C) By giving like wine and like pig
D) He cannot take because the sale is invalid

Key Takeaway
A Muslim shafi'i must pay the value of prohibited items; a dhimmi shafi'i may deliver the like of wine but must pay the value of a pig.

The Shafi'i's Options for Improvements by the Purchaser
Ruling on shafi'i's options for improvements made by buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the options available to the shafi'i when the purchaser has built or planted on the property, and evaluate the reasoning behind the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The shafi'i's right is stronger: he can demand removal of improvements or take at value as removed—even without permission.

This passage addresses a key question in shuf'ah (preemption): what happens when the purchaser has added buildings or plants to the property before the shafi'i exercises his right? The Hanafi school presents an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his student Abu Yusuf, with the latter aligning with the Shafi'i view. Understanding this dispute reveals how the school weighs competing interests—the shafi'i's established right vs. the purchaser's investment.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the two options the shafi'i has under Abu Hanifa: (1) take the property at the sale price plus the value of improvements as if they were removed (qal'), or (2) require the purchaser to physically remove them.
The value 'as removed' (mukalla' bi-qīma mawlū'a) refers to the salvage value, not the construction cost.
2
Identify the opposing view
Abu Yusuf (and al-Shafi'i) hold that the shafi'i cannot force removal. Instead, he must either take the property at the price plus the value of improvements in place, or leave the property altogether.
This view is based on the idea that the purchaser is muḥiqq (rightful) in building.
3
Analyse the opponent's proof
The opponent argues that requiring removal (qal') is a ruling of transgression (ḥukm al-ʿudwān). Since the purchaser owned the land at the time of construction, he acted lawfully; forcing demolition would cause him excessive harm.
The principle is that harm should not be inflicted on a person who is not a transgressor.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: the purchaser built in a place where a strong right of the shafi'i was attached (mutal'allaq) without permission from the right-holder. This is analogous to a pledgor building on pledged property without the pledgee's consent. The shafi'i's right is stronger, so the rule repels the greater harm (loss of the right) by bearing the lesser (compensation for improvements).
The analogy to rahn (pledge) highlights that the right of the shafi'i supercedes the purchaser's ownership.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the shafi'i can demand demolition of the building or removal of plants. Under Abu Yusuf/Shafi'i, he cannot; he must accept the improvements in place and pay their current value, or walk away from the preemption right.
This difference affects the feasibility of preemption: requiring removal can be a strong deterrent for the purchaser.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي بناء المشتري وغرسه بالثّمن وقيمة البناء والغرس مقلوعين، كما في الغصب، وإن شاء كُلف المشتري قلعهما. أي أخذ الشفيع فيما إذا بنى المشتري أو غرس بالثَّمن وقيمتهما مقلوعَيْن، أو كُلّفَ المشتري قلع البناء أو الغرس، والمراد بقيمتهما مقلوعين قيمتهما مستحقي القلع، كما مرَّ في «الغصب»، وعن أبي يوسف له : أنه لا يكلف بالقلم، بل يخير بين أن يأخذ بالثّمن وقيمة البناء والغرس، وبين أن يترك، وهـو قـول الـشَّافِعِي) ه؛ لأنَّ التكليف بالقلع من أحكام العدوان والمشتري هنا محق في البناء، قلنا : بنى في موضع تعلق به حق متاكد من غير تسليط وصار كالموهوب له والمشترى شراء فاسداً، وكما إذا زرع المشتري فإنّه لا يكلف بالقلع؛ وهذا لأنّ في إيجاب الأخذ بالقيمة دفع أعلى الضررين بتحمل الأدنى، فيصار إليه. ووجهها في «المتن» على ما قال الشارح له إنه بني في محل تعلّق به حق متأكد للغير من غير تسليط من جهةِ مَن له الحق فينتقض كالراهن إذا بنى في المرهون؛ وهذا لأن حقه أقوى من حق المشتري؛ لأنه يتقدَّمُ عليه؛ ولهذا ينقض بيعه وهبتُه وغيره من تصرفاته بخلاف الهبة والشراء الفاسد؛ لأنه حصل بتسليط من جهة مَن له الحق، وزيادة التفصيل في «الهداية». ورجع الشفيع بالثمن فقط إن بنى أو غرس، ثم استحق. يعني لو أخذها الشفيع فبنى فيها، أو غرس ثمّ استحق رجع الشفيع بالثمن على المشتري أو على البائع؛ لأنه تبين أن الشفيع أخذه بغيرِ حق، ولا يرجع الشفيع بقيمة البناء والغرس، لا على البائع إن أخذها منه، ولا على المشتري إن أخذها منه.
And regarding the building of the purchaser and his planting, he [the shafi'i] takes at the price and the value of the building and planting as if removed, and if he wishes, the purchaser is required to remove them. That is, the shafi'i takes when the purchaser built or planted at the price and their value as removed, or the purchaser is required to remove the building or planting. What is meant by 'their value as removed' is their value when deserving of removal, as passed in 'al-Ghasb'. And from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) that he is not required to remove, but rather he chooses between taking at the price and the value of the building and planting, and leaving, and that is the statement of al-Shafi'i). Because requiring removal is among the rulings of transgression, and the purchaser here is rightful in the building. We said: He built in a place where a strong right of another is attached without permission. And it becomes like the donee and the one who buys in a corrupt sale, and like when the purchaser plants, he is not required to remove. This is because in making the taking with value mandatory, it is a repulsion of the greater harm by bearing the lesser, so it is resorted to. And its justification in the text according to what the commentator said: He built in a place where a strong right of another is attached without permission of the one who has the right, so it is annulled like the pledgor when he builds in the pledged property. This is because his right is stronger than the right of the purchaser, for it supercedes his. For this reason his sale, gift, and other transactions are annulled, unlike the gift and the corrupt sale, because they happened with the permission of the one who has the right. And additional detail is in 'al-Hidayah'. And the shafi'i recovers only the price if he built or planted and then the property is claimed. Meaning, if the shafi'i took it and built in it or planted and then it was claimed, the shafi'i recovers the price from the purchaser or from the seller, because it becomes clear that the shafi'i took it without right. And the shafi'i does not recover the value of the building and planting, neither from the seller if he took it from him, nor from the purchaser if he took it from him.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the shafi'i's options when the purchaser has built or planted on the property before the shafi'i exercises his preemption right. The matn gives the shafi'i two options: (1) take the property at the sale price plus the value of the building/planting as if they were to be removed, or (2) require the purchaser to remove them. The commentary explains that the value is calculated as the value when they are 'deserving of removal' (mustahiq al-qal'), similar to the rules in ghasb (usurpation). Abu Yusuf (and al-Shafi'i) hold that the purchaser cannot be forced to remove; instead, the shafi'i must either take at the price plus the value of improvements in place or leave the property. The Hanafi response (i.e., Abu Hanifa's position) is that the purchaser built in a place where a strong right of the shafi'i was attached without permission from the one having that right. Analogy is made to the pledgor building in the pledged property (right of mortgagee) and to a donee or buyer in a corrupt sale (where the right of the recipient is weaker). The Hanafi argument is that this rule repels the greater harm (loss of right) by bearing the lesser (compensation for improvements). The passage also covers a separate ruling: if the shafi'i takes the property, builds or plants, and then the property is claimed (e.g., a third party proves ownership), the shafi'i recovers only the purchase price from the seller or purchaser, not the value of improvements, because his possession was without right.

Key Terms
قلع (qal'): Physical removal of a building or plants from the land.
استحقاق (istihqaq): A claim by a third party proving ownership of the property after the shafi'i has taken possession.
الشفيع بالثمن فقط (al-shafi'i bil-thaman faqat): The shafi'i recovers only the purchase price he paid, not the value of improvements, if the property is later claimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what options does the shafi'i have if the purchaser has built on the property before preemption?
A) Only take at the price plus value of building as removed.
B) Either require the purchaser to remove the building or take at price plus value as removed.
C) Only leave the property.
D) Take the property at the original price only (no compensation for building).

Key Takeaway
The shafi'i under Abu Hanifa can either take with compensation for improvements as removed or require the purchaser to demolish them, reflecting the strength of his preemptive right.

Preemptor Recovers Only Price, Not Building Value
Hanafi ruling with proof on preemptor's recovery after reclamation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the preemptor recovers only the purchase price when land taken by shuf‘a is later reclaimed, and explain the legal reasoning based on the nature of his possession (jabr vs. permission).

Core Idea
The preemptor recovers only the price because his possession is by compulsion, not consent.

When a preemptor (shafi') takes land by shuf‘a and builds or plants on it, and a third party later reclaims the land (proving rightful ownership), what does the preemptor recover? The Hanafi school rules that he recovers only the price he paid—not the value of his building or plantation. This lesson unpacks the proof and contrast with the Shafi‘i position that would require demolition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the opening line: 'ورجع الشفيع بالثّمن فقط إن بني أو غرس، ثم استحق'. The preemptor recovers only the price, not the value of building or plantation. Note that this applies when the land is reclaimed after he built/planted.
The ruling is specific to shuf‘a reclamation, not a general buyer scenario.
2
Understand the central legal distinction
The commentator contrasts the preemptor with a buyer: the buyer built with the seller's permission (taslit), so if the buyer's land is reclaimed, he may recover building value from his seller (as a deceived buyer). The preemptor took the land by force (jabr), without permission. Hence, he is not entitled to building value.
Key concept: 'بنى في موضع تعلق به حق متاكد من غير تسليط' – built where a strong right existed but without permission.
3
Examine the Shafi‘i objection and Hanafi response
The Shafi‘i school argues that the preemptor must demolish his building because he built without permission (aggression). The Hanafi response is that the preemptor had a strong right (haqq muta'akkid) akin to a mortgagor building on mortgaged property, so his building was not aggression.
The Hanafi school uses analogy (qiyas) to the case of a mortgagor who builds with the mortgagee's implicit permission.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Shafi‘i: preemptor must demolish at his own cost. Hanafi: preemptor leaves building intact, recovers only price. This difference affects real property disputes.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is the Hanafi majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورجع الشفيع بالثّمن فقط إن بني أو غرس، ثم استحق. (وقوله: لأن التكليف بالقلع من أحكام العدوان والمشتري هنا محق في البناء، وهـو قـول الـشَّافِعِي) ه؛ لأنَّ التكليف بالقلع من أحكام العدوان والمشتري هنا محق في البناء، قلنا: بنى في موضع تعلق به حق متاكد من غير تسليط. ورجع الشفيع بالثّمن فقط إن بني أو غرس، ثم استحق: أي إن أخذ الشفيع بالشفعة وبنى أو غرس، ثُمَّ استحقت الأرض رجع بالثمن فقط. وبكل الثمن إن خربت، أو جف الشجر. وأخذ العرصة لا النقض بحصتها إن هدم المشتري البناء. وعن أبي يوسف: إنّه يرجعُ على بائعه بقيمة البناء؛ لأنه متملك عليه، فنزلا منزلة البائع والمشتري. قوله: بخلاف المشتري؛ إشارة إلى الجواب عن فتوى أبي يوسف برجوع القيمة فيهما، بناءً على أنّ الشفيع من أخذها منه صار كالمشتري المغرور من جهة البائع. قوله: وبكل الثمن... الخ؛ يعني إذا انهدمت الدار أو أحرق بناؤها أو جف شجر البستان بغير فعل أحدٍ فالشفيع بالخيار إن أخذها بجميع الثمن؛ لأنّ البناء والغرس تابع، حتى دخلا في البيع من غير ذكر، فلا يقابلها شيء من الثمن ما لم يصر مقصوداً؛ وذلك لأن قيام البناء بالأرض كقيام الوصف. وفواتُ الوصف لا يسقط شيئاً من الثمن إذا كان بآفة سماوية؛ لأن الثمن مقابلة الأصل دون الوصف، وإن شاء ترك؛ لأنّ للشفيع أن يمتنع عن تملك الدار بماله. قوله: وأخذ العرصة...الخ؛ يعني إن نقض المشتري البناء قيل للشفيع: إن شئت فخذ العرصة بحقها من الثمن، وإن شئت فدع؛ لأنه صار مقصوداً بالإتلاف.
And the preemptor recovers only the price if he built or planted, then [the land] was reclaimed. (His saying: because the obligation to demolish is from the rulings of aggression, and the buyer here is rightful in building. This is the saying of al-Shafi'i) because the obligation to demolish is from the rulings of aggression, and the buyer here is rightful in building. We said: He built in a place to which a strong right of another is attached without permission. And the preemptor recovers only the price if he built or planted, then [the land] was reclaimed: meaning if the preemptor took by preemption and built or planted, then the land was reclaimed, he recovers only the price. And by the entire price if it [the building] collapsed or the trees dried up. And he takes the land, not the rubble for its share, if the buyer demolished the building. And from Abu Yusuf: He recovers from his seller the value of the building because he is possessed [of it], so they are placed in the position of seller and buyer. His saying: unlike the buyer; an indication of the answer to the fatwa of Abu Yusuf about recovery of value in both, based on that the preemptor from whom he took became like the deceived buyer from the seller. His saying: and by the entire price... meaning if the house collapsed or its building burnt or the trees of the orchard dried up without anyone's action, the preemptor has the option: if he takes it, [he takes] with the entire price because the building and planting are appurtenant, they enter the sale without mention, so nothing of the price is allocated to them unless they become the object; and that is because the standing of the building on the land is like the description. And the loss of description does not reduce anything of the price if it is by a natural calamity, because the price is in exchange for the essence not the description. And if he wishes, he can leave it because the preemptor may refuse to own the house with his wealth. His saying: and he takes the land... meaning if the buyer demolished the building, it is said to the preemptor: if you wish, take the land with its right of the price, and if you wish, leave it, because it became intended by destruction.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling with proof on the rights of the preemptor (shafi') when he builds or plants on land taken by shuf'a and the land is later reclaimed (istihqaq). The core ruling is that the preemptor recovers only the purchase price (thaman), not the value of the building or plantation. The commentary explains that the preemptor, unlike the buyer, is not compelled to demolish his building because the buyer built with permission (taslit) from the seller, while the preemptor took the land by force (jabr). However, since the preemptor's right is strong (haqq muta'akkid), he is not treated as an aggressor. The passage then covers two special cases: (1) If the building collapses or trees dry up by natural causes before the preemptor exercises his option, he must pay the full price if he takes the land, because the building is appurtenant and its loss does not reduce the price. (2) If the buyer deliberately demolishes the building, the preemptor takes only the land (al-'arsa) with its share of the price, not the rubble, because the building no longer exists as an appurtenance. The passage also reports an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that the preemptor can recover the value of the building from his own seller (the one who sold the land to him), analogizing the preemptor to a deceived buyer (mughtarr). The commentary refutes this, noting that the preemptor is not deceived by the seller but took by force. The reliance (mufta bihi) is the majority Hanafi position that the preemptor recovers only the price.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf‘a): The right of preemption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by substitution.
استحقاق (istihqāq): Reclamation: a third party proves rightful ownership of the land, voiding the sale.
حق متاكد (haqq muta'akkid): A strong right that is almost ownership, such as the preemptor's right after taking by shuf'a.
تسليط (taslīṭ): Permission or authorization given by the rightful owner to another to act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When land taken by shuf‘a is later reclaimed, what does the preemptor recover if he built on it?
A) Full price plus building value
B) Only the price, not building value
C) Only building value
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The preemptor recovers only the price because his possession is by compulsion, not by permission; the building is appurtenant and its loss by natural calamity does not reduce the price.

8 min
Special Cases and Abu Yusuf's View on Building Value
Hanafi ruling with proof on special cases and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings when the building is destroyed naturally versus when the buyer demolishes it, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Yusuf and the majority regarding recovery of building value from the seller.

Core Idea
Natural loss doesn't reduce price; buyer's demolition changes option; Abu Yusuf grants building value from seller.

Beyond the core ruling, the passage addresses two special scenarios: what happens if the building is destroyed by a natural calamity? And what if the buyer (who sold the land to the preemptor) intentionally demolishes the building? Additionally, an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is reported: Abu Yusuf allows the preemptor to recover building value from his seller, analogizing him to a deceived buyer. This lesson unpacks these details.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the case of natural destruction
If the building collapses or trees dry up without anyone's action, the preemptor has an option: take the land at the full price or leave it. The reason: the building is an appurtenance (tabi') and enters the sale without allocation of price. Loss of an appurtenance by natural cause does not reduce the price because the price was for the essence (asl), not the description.
Key phrase: 'قيام البناء بالأرض كقيام الوصف' – building is like a description of the land.
2
Examine the case of buyer demolition
If the buyer (the person from whom the preemptor took the land by shuf'a) demolishes the building, the preemptor does not get the rubble. Instead, he is given an option: take only the land (al-'arsa) with its share of the price, or leave it entirely. The building, once demolished, is no longer an appurtenance; it becomes an intended object (maqsud) by the destruction.
This differs from natural destruction where the building remains as a ruin (still appurtenant).
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's position on building value
Abu Yusuf (one of the Sahibayn) holds that the preemptor can recover the value of the building from his seller (the one who sold the land to him). His reasoning: the preemptor is like a 'deceived buyer' (mughtarr) – he thought he owned the land, built in good faith, and the seller's defective title caused the loss.
Abu Yusuf's view is reported but not mufta bihi.
4
Analyze the majority's refutation
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) reject Abu Yusuf's analogy. They argue that the preemptor is not deceived by his seller; he took the land by his own right of shuf'a, which is a strong right (haqq muta'akkid). He built on his own authority, not relying on the seller's consent. Hence, he cannot claim from his seller.
The commentator says 'بخلاف المشتري' – unlike a buyer, indicating the distinction.

And the preemptor recovers only the price if he built or planted, then [the land] was reclaimed. (His saying: because the obligation to demolish is from the rulings of aggression, and the buyer here is rightful in building. This is the saying of al-Shafi'i) because the obligation to demolish is from the rulings of aggression, and the buyer here is rightful in building. We said: He built in a place to which a strong right of another is attached without permission. And the preemptor recovers only the price if he built or planted, then [the land] was reclaimed: meaning if the preemptor took by preemption and built or planted, then the land was reclaimed, he recovers only the price. And by the entire price if it [the building] collapsed or the trees dried up. And he takes the land, not the rubble for its share, if the buyer demolished the building. And from Abu Yusuf: He recovers from his seller the value of the building because he is possessed [of it], so they are placed in the position of seller and buyer. His saying: unlike the buyer; an indication of the answer to the fatwa of Abu Yusuf about recovery of value in both, based on that the preemptor from whom he took became like the deceived buyer from the seller. His saying: and by the entire price... meaning if the house collapsed or its building burnt or the trees of the orchard dried up without anyone's action, the preemptor has the option: if he takes it, [he takes] with the entire price because the building and planting are appurtenant, they enter the sale without mention, so nothing of the price is allocated to them unless they become the object; and that is because the standing of the building on the land is like the description. And the loss of description does not reduce anything of the price if it is by a natural calamity, because the price is in exchange for the essence not the description. And if he wishes, he can leave it because the preemptor may refuse to own the house with his wealth. His saying: and he takes the land... meaning if the buyer demolished the building, it is said to the preemptor: if you wish, take the land with its right of the price, and if you wish, leave it, because it became intended by destruction.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling with proof on the rights of the preemptor (shafi') when he builds or plants on land taken by shuf'a and the land is later reclaimed (istihqaq). The core ruling is that the preemptor recovers only the purchase price (thaman), not the value of the building or plantation. The commentary explains that the preemptor, unlike the buyer, is not compelled to demolish his building because the buyer built with permission (taslit) from the seller, while the preemptor took the land by force (jabr). However, since the preemptor's right is strong (haqq muta'akkid), he is not treated as an aggressor. The passage then covers two special cases: (1) If the building collapses or trees dry up by natural causes before the preemptor exercises his option, he must pay the full price if he takes the land, because the building is appurtenant and its loss does not reduce the price. (2) If the buyer deliberately demolishes the building, the preemptor takes only the land (al-'arsa) with its share of the price, not the rubble, because the building no longer exists as an appurtenance. The passage also reports an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that the preemptor can recover the value of the building from his own seller (the one who sold the land to him), analogizing the preemptor to a deceived buyer (mughtarr). The commentary refutes this, noting that the preemptor is not deceived by the seller but took by force. The reliance (mufta bihi) is the majority Hanafi position that the preemptor recovers only the price.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqāq): Reclamation: a third party proves rightful ownership of the land, voiding the sale.
تسليط (taslīṭ): Permission or authorization given by the rightful owner to another to act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer (who sold the land to the preemptor) demolishes the building, what does the preemptor receive?
A) The value of the demolished building from the buyer
B) The land and the rubble
C) The land with its share of the price, no rubble
D) Nothing, because the building was his

Key Takeaway
Natural destruction gives the preemptor an option to take land at full price; buyer demolition limits him to the land only; and the majority view denies recovery from the seller.

Ruling on Legal Devices to Drop Shuf'ah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on using legal devices to drop pre-emption right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on using legal devices (hila) to drop the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) and state the mufta bihi position with its conditions.

Core Idea
Using a legal device to drop shuf'ah before it becomes obligatory is permissible; after obligation, it is disliked except when no harm is intended.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a nuanced issue within the Hanafi school: can one use a legal device to avoid the obligation of pre-emption? The discussion reveals a disagreement between the two greatest disciples of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this difference requires careful attention to the timing of the device (before or after the right becomes established) and the intention behind it. The operative fatwa follows Abu Yusuf, but is restricted by conditions from later authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Abu Yusuf permits the device after obligation if no harm; Muhammad dislikes it absolutely.
2
Understand the key distinction
The device is seen as 'preventing the obligation' (man' 'an al-wujub) rather than 'cancelling an established right' (isqat al-haqq al-thabit).
This distinction is critical for Abu Yusuf's reasoning.
3
Analyze the timing
Before the right becomes obligatory, using a device is permissible by consensus. After obligation, it is disputed.
The passage cites al-Nihayah and al-Khulasah for consensus on dislike after obligation.
4
Examine the condition for permissibility
Abu Yusuf permits the device after obligation only if the buyer's intention is to defend his property, not to harm the preemptor.
Al-Mabsut clarifies this.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The fatwa is given with Abu Yusuf's view, but conditioned by al-Sirajiyyah: the neighbor must not be in need of the property.
Later authorities like the commentator of al-Ashbah considered this condition beneficial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفتى في الشفعة بقول أبي يوسف له ؛ لأنه منع " عن وجوب الحق لا إسقاط للحقِّ الثَّابت وهكذا يقول في الزكاة ، لكن هذا في غاية الشناعة ؛ لأنه إيثار للبخل، وقطع رزق الفقراء الذي قدَّرَهُ الله له في مال الأغنياء، والانخراط في سلك الذين يكنزون الذهب والفضة ولا ينفقونها في سبيل الله الله
١ ] قوله : في الشفعة ؛ يفتي بقول أبي يوسف الله ، قيده في «السراجية» بما إذا كان الجار غير محتاج إليه، واستحسنه محشى «الأشباه»، ونقل في «النهاية»: إن منهم من قال : إنه لا خلاف فيها، وفي «البزازيّة»: وإن قبل الثبوت لا بأس به، عدلاً كان الشفيع أو فاسقاً في المختار؛ لأنه ليس بإبطال.

لكنه ذكر في «المبسوط») : لا بأس بالاحتيال بعد الوجوب أيضاً إذا لم يكن قصد المشتري الإضرار به، وإنّما قصده الدفع عن ملك نفسه، ثم قال : قيل : هذا قولُ أبي يوسف ، فأما عند محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فيكره.
في «بيوع» «الملتقط»: كلّ حيلة لا تؤدّي إلى الضرر يجوز عن الربا، ولا يأثم، وكل حيلة لا يؤدّي إلى الضرر بأحدهما لا يجوز في الديانة وإن جاز في الفتوى، وعليه يحمل ما جاء في الكراهة ، قاله شيخ الإسلام.
[۳] قوله : في الزكاة ؛ وكذا في الحج وآية السجدة، كأن يبيع السائمة بغيرها قبل حولان الحول، أو يهب لابنه المال قبله أو قبل أشهر الحج ، أو يقرأ سورة السجدة ، ويدع آيتها ، أو يقرأها سراً بحيث لا يسمعُ نفسه على المشهور ؛ لأنَّ المعتبر اسماعُ نفسه لا مجرد تصحيح الحروف.
[٤]قوله : والانخراط ؛ اعلم أنّ الانخراط من باب الانفعال، بمعنى الدخول، يعني : درميان جماعتي رفتن وجوب تراشیدن و سخن بدرواد اشتن كسي را. كذا في «كنز اللغات».
And a fatwa is given in shuf'ah according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, because it is prevention of the obligation of the right, not cancellation of an established right. And likewise he says in zakah. But this is extremely disgraceful because it is preference for miserliness, cutting off the sustenance of the poor which Allah has decreed for them in the wealth of the rich, and entering into the ranks of those who hoard gold and silver and do not spend them in the way of Allah.
[1] His saying: in shuf'ah; a fatwa is given according to the saying of Abu Yusuf. He restricted it in al-Sirajiyyah to when the neighbor is not in need of it, and the commentator of al-Ashbah deemed it good. And it is transmitted in al-Nihayah that some of them said there is no disagreement in it. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: even before establishment there is no harm, whether the preemptor is just or corrupt in the chosen view, because it is not nullification.
[2] His saying: because it is prevention... etc.; that is, prevention from the obligation of the right, not cancellation of it. An indication to the portrayal of the issue of dropping shuf'ah before it becomes obligatory. Indeed, the legal device to drop it after obligation is disliked by consensus according to what is in al-Nihayah and al-Khulasah. But it is mentioned in al-Mabsut: there is no harm in using a legal device after obligation also if the buyer did not intend harm to him, but rather intended to defend his own property. Then it is said: this is the saying of Abu Yusuf. As for according to Muhammad, it is disliked.
In the Buyu' of al-Multaqat: every legal device that does not lead to harm is permissible regarding usury, and one does not sin. And every legal device that does not lead to harm to either of them is not permissible in religion (al-diyanah) even if permissible in fatwa. Upon this is carried what has come about dislike, said by Shaykh al-Islam.
[3] His saying: in zakah; and similarly in hajj and the verse of prostration, such as selling grazing livestock for other than it before the year passes, or giving his son wealth before it, or before the months of hajj, or reciting Surat al-Sajdah and omitting its verse, or reciting it silently such that he does not hear himself according to the well-known view, because the consideration is hearing oneself, not mere correct articulation of letters.
[4] His saying: and entering; know that 'al-inkhirat' is from the pattern of infi'al, meaning entering. That is: going among a group, and obligation of shaving, and speaking ill of someone. Thus in Kunz al-Lughat.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about using legal devices (hila) to drop the right of shuf'ah (pre-emption). The author explains that the fatwa is given according to Abu Yusuf's view that such devices are permissible, because they prevent the right from becoming obligatory rather than canceling an already established right. The author then notes that this reasoning is also applied to zakah, but he criticizes it as disgraceful because it encourages miserliness and harming the poor. The passage clarifies that the ruling differs based on timing: before the right becomes established, using a legal device is permissible (mubah) or not disliked, while after it is established, there is a disagreement. According to the consensus in al-Nihayah and al-Khulasah, legal devices after obligation are disliked (makruh). However, in al-Mabsut, Abu Yusuf permits them even after obligation if the buyer's intention is to protect his own property, not to harm the preemptor. Muhammad considers them disliked in all cases. The passage then quotes al-Multaqat distinguishing between legal devices in transactions like usury (riba) and in shuf'ah: if the device does not lead to harm, it is permissible and not sinful; if it does lead to harm to either party, it is not permissible in religion (diyanah) but may be permissible in external fatwa. Finally, the author compares shuf'ah to zakah, hajj, and recitation of the prostration verse, giving examples of permissible legal devices to avoid obligations, such as selling livestock before the year ends or giving wealth in advance, and reciting the sajdah verse silently. The overall message is that the Hanafi school has nuanced views on legal devices, prioritizing harm prevention.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption or first refusal for a neighbor or co-owner to buy a property before an outsider.
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal device or stratagem to achieve a permissible result while technically observing the letter of the law, often to avoid an obligation without open violation.
الإسقاط (al-isqat): The act of dropping or cancelling a right before or after its establishment. In this passage, it distinguishes between preventing obligation and cancelling an existing right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Before the right becomes obligatory
B) After the right becomes obligatory
C) Only if the preemptor is in need
D) Only if the buyer intends harm

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of using hilah to drop shuf'ah depends on timing and intention; the operative fatwa follows Abu Yusuf with the condition that the neighbor is not in need.

Definition of Shuf'ah and Rulings on Dropping It
Definition of shuf'ah and ruling on dropping it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition and purpose of shuf'ah and identify when it is permissible to drop it according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is a protective right: it cannot be dropped if the buyer harms the neighborhood, but a legal device may be used against a contentious preemptor.

This lesson opens the Book of Shuf'ah by defining its purpose: repelling harm from neighbors. The author then presents a key ruling: the right of pre-emption is not absolute; its dropping depends on the character of the buyer and the preemptor. We will examine the definition, the purpose, and the two scenarios that determine whether shuf'ah can be dropped.

Guided Path
1
State the definition of shuf'ah
Identify the term and its purpose: shuf'ah is the right of pre-emption to buy property before an outsider, legislated to prevent harm from neighbors.
2
Identify the ruling on dropping shuf'ah
Read the two scenarios: when the buyer is harmful, dropping is not permissible; when the buyer is righteous and the preemptor is contentious, a legal device is used to drop it.
3
Apply the condition
Determine which scenario applies based on the buyer's character and the preemptor's attitude.
4
Note the legal device (hilah)
Understand that a hilah is permitted to circumvent the unreasonable preemptor's objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة تعريفها وأنواعها وأحكامها
والاستبشار بما بشرهم الله تعالى.
وأقول : الشفعة إنما شرعت لدفع ضرر الجـوار ، فالمشتري إن كان ممن يتضرر به الجيران لا يحلُّ إسقاطها، وإن كان رجلاً صالحاً ينتفع به الجيران ، والشفيع متعنت لا يحب جواره فحينئذ يحتالُ في إسقاطها).
Book of Shuf'ah: Its definition, types, and rulings.
And rejoicing in what Allah has given them glad tidings of.
And I say: Shuf'ah was only legislated to repel the harm of neighbors. So if the buyer is one from whom neighbors suffer harm, it is not permissible to drop it. But if he is a righteous man from whom neighbors benefit, and the preemptor is contentious and does not like his neighborhood, then a legal device is used to drop it.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage serves as the opening definition of the Book of Shuf'ah in the text. It defines the purpose of shuf'ah: to prevent harm arising from a new neighbor. The author then provides a practical ruling: if the new buyer is harmful to the neighbors, then the right of shuf'ah (pre-emption) cannot be dropped by any means—the preemptor must enforce it to protect the community. Conversely, if the buyer is a beneficial, righteous person, and the preemptor is merely being difficult or unjustly dislikes the buyer, then a legal device (hilah) may be used to drop the preemptor's right, allowing the beneficial neighbor to stay. This highlights the principle that shuf'ah is not a personal privilege to cause harm but a tool for communal well-being. The phrase 'والاستبشار بما بشرهم الله تعالى' is a quote reference to Quran 3:21, indicating that those who oppose the Prophet's message will face painful punishment, but here it seems to be a heading for a section on rejoicing in glad tidings—possibly a side comment not directly related to the legal definition.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption or first refusal for a neighbor or co-owner to buy a property before an outsider.
الاستبشار (al-istibshar): Rejoicing or receiving glad tidings; here it quotes the Quranic phrase 'بَشِّرْهُم بِعَذَابٍ أَلِيم' (give them tidings of a painful torment) from Ale Imran 3:21, but used as a heading for a section on this verse.
متعنت (muta'annit): A person who is stubborn, unreasonable, or contentious; one who causes difficulty without justification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the buyer is a righteous man
B) When the buyer causes harm to the neighbors
C) When the preemptor is contentious
D) When the preemptor dislikes the new neighbor

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is a protective right that cannot be dropped if the buyer is harmful, but may be dropped via a legal device if the preemptor is unreasonable.

Shuf'ah: Definition, Purpose, and Ruling on Dropping It
Defines shuf'ah and explains the conditional ruling on dropping it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shuf'ah according to the Hanafi school, explain its purpose, and state when it is prohibited to drop the right and when a legal stratagem may be used.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah was legislated to prevent neighbor harm; dropping it is prohibited if the buyer is harmful.

Shuf'ah (pre-emption) is a key right in Islamic property law. It allows a neighbor or co-owner to step into the shoes of a buyer to prevent harm from an undesirable neighbor. This lesson unpacks the definition, the underlying rationale, and a critical ruling: when dropping this right is not permissible, and when a stratagem (hilah) may be used. Understanding this nuance helps you apply the rule correctly in real-life situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its legal basis
State the definition of shuf'ah as the right to take over sold property to prevent harm. Note that it is legislated (shurri'at) for that purpose.
The root word shuf'ah comes from 'shaf' meaning joining or pairing, indicating the right to join the property.
2
Understand the purpose (illah) of shuf'ah
The passage explicitly says shuf'ah was legislated to repel neighbor harm (li daf'i darar al-jiwar). This is the underlying cause (illah) that triggers the ruling.
Harm prevention is a core maqasid (objective) of Islamic law.
3
Analyze the conditional ruling on dropping shuf'ah
The passage distinguishes two scenarios: buyer harmful → dropping not permissible; buyer righteous & shafi' obstinate → stratagem allowed. Identify the condition (presence/absence of harm) and the corresponding hukm.
The ruling is not absolute; it depends on the character of the buyer and the motive of the shafi'.
4
Recognize the role of hilah (legal stratagem)
When dropping is normally prohibited, but the shafi' is being unfair, a hilah can be used to drop the right. Understand that hilah is a valid tool in Hanafi fiqh to prevent injustice without violating the law.
The hilah is not a trick to evade a ruling, but a permitted method to achieve a lawful outcome when a condition changes.
5
Connect to broader Hanafi principles
This ruling illustrates the Hanafi maxim 'harm must be removed' (al-darar yuzal) and the principle of 'blocking the means to harm' (sadd al-dhara'i).
The prohibition of dropping shuf'ah when buyer is harmful is an application of these qawa'id.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage opens the book of Shuf'ah by defining it and stating its purpose. The author explains that shuf'ah was legislated to prevent harm that a neighbor may suffer from a new owner. The ruling is that if the buyer is harmful, it is not permissible for the shafi' (pre-emptor) to drop the right of shuf'ah. However, if the buyer is righteous and the shafi' is being obstructive, then a legal stratagem (hilah) may be employed to drop the shuf'ah. The footnote clarifies the word 'istibshar' in the title, connecting it to the Quranic verse about painful tidings, interpreting it as a form of entering into the glad tidings.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of a pre-emptor to take over the property sold to a neighbor by paying the price, to prevent harm from a harmful neighbor.
الشفيع (al-shafi'): The person who has the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah); the neighbor or co-owner.
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result without violating the law, used here to drop shuf'ah when the buyer is righteous and the shafi' obstinate.
الاستبشار (al-istibshar): Entering into glad tidings; in the title, it refers to the good news of the Quranic verse (3:21).

Key Takeaway

What Invalidates Shuf'ah: Abandonment, Surrender, and Settlement
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guardian's surrender of minor's shuf'ah, plus general invalidators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the actions that invalidate shuf'ah and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the guardian's authority to surrender the minor's right.

Core Idea
Abandoning immediate claim, failing to witness, or surrendering after sale invalidates shuf'ah; guardian's surrender is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This passage from a classical Hanafi text outlines the specific actions that nullify the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). It also presents a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school on whether a guardian (father, executor, or agent) can surrender the shuf'ah of a minor. Understanding these rules is essential for anyone dealing with joint property sales and the protection of co-owners' rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three invalidating actions
List from the passage: (a) abandoning the immediate claim (al-muwathabah), (b) failing to bear witness (al-ishhad), (c) surrendering the right after the sale. Note: surrender before the sale does not invalidate.
2
Analyze the dispute on guardian's surrender
The text states that surrender by father, executor, or agent invalidates shuf'ah according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad and Zufar disagree. Understand the reasoning: trade analogy vs. harm prevention.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Examine the ruling on settlement for compensation
A settlement where the shafi' receives compensation in exchange for dropping shuf'ah is invalid because shuf'ah is merely a right to acquire ownership, not a concrete right to be traded. If compensation was taken, it must be returned.
4
Understand the timing condition
Abandonment of immediate claim or failure to witness only invalidates if the shafi' was able to act (not in prayer, not ill). Surrender only invalidates after the sale, not before.
Excuses preserve the right.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider: a co-owner learns of a sale but delays two weeks before claiming shuf'ah. Without a valid excuse, the right is lost. If he was in the obligatory prayer, it is preserved.
Think about what constitutes a valid excuse.
