⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
افصل ما يبطل الشفعة ويبطلها ترك طلب المواثبة أو الإشهاد ، وتسليمها بعد البيع فقط ولو من الأب أو الوصي أو الوكيل وصلحه منها على عوض ورد عوضه افصل ما يبطل الشفعة ( ويبطلها ترك طلب المواثبة أو الإشهاد ، وتسليمها بعد البيع فقط) : أي التسليم قبل البيع لا يبطلها)، (ولو من الأب أو الوصي أو الوكيل): أي الوكيل بطلب الشفعة ؛ فإنَّ تسليم هؤلاء يبطل الشفعة عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد وزفرَ له ، فإنَّ هذا إبطال حق ثابت للصغير، وإنَّها شرعت لدفع الضرر، ولهما : أنه في معنى ترك الشراء. و صلحه (۳) منها على عوض ورد عوضه) : أي الصلحُ على العوض [١] قوله : ويبطلها تركه طلب المواثبة...الخ ؛ يعني إذا ترك الشفيع المواثبة أو الإشهاد بعدما علم بالبيع ، وهو يقدر على ذلك، بأن لم يأخذ أحد فمه، أو لم يكن في الصلاة المفروضة بطلت شفعته ؛ لإعراضه عن الطلب، أما إن لم يقدر فلا تبطل شفعته. [٢) قوله : ولو من الأب أو الوكيل أو الوصي ؛ قال محمد وزفر : إنه حق ثابت للصغير، فلا يملك الأب أو الوصي إبطاله ؛ ولأنه شرع لدفع الضرر، فكان إبطاله إضراراً بالصغير، وقال الشيخين الله : إنّ الشفعة في معنى التجارة فيملكان ترك التجارة. [٣] قوله : وصلحه... الخ ؛ عطف على تركِ طلب المواثبة، قال في «الفوائد العارفية»: إنّ العوض يكون بمقابلة حق التملكِ الذي كان للشفيع، وهو ليس عيناً ؛ لأنه صفته، فيجب رد العوض بطلب الشفعة، وردُّ الشفيع إلا بعوض ؛ لأنَّ حق الشفعة ليس بحق متقرّر في المحل بل هو مجرد التملك، فلا يصح الاعتياض عن حق الشفعة، ولا يتعلّق إسقاطه بالجائز من الشرط ، فبالفاسد أولى، فبطل الشرط. (۱) لأنه أسقطها قبل وجود سببها إن كان سببها البيع، وقبل وجود شرطه إن كان سببها اتصال الأملاك ، والبيع شرطه . ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(۲ : ۳۹۸).
Section on what invalidates shuf'ah: It is invalidated by abandoning the immediate claim or bearing witness, and by surrendering it after the sale only, even if from the father, executor, or agent, and his settlement from it for a compensation and returning its compensation. Section on what invalidates shuf'ah (And it is invalidated by abandoning the immediate claim or bearing witness, and by surrendering it after the sale only): i.e., surrender before the sale does not invalidate it, (even if from the father, executor, or agent): i.e., the agent for claiming shuf'ah; for the surrender of these persons invalidates shuf'ah according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad and Zufar, because this is an invalidation of a right established for the minor, and it was legislated to repel harm. And for them (Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa): that it is in the sense of abandoning purchase. And his settlement (from it for a compensation and returning its compensation): i.e., the settlement for compensation. [1] His saying: 'And it is invalidated by his abandoning the immediate claim...' i.e., if the shafi' abandons the immediate claim or bearing witness after knowing the sale while able to do so, such as not being gagged or not in the obligatory prayer, his shuf'ah is invalidated due to his turning away from the claim. But if he is not able, his shuf'ah is not invalidated. [2] His saying: 'even if from the father, executor, or agent'; Muhammad and Zufar said: It is a right established for the minor, so the father or executor does not have the power to invalidate it; and because it was legislated to repel harm, so invalidating it would cause harm to the minor. And the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) said: Shuf'ah is in the nature of trade, so they have the power to abandon trade. [3] His saying: 'and his settlement from it...' is coordinate to 'abandoning the immediate claim'. It is said in al-Fawa'id al-'Aarifiyya: The compensation is in exchange for the right of ownership which the shafi' had, and it is not an actual thing because it is a description, so the compensation must be returned upon claiming shuf'ah, and the shafi' returns it except for compensation; because the right of shuf'ah is not a settled right in the property but is mere ownership, so exchanging it for compensation is not valid, and its dropping cannot be attached to a valid condition, so all the more to an invalid one, so the condition is invalid. (1) Because he dropped it before its cause existed if the cause is the sale, and before its condition existed if its cause is the joining of properties, and the sale is its condition. See: Fath Bab al-'Inaya (2:398).
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This section details the actions that invalidate the right of shuf'ah. The author explains that if the shafi' abandons the immediate claim (al-muwathaba) or fails to bear witness (al-ishhad) after learning of the sale while able, the shuf'ah is invalidated. Surrender of the right after the sale also invalidates it, even if done by a guardian (father, executor) or agent. However, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that such surrender invalidates the shuf'ah, as it is akin to abandoning a purchase. Muhammad and Zufar disagree, arguing that the shuf'ah is a right established for the minor to prevent harm, and the guardian cannot arbitrarily nullify it. The text also discusses settlement for compensation: if the shafi' agrees to drop his right for a payment, the settlement is invalid because the right of shuf'ah is not a concrete ownership; it is only a right to acquire ownership, so compensation cannot be taken in exchange. If he receives compensation, he must return it when claiming shuf'ah. The footnote clarifies that the invalidation occurs because the action precedes the existence of the cause or condition of shuf'ah.

Key Terms
المواثبة (al-muwathabah): The immediate claim of shuf'ah; the shafi' must demand the right promptly upon learning of the sale.
الإشهاد (al-ishhad): The act of bearing witness that the shafi' has claimed his right of shuf'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling of a guardian (father) surrendering the minor's right of shuf'ah?
A) It is invalid according to all Hanafi imams
B) It is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but invalid according to Muhammad and Zufar
C) It is valid according to all Hanafi imams
D) It is irrelevant because the minor cannot have shuf'ah

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is invalidated by abandoning immediate claim, failing to bear witness, surrendering after sale, or an invalid settlement; the guardian's power to surrender is disputed.

Factors Invalidating Pre-emption and the Guardian's Surrender
Listing and explaining factors that nullify pre-emption, with intra-Hanafi dispute on guardian's surrender

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the factors that invalidate the right of pre-emption and explain why the father's surrender is effective according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Surrender after sale invalidates pre-emption; the guardian's surrender is valid because pre-emption is like commerce.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) is a powerful tool for co-owners and neighbors to acquire sold property, but it can be lost through certain actions. This lesson examines the specific invalidating factors outlined in the Hanafi school, with special attention to an internal debate about whether a guardian can surrender the right on behalf of a minor. Understanding these rules safeguards the pre-emptor's claim and clarifies the limits of guardianship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Invalidating Actions
List the four main actions from the text that invalidate pre-emption: (1) abandoning immediate demand (muwathabah) or testimony (ishhad), (2) surrendering the right after the sale, (3) settlement (sulh) for compensation, (4) death of the pre-emptor. Note that surrender before the sale does not invalidate.
The text uses 'or' for muwathabah/ishhad, meaning either omission suffices.
2
Analyze the Guardian's Surrender
Focus on the phrase 'even if from the father, executor, or agent'. This creates a dispute within the Hanafi school. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that such surrender invalidates; Muhammad and Zufar disagree. Extract the legal reasoning (qiyas) from each side.
The relied-upon view is that of the 'shaykhayn' (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
3
Understand the Analogy to Commerce
The shaykhayn argue that pre-emption is analogous to seeking profit (tijarah). Just as a guardian can abandon a commercial opportunity, he can surrender pre-emption. This is the basis for their ruling.
The opposing view stresses that pre-emption is to repel harm, so surrendering it harms the minor.
4
Examine the Ruling on Settlement for Compensation
Settlement (sulh) with compensation invalidates pre-emption, but the compensation must be returned because the right of pre-emption is not a fixed right in the property. The condition for compensation is void.
This distinguishes pre-emption from rights like retaliation where compensation is valid.
5
Note the Effect of Death
The pre-emptor's death invalidates pre-emption (it is not inherited), but the buyer's death does not. The cause (ownership) must exist at the time of taking.
If the pre-emptor sells his share before taking, the right is also lost.

كتاب الشفعة تعريفها وأنواعها وأحكامها
وموت الشفيع لا المشتري
[1]
۲۰۱
يبطل الشفعة ، لأنه تسليم ، لكنَّ الصلح غير جائز ؛ لأَنَّهُ مُجرَّدُ حقِّ السَّمَلكِ فيجب رد العوض. (وموتُ الشفيع لا المشتري ) ، فإنَّ الشفيع إذا مات تبطل
(1)
الشفعة، ولا تورث عنه وبيان ذلك : لو قال الشفيع : أسقطت شفعتي فيما اشتريت على أن تسقط شفعتك فيما اشتريت، فإنه يسقط المشتري شفعته فيما اشترى الشفيع، فعلم أنّ إسقاط الشفعة لا يتعلّق بالشرط الجائز. [١]قوله : يبطل الشفعة...الخ ؛ لأنّ حق الشفعة ليس بحق متقرر، فإن قيل : حق الشفعة ليس بحق متقرّر في المحل حتى يصح الاعتياض عنه : : كحق القصاص والطلاق والعتاق في كونها غير أموال، والاعتياضُ عنها صحيح، قلنا بما في «الهداية» بخلاف القصاص ؛ لأنه حق متقرّر ، وبخلاف الطلاق والعتاق ؛ لأنه اعتياض عن ملك في المحلّ. ونظيره إذا قال للحرّة : اختاريني بألف أو قال العنين لامرأته : اختاري ترك الفسخ بألف، فاختارت سقط الخيار، ولا يثبت العوض والكفالة بالنفس في هذا بمنزلة الشفعة في رواية وفي أخرى لا تبطل الكفالة، ولا يجب المال، وقيل: هذه رواية في الشفعة، وقيل: هي في الكفالة خاصة، يعني لا تبطل الكفالة بالصلح علـى مـال ، وتبطل الشفعة بالصلح على مال، وقد فصل هذا في موضعه فتدبّر. (٢) قوله : لا المشتري ؛ أي إن مات المشتري لم تبطل الشفعة ؛ لأن المستحق باق ولم يتغير سبب حقه، بخلاف موتِ الشفيع، فإنّ السبب الذي كان يأخذ به الشفعةً يزول بموته وهو ملكه، وقيامُ السبب إلى وقتِ الأخذ شرط ؛ ولهذا لو باع ملكه قبل أن يأخذ المشفوع لم يكن له أن يأخذ بالشفعة، فكذا إذا زال بموته.
والثابت للوارث جواراً وشركة حادث بعد البيع، فلا يستحق به الشفعة، فلا تباع الدار في دين المشتري ووصيته، ولو باعه القاضي أو الوصي أو أوصى المشتري فيها بوصية، فللشفيع أن يبطله ، ويأخذ الدار لتقدّم حقه ؛ ولهذا ينقض تصرفه في حياته.

Section on what invalidates pre-emption: It is invalidated by abandoning immediate demand or testimony, and surrendering it after sale only, even if from the father, executor, or agent, and his settlement from it for compensation and returning the compensation. Section on what invalidates pre-emption (and it is invalidated by abandoning immediate demand or testimony, and surrendering it after sale only): meaning surrender before sale does not invalidate it), (even if from the father or executor or agent: meaning the agent for requesting pre-emption; for the surrender of these invalidates pre-emption according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad and Zufar, may Allah have mercy on them. For this is an annulment of a right established for the minor, and it was legislated to repel harm, while for them (the two shaykhs): that it is in the meaning of abandoning purchase. And his settlement from it for compensation and returning the compensation: meaning settlement for compensation [1] His statement: and it is invalidated by his abandoning immediate demand etc., meaning if the pre-emptor abandons immediate demand or testimony after learning of the sale, and he is able to do so, such that he did not take anything from his mouth, or he was not in the obligatory prayer, his pre-emption is invalidated due to his turning away from demand, but if he is not able then his pre-emption is not invalidated. [2] His statement: even if from the father or agent or executor; Muhammad and Zufar said: it is a right established for the minor, so the father or executor cannot annul it; and because it was legislated to repel harm, so its annulment is harmful to the minor. And the two shaykhs said: pre-emption is in the meaning of commerce, so they have the ability to abandon commerce. [3] His statement: and his settlement... etc.; it is conjoined to abandoning immediate demand. He said in al-Fawa'id al-Arifiyya: the compensation is in exchange for the right of ownership that belonged to the pre-emptor, and it is not a 'ayn (tangible property) because it is a description, so the compensation must be returned if the pre-emption is sought, and the pre-emptor must return unless with compensation, because the right of pre-emption is not a fixed right in the property but is merely ownership, so compensation for the right of pre-emption is not valid, and its dropping is not attached to a permissible condition, so by an invalid one it is more so, so the condition becomes void. (1) Because he dropped it before the existence of its cause if its cause is the sale, and before the existence of its condition if its cause is the joining of properties, and the sale is its condition. See Fath Bab al-Inaya (2:398). Chapter on pre-emption, its definition, types, and rulings: And the death of the pre-emptor, not the buyer. [1] It invalidates pre-emption, because it is surrender, but the settlement is not permissible because it is merely a right of ownership, so the compensation must be returned. (And the death of the pre-emptor, not the buyer), for if the pre-emptor dies the pre-emption is invalidated and is not inherited from him. The explanation of that: if the pre-emptor says: I have dropped my pre-emption in what you bought on condition that you drop your pre-emption in what I bought, then the buyer drops his pre-emption in what the pre-emptor bought. So it is known that dropping pre-emption is not attached to a permissible condition. [1] His statement: it invalidates pre-emption... etc.; because the right of pre-emption is not a fixed right. If it is said: the right of pre-emption is not a fixed right in the property such that compensation for it is valid, like the right of retaliation, divorce, and manumission in that they are not wealth, and compensation for them is valid. We say according to al-Hidayah: unlike retaliation, for it is a fixed right, and unlike divorce and manumission, for it is compensation for ownership in the property. Its analogy is if he says to a free woman: choose me for a thousand, or the impotent man says to his wife: choose to abandon annulment for a thousand, and she chooses, then the option falls and the compensation is not established. And bail for the person in this is like pre-emption in one narration, and in another the bail is not invalidated and the money is not owed. It is said: this is a narration concerning pre-emption, and it is said: it is specifically about bail, meaning the bail is not invalidated by settlement for money, and pre-emption is invalidated by settlement for money. This has been detailed in its place, so reflect. (2) His statement: not the buyer; meaning if the buyer dies, pre-emption is not invalidated, because the entitled person remains and the cause of his right has not changed, unlike the death of the pre-emptor, for the cause by which he takes pre-emption ceases with his death, which is his ownership, and the existence of the cause until the time of taking is a condition; hence if he sells his property before taking the pre-empted property, he does not have the right to take by pre-emption, so also if it ceases by his death. And what is established for the heir is adjacency and partnership arising after the sale, so he does not deserve pre-emption through it. So the house is not sold for the debt of the buyer or his will, and if the judge or executor sells it, or the buyer makes a will concerning it, the pre-emptor may annul it and take the house because his right is prior; hence his disposition during his life is rescinded. This is a summary of some glosses of al-Hidayah (3).
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah discusses the factors that invalidate the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) in Hanafi law. The main ruling states that shuf'ah is invalidated by the pre-emptor's abandonment of immediate demand (muwathabah) or testimony (ishhad) after learning of the sale while being able, by surrender of the right after the sale (even by a father, executor, or agent acting on behalf of a minor), by settlement (sulh) for compensation, and by the death of the pre-emptor (not the buyer).

The author first explains that surrender before the sale does not invalidate pre-emption. For the father, executor, or agent, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that their surrender invalidates pre-emption because it is analogous to abandoning a commercial opportunity (fi ma'na al-tijarah). Muhammad and Zufar disagree, arguing that the right of the minor is established and cannot be annulled, as pre-emption was legislated to repel harm, and annulling it would harm the minor. The relied-upon position is that of the shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), as indicated by the commentary.

Regarding settlement (sulh) for compensation, the commentary states that such a settlement invalidates pre-emption, but the condition for compensation is invalid because the right of pre-emption is not a fixed right in the property but merely a right to acquire ownership. Therefore, if the pre-emptor accepts compensation, he must return it; the settlement nullifies the pre-emption but does not establish a right to the compensation. The passage analogizes to other rights like retaliation, divorce, and manumission but distinguishes them.

Finally, the death of the pre-emptor invalidates pre-emption and it is not inherited, because the cause (ownership) ceases with death. The death of the buyer does not affect the right. The pre-emptor's right is prior, so any disposition by the buyer during his life or after death can be annulled.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by compulsory acquisition.
المواثبة (al-muwathabah): The immediate verbal demand for pre-emption upon learning of the sale, without delay.
الإشهاد (al-ishhad): Testifying or bearing witness to one's demand for pre-emption, often done before witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, if a father surrenders the pre-emption right of his minor son after a sale, what is the effect?
A) The pre-emption is invalidated, and the father must compensate the son.
B) The pre-emption remains valid because the father cannot annul a right established to repel harm.
C) The pre-emption is invalidated because it is analogous to commerce, and the guardian can abandon it.
D) The pre-emption is only invalidated if the father also receives compensation.

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption is invalidated by abandoning immediate demand or testimony, surrendering after sale (including by a guardian), settlement for compensation, or death of the pre-emptor; the guardian's surrender is effective because pre-emption is a commercial right.

Three Rulings on Pre-emption: Price, Multiple Parties, and Partition
Expands three rulings on shuf'a with examples and reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rulings on pre-emption regarding payment with fungible goods, partial taking from multiple buyers, and partitioned shares, and explain the Hanafi reasoning for each.

Core Idea
Pre-emption survives with fungible price, allows partial taking from multiple buyers, and applies to partitioned shares as part of delivery.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies three practical scenarios in shuf'a (pre-emption). Understanding these rulings helps you apply the right when faced with diverse sale terms, multiple buyers or sellers, and property that has been partitioned after sale. The Hanafi school relies on the principles of harm removal and the nature of the transaction to reach these conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling on price type
The passage distinguishes between price paid in fungibles (mithliyat) and price paid in a specific commodity. Understand when pre-emption is preserved or lost.
Key: the pre-emptor's ability to take the exact goods (mithli) vs. being forced to pay value (qima).
2
Analyze the second ruling on multiple buyers vs. sellers
Note that when a group buys from one, the pre-emptor may take only one buyer's share. When a group sells to one, he must take all or nothing. Trace the reasoning: divisibility of the contract and harm removal.
3
Examine the third ruling on partitioned shares
If an undivided half is sold and then partitioned by seller and buyer, the pre-emptor takes the half as a separate plot. Reason: partition completes delivery.
4
Connect the rulings to underlying principles
Each ruling is based on a general Hanafi principle: harm removal, protection of the pre-emptor's ease, and treating partition as part of possession.
5
Apply the rulings to a new scenario
Given a hypothetical sale, determine which ruling applies and what a shafi' may do.

Book of Pre-emption: its definition, types, and rulings. And likewise not if [the price is] with a commodity. And he may take the share of one of the buyers, not one of the sellers. And the half [taken] separately if an undivided half of a house was sold and then partitioned. Similarly not: meaning he heard the sale for a thousand and submitted, but it was less, or it was by a measure, or by a weight or countable commodity of approximate value equal to a thousand or more, so pre-emption is established for him because these things are of the category of items with equivalents (dhawat al-amthal), so the pre-emptor takes them, and perhaps taking these things is easier for him. And if their value is more than a thousand, then he has the right of pre-emption, contrary to if it appeared that the sale was for a commodity valued at a thousand or more, for then pre-emption does not remain for him because the pre-emptor takes here by value. So if its value is a thousand, he has accepted the sale at that, and if its value is more, then accepting the sale at a thousand is accepting the sold property at more by primary reasoning. And he may take the share of one of the buyers, not one of the sellers: meaning if a group bought from one, the pre-emptor may take the share of one of them. And if a group sold to one, he does not take the share of one of the sellers and leave the remaining share, but rather if he wishes he takes all, because here the transaction is divided over the buyer, but there it is not divided. Also in the first case the removal of harm from the neighbor is realized, not in the second. And the half separately: an undivided half of a house was sold and then partitioned: meaning he bought an undivided half of a house, then the seller and buyer partitioned, so the pre-emptor takes the half separately because the partition is part of taking possession. His saying: 'and likewise not with a commodity, etc.' like clothes and slaves. And in al-Muhit: If he heard that the price was a slave, then it turned out to be a slave-girl, then if the value of the slave-girl equals the value of the slave or more, it is void; if less, it is not void, like if he was informed that the price was a thousand and it turned out to be less. And if he was informed that the price was a thousand dirhams and he submitted, but it was a hundred dinars – and he did not mention it in al-Asl either – and al-Karkhi said: it is considered; if the value of the dinars is a thousand dirhams or more, the submission is valid; this is the saying of Shaykh al-Islam. So in al-Tajrid. And if it occurs on the other side: meaning a man bought an undivided half of a house, then the buyer partitioned with the seller, the pre-emptor takes the buyer's share that he obtained by partition. See Majma' al-Anhur 2:486.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage addresses three specific rulings within the chapter of pre-emption (shuf'a). The commentator expands upon the base text of al-Wiqayah. First ruling: If the pre-emptor hears that the sale price is a thousand dirhams and he submits (i.e., does not immediately claim pre-emption), but it turns out the actual price was less, or was paid in kind (measure, weight, or count) of approximate value equal to or greater than a thousand, pre-emption remains. This is because such goods are of the category of mithliyat (fungibles), so the pre-emptor can take them directly. If their value is more than a thousand, pre-emption still holds. However, if the payment was a specific commodity whose value was a thousand or more, pre-emption is lost because the pre-emptor would have to pay its actual value, and submitting to a sale at a thousand implies acceptance even if the commodity is worth more. Second ruling: When a group buys from one seller, the pre-emptor may take the share of any one buyer, leaving the rest. But when a group sells to one buyer, he cannot take only one seller's share; he must take the entire property or leave it. The reason is that the sale contract is divided against the buyers (each has a separate share), but against the sellers the contract is indivisible (the buyer buys the whole). Also, removing the neighbor's harm is achieved by partial taking in the first case, not the second. Third ruling: If an undivided half of a house is sold and then the seller and buyer partition it, the pre-emptor takes the half as a separated parcel (mufrazan) because the partition is considered part of the delivery (qabd). The footnote elaborates that this applies even if the partition was on the side not adjacent to the pre-emptor's property. The commentator adds references from other texts like al-Muhit and al-Tajrid, and discusses an edge case regarding a slave versus slave-girl, and the opinion of al-Karkhi and Shaykh al-Islam.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the sold share of a property from the buyer by paying the price.
شفيع (shafi'): The person entitled to exercise pre-emption.
مفرز (mufraz): Separated or partitioned; opposite of مشاع (undivided).
صفقة (safqa): Transaction; the sale contract considered as a unit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a group buys a property from one seller, can the shafi' take the share of only one of the buyers?
A) Yes, because the transaction is divided against the buyers.
B) No, he must take the entire property.
C) Yes, but only if the seller agrees.
D) No, because the harm to the neighbor is not removed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school preserves the pre-emptor's right by considering the nature of the price, the divisibility of the sale contract, and partition as part of delivery.

The Book of Partition: Definition, Elements, and Proof
Opens the chapter on qisma with definition, conditions, and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-qisma and state its pillar, condition, ruling, cause, and the proof for its legitimacy from the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Partition determines shares in common property — by physical division or exchange, and is obligatory when a partner requests it.

Partition (qisma) is a fundamental topic in Hanafi fiqh dealing with the separation of jointly owned property. This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah lays out the key components: the definition, the conditions for valid partition, the ruling (hukm), and the proofs from the Quran and Sunnah. Understanding these elements is essential for correctly applying the rules of co-ownership and pre-emption (shuf'a).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of qisma
The text defines qisma as the determination of a common right. Note that for fungible goods (mithli), the method is physical separation (ifraz); for non-fungibles, it is an exchange (mubadala) that resembles a sale.
This dual nature differentiates Hanafi from Shafi'i (who consider only mubadala as partition).
2
Extract the pillar (rukn) and conditions (shart)
The pillar is the act of separation itself. The condition is that the property must be such that its benefit does not change or get lost by division. Any property that loses utility upon division (e.g., a small room) cannot be partitioned.
If the condition fails, partition is not possible and the partners must sell and divide proceeds.
3
State the ruling (hukm) and cause (sabab)
The ruling is that partition determines each partner's share in ownership and usufruct. The cause is the request of one partner to benefit exclusively from his share. The judge is obliged to enforce partition upon such request.
This is wajib upon the judge, not always upon the partners.
4
Locate the proofs (dalil) for legitimacy
The text cites Quran 54:28 ('…the water is divided between them') as a proof. It also mentions the Prophet's division of Khaybar, and the consensus (ijma) of the community. These establish the permissibility and desirability of partition.
The Quranic word 'qismatun' is taken as evidence for division of common property.
5
Distinguish between ifraz and mubadala
The commentator explains that the word 'ghalaba' (dominant) indicates that for mithli (fungible) goods, partition is predominantly a physical separation; for non-mithli goods, it is an exchange that may involve appraisement and compensation to equalize shares.
This distinction affects the ruling: mubadala is subject to the rules of sale (e.g., gharar, options).

Book of Partition. It is the determination of the common right, and in it separation (ifraz) is dominant for fungibles (mithli), and exchange (mubadala) for others. [1] (It is the determination of the common right, and in it separation is dominant for fungibles, and exchange for others. [1] His saying: 'Book of Partition, etc.' The suitability of partition to pre-emption is that both are results of the common share, because the strongest cause of pre-emption is partnership. So if one of the two partners wants to separate while retaining his ownership, he seeks partition; and if without retention he sells, then pre-emption becomes due. And he presented pre-emption because keeping what is as it was is the foundation. And here we need to know the legality of partition, its interpretation, its pillar, its condition, its ruling, its cause, and its proof. As for the proof of its legitimacy, it is His saying: 'And inform them that the water is divided between them' (54:28). And from the Sunnah is what is narrated: when he conquered Khaybar, he divided it among the warriors. And upon this is the consensus of the community. As for its linguistic interpretation, it is 'partition' like 'capability' is for 'being able'. And as for its legal interpretation, the author will mention it. And as for its pillar, it is the act by which separation occurs. And as for its condition: it is property whose benefit does not change by partition and is not lost. And as for its ruling: it is the determination of the share of each from the other's share in both ownership and usufruct. And its cause: the request of each of the two partners to benefit from his share exclusively. And its benefits: that one of the two partners suffers from the other: bad character, cunning, and stubbornness, and he has no way out of these matters except resorting to partition. And its description: it is obligatory upon the judge when some partner requests. [2] His saying: 'ghalaba' – with tashdid and takhfif – on the active voice for the first, and on the passive voice for the second, meaning: the meaning of partition emphasizes separation in fungibles, and exchange in others. (1) Surah al-Qamar: 28. (2) Takmilat al-Bahr 8:167.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on partition (qisma). The commentator first explains the connection between qisma and shuf'a: both deal with the common share. If one partner wants to keep his ownership but separate the property, he seeks qisma; if he wants to sell, shuf'a applies. The author then outlines the key aspects of qisma: its legal proof (dalil) is from the Quran (Surah al-Qamar, verse 28: 'And inform them that the water is divided between them'), the Sunnah (the Prophet's division of Khaybar), and consensus. The linguistic meaning of qisma is 'partition', analogous to qudra (ability). Its legal definition will be given by the author (al-Wiqayah). The pillar (rukn) is the act by which separation is effected. The condition (shart) is that the property must be such that its benefit does not change through partition and is not lost. The ruling (hukm) is that the shares of each partner become distinct in ownership and use. The cause (sabab) is the request of one partner for exclusive use. The benefits include avoiding conflict. The description (sifa) is that it is obligatory (wajib) upon the judge to order partition when any partner requests it. The commentator also notes the word 'ghalaba' (dominant) and explains that in fungible goods (mithli), the dominant method is physical separation (ifraz), while in non-fungibles, it is exchange (mubadala) through appraisement. The passage includes references to Takmilat al-Bahr.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qisma): Legally: the determination of a common right, primarily by separation (ifraz) in fungibles and by exchange (mubadala) in non-fungibles.
المثلي (al-mithli): Fungible goods that have equivalents in the market (e.g., grains, oils) and can be divided without loss of value.
الإفراز (al-ifraz): Separation; the physical division of a common property into distinct shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Both partners must agree.
D) The judge must initiate the request.

Key Takeaway
Partition (qisma) is the determination of a common right; its validity requires that the property's benefit not be lost, and its proof lies in the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

Definition and Types of Partition (Qismah)
Defining qismah, its types, and the rule of compulsory partition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qismah, distinguish between ifraz and mubadalah, state the ruling of partition, and identify the condition for compulsory partition.

Core Idea
Partition (qismah) is not a sale but a separation of shares; in non-fungibles it functions as an exchange.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh text defines the Book of Partition. It clarifies that qismah is the demarcation of a shared right, and it predominantly takes one of two forms: pure separation (ifraz) for fungible goods, and exchange (mubadalah) for non-fungibles. The ruling is that the judge must enforce partition when requested, but compulsion only applies when the property is of the same genus. Understanding this distinction is crucial for resolving disputes among partners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of qismah
Read the text: 'هي تعيين الحقِّ الشائع' – it is the demarcation of a shared right. Note that it is not a transfer of ownership but a separation of existing shares.
2
Distinguish ifraz and mubadalah
The text states: 'غَلَبَ فيها الإفراز في المثلي، والمبادلة في غيره'. For mithli (fungibles), partition is pure separation; for non-fungibles, it is exchange. Understand why: fungible units are interchangeable, so each takes his share exactly; non-fungibles require compensation for value differences.
Ifraz: each takes his share without exchange. Mubadalah: partners swap portions to equalize.
3
Identify the ruling and its subject
The text says: 'فهي واجبة على الحاكم عند طلب بعض الشركاء' – partition is wajib (obligatory) upon the judge when some partners request. For the individual partner, it is permissible (ja'iz).
Obligation is on the judge (fard kifayah), not on the requesting partner.
4
Analyze the condition for compulsory partition
The sharh clarifies: compulsion (jabr) applies only when the property is of the same genus (muttaḥid al-jins), whether mithli or ghayr mithli. For example, two partners in a house (same genus) can be compelled to partition, but not if one has a house and the other a car.
The exception: mubadalah normally lacks compulsion, but here it is compelled because it contains the meaning of ifraz (separation) to serve the partner's need.
5
Examine the evidence (dalil) for legality
The text cites the Quranic verse (54:28) 'وَنَبهُم أَنَّ الْمَاءَ قِسَمَةٌ بَيْنَهُمْ' and the prophetic practice of dividing Khaybar. Also mentions ijma'. Recognize that the dalil has qat'i thubut but zanni dalala, which is sufficient for wujub in this context.
The word 'qismah' in the verse indicates the permissibility and practice of dividing shares.

وأما ركنها : فالفعلُ الذي يقع به الإفراز.
وأما شرطها : فمتاع لا تتبدل منفعته بالقسمة، ولا يفوت. وأما حكمها : فتعيين نصيب كلِّ واحدٍ منهما من نصيب الآخر ملكاً وانتفاعاً. وسببها : طلب كلِّ واحدٍ من الشريكين الانتفاع بنصيبه على الخصوص. وأما محاسنها : أن أحد الشريكين يحصل من صاحبه سوء الخلق، والفِطَن، وقُوَّةُ الرأس، وليس له مخرج من هذه الأمور إلا الرجوع إلى الاقتسام. وأما صفتها : فهي واجبة على الحاكم عند طلب بعض الشركاء.

(تكملة البحر) (٢)

[٢]قوله : غلب - بالتخفيف والتشديد - على صيغة المعلوم على الأول، وعلى صيغة المجهول على الثاني ؛ أي رجّح من معنى القسمة الإفراز في المثلي، والمبادلة في غير المثلي، فيأخذ كل شريك حصته بغيبة صاحبه في الأول لا في الثاني، وإن أجبر عليها في متحد الجنس فقط عند طلب أحدهم.

أي المبادلة غالبة في غير المثلي، مع أنه يُجْبَرُ على القسمة في غير المثلي إذا كان متّحد الجنس، مع أن المبادلة لا يجري فيه الجبر، فإنَّه إنما يُجْبَرُ عليها ؛ لأنَّ فيها معنى الإفراز مع أنَّ الشَّرِيكَ يريد الانتفاع بحصته، فأوجب الجبر على أن المبادلة قد يجري فيها الجبر إذا تعلق حق الغير به، كما في قضاء الدين.

والمراد بالمثلي : المكيلُ والمعدود المتقارب ؛ لعدم التفاوت بين العامة، فكان أخذ أحدهما من نصيبه مثل ما ترك على الآخر صورة ومعنى، فتعين أخذ المثل كذلك بمنزلة أحد المعين، والمراد من الإفراز التميز المحض، ومن المبادلة : هو الإعطاء من الجانبين.

[١] قوله : كما في قضاء الدين ؛ يعني إنّ المديون يجبر على القضاء، والديون تقضى بأمثالها فصار ما يؤدى بدلاً عما في ذمّته، وهذا جبر ظاهر في المبادلة قصداً، وقد جاز؛ فلأن يجوز بلا قصد إليه أولى ؛ لأنَّ المقصود الأصلي هاهنا انتفاع أحدهم بنصيبه على الخصوص دون الإجبار على غيره. قاله الجلبي.
Book of Partition. It is the demarcation of a shared right, and it predominantly involves separation in fungibles and exchange in non-fungibles. His statement: 'Book of Partition, etc.' The suitability of partition with preemption is that both result from a common share. Since the strongest cause of preemption is partnership, when one of the two partners wishes to separate while retaining his ownership, he requests partition; and without retention, he sells, so preemption becomes due. He (the author) presented preemption first because maintaining what was as it was is the original. Here we need knowledge of the legality of partition, its interpretation, its pillar, its condition, its ruling, its cause, and its evidence. As for the evidence of its legality, it is His statement: 'And inform them that the water is divided between them' [54:28], and from the Sunnah it is narrated that when he conquered Khaybar he divided it among the combatants, and upon this is the consensus of the ummah. As for its linguistic interpretation, it is the term for division, like 'qudrah' for ability. As for its legal definition, the author mentions it. Its pillar: the action by which separation occurs. Its condition: wealth whose utility does not change by partition and does not perish. Its ruling: the designation of each partner's share from the other's share in ownership and use. Its cause: each partner's desire to benefit exclusively from his share. Its benefits: that one partner receives from the partner bad character, intelligence, and strength of head, and he has no exit from these matters except resorting to division. Its description: it is obligatory upon the judge when some partners request it. (Completion of al-Bahr). His statement: 'predominates' - with tashdid and takhfif - in the active voice in the first, and passive voice in the second; i.e., the meaning of partition preponderates separation in fungibles and exchange in non-fungibles, so each partner takes his share in the absence of the other in the first but not in the second, and he is compelled to it only in same-genus property when one of them requests. Meaning: exchange prevails in non-fungibles, yet he is compelled to partition in non-fungibles if it is the same genus, even though exchange does not involve compulsion. He is only compelled to it because it contains the meaning of separation, and the partner desires to benefit from his share, so compulsion is imposed. However, exchange may involve compulsion when the right of another is attached to it, as in debt repayment. What is meant by mithli: what is measured or counted with approximate equivalence, due to lack of significant difference among units; thus taking one's share like what he leaves for the other in form and meaning, so taking the like is in the position of a defined specific. What is meant by separation is pure differentiation, and exchange is giving from both sides. His statement: 'like in debt repayment' means that the debtor is compelled to repay, and debts are repaid with their equivalents, so what is given becomes a substitute for what is in his liability. This is compulsion explicitly in exchange by intention, and it is permissible; so to permit it without intention is more appropriate because the primary purpose here is for one of them to benefit from his share specifically, without compulsion on the other. Al-Jalabi said.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Partition with a definition: it is the demarcation of a shared right. The commentator (sharh) provides a full anatomy of the ruling: its evidence (Quran, Sunnah, ijma'), its linguistic and legal definition, its pillar (the action of separation), condition (wealth whose utility doesn't change), ruling (designation of shares), cause (desire for exclusive use), benefits, and its description as obligatory upon the judge upon request. The key distinction is between 'mithli' (fungibles) and 'ghayr mithli' (non-fungibles). In fungibles, partition is pure separation (ifraz) because units are interchangeable; in non-fungibles, it is exchange (mubadalah). Compulsory partition (jabr) applies only when the property is of the same genus, even for non-fungibles, because the intention is to serve the partner's need for exclusive benefit. The commentator clarifies that while exchange normally lacks compulsion, it may be compelled when third-party rights are involved, analogous to debt repayment. The passage also defines mithli as measured or counted goods with little variation, and distinguishes ifraz from mubadalah.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qismah): Partition/demarcation of a shared right among partners to enable exclusive use.
المثلي (al-mithli): Fungible goods that are measured or counted with little variation among units, hence interchangeable.
الإفراز (al-ifraz): Pure separation: each partner physically takes their share without exchange of value.
المبادلة (al-mubadalah): Exchange: partners swap portions to equalize value in non-fungibles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a partner compelled (jabr) to accept partition?
A) In all types of shared property.
B) Only in fungibles of the same type.
C) Only in non-fungibles of different types.
D) Only in the same genus of property, regardless of whether it is fungible or non-fungible.

Key Takeaway
Partition is the demarcation of a shared right, primarily either pure separation (ifraz) for fungibles or exchange (mubadalah) for non-fungibles, and the judge must enforce it upon request, but compulsion only applies when the property is of the same genus.

Fee Distribution for the Appointed Divider: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fee distribution and conditions for divider

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on fee distribution for a qassam, identify the conditions for appointing a divider, and explain the prohibition of fee-sharing.

Core Idea
The divider's fee is for the work of distinguishing shares – Abu Hanifa: equal per head; Sahibayn: proportional to shares.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya unfolds an intra-Hanafi debate on how the appointed divider's fee should be split among co-owners. While the appointment itself is valid, the fee distribution hinges on whether the fee compensates for the act of distinguishing shares (tamyiz) or is a maintenance cost of ownership (mu'nat al-milk). Let us walk through the arguments and see which position prevails in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify what the passage is about: the appointment of a divider with a fee and how the fee should be distributed among co-owners.
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement.
2
Report Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
State that Abu Hanifa rules the fee is on the number of heads (equal per co-owner) because the fee is compensation for the act of distinguishing (tamyiz), which does not vary.
Key Arabic: الأجر مؤنة مقابل بالتمييز، وهو لا يتفاوت.
3
Report the Sahibayn's position and their proof
State that the two disciples hold the fee is proportional to shares because it is a maintenance cost of ownership (mu'nat al-milk).
Key Arabic: لأنه مؤنة الملك.
4
Analyze the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Consider a property with co-owners owning 70% and 30%. Under Abu Hanifa, fee split 50/50; under Sahibayn, 70/30.
This affects the cost to each co-owner.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
In later Hanafi fatwa, Abu Hanifa's equal distribution is relied upon for convenience and to avoid calculation disputes.
Check if any exceptions apply.

ظهورها فلتكن الأمانة كذلك، وإن اعتبر ظهورها فيستلزمه ظهور الأمانة قطعاً؛ لأن استلزام ظهور الملزوم ظهور اللازم، مما لا يشتبه على أحد. جلبي. ولا يشترك القسام وصحت برضا الشركاء إلا عند صغر أحدهم لأن الأمر قد يضيق على الناس، والأجر يصير غالباً. ولا يشترك القسام: أي إن قسم واحد لا يكون الأجر مشتركاً بينهم، فإنه يفضي إلى غلاء الأجر. وصحت برضا الشركاء إلا عند صغر أحدهم؛ إذ حينئذ لا بد من أمر القاضي And if he appoints [a divider] with a fee, it is valid, and it [the fee] is according to the number of heads. And it is necessary that he be just and knowledgeable about it, and one [divider] is not appointed exclusively. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: The fee is obligatory according to the proportion of shares, because it is the maintenance of ownership. His [Abu Hanifa's] argument: The fee is maintenance in exchange for distinguishing [shares], and it does not vary, rather it may be difficult in small [properties] and may be reversed, so considering it became impossible, so the basis of distinguishing was considered. And it is necessary that he be just and knowledgeable about it, and one is not appointed exclusively for the co-divider's [؟] and farther from suspicion, because when his work fee reaches him continuously, he does not incline toward some out of hope for bribery. Al-Jalabi said it. His statement: 'And if he appoints with a fee...etc.'; meaning with a fee upon the co-divider [co-owners] because the benefit is specifically for them, and the equivalent fee is estimated so that it is not judged with increase, and it is better that he be provided from the treasury as mentioned before. His statement: 'And it is necessary that he be just...etc.'; and the author did well where he did not take trustworthiness with justice like the author of al-Hidayah, and it [justice] necessarily entails it [trustworthiness], and the excuse of al-Inayah and al-Kifayah: that mentioning trustworthiness after justice, although it is from its necessities, due to the possibility that he may not be apparently trustworthy, perhaps its meaning is that mentioning a thing in one place once as subordinate and another time as original may be to indicate its manifestation in that place. So it is rebutted that the condition considered in the divider if it is justice itself without consideration of its manifestation, then let trustworthiness be like it, and if its manifestation is considered, then it necessarily entails the manifestation of trustworthiness; because the entailment of manifestation of the implied from the manifestation of the implication is not ambiguous to anyone. Al-Jalabi. And the dividers should not share [the fee], and it [division] is valid with the consent of the co-owners except when one of them is minor, because the matter may become constrained for the people, and the fee becomes high. 'And the dividers should not share': meaning if one divides, the fee is not shared among them, for it leads to high fee. 'And it is valid with the consent of the co-owners except when one of them is minor'; since then there must be the order of the judge for the division, even without a fee. 'The matter becomes constrained for the people, the fee becomes high' – with the letter ghain and ya – meaning [in Persian] 'karan' and 'kamyab'. As in al-Fawa'id al-Arifiyyah. His statement: 'And the dividers should not share...etc.'; al-qassam with dammah on qaf and tashdid on sin, plural of qasim, and the meaning: The judge should not let them share, so that the fee does not become high. The explanation: The judge does not leave the appointed dividers themselves upon their opinion to share, such that the matter of division does not go beyond them to others. Rather, the judge says to each one of them: 'You are authorized for the division without the participation of the other.' So each one of them is permitted and authorized for division from the side of the judge. Then every divider competes for whatever fee is available, so the shared properties are divided with the cheapest fees so that that fee is not lost. The result: The imam does not leave the two dividers to share in the fee, but rather commands each to be individual in that, otherwise they may agree on the increased fee. As in al-Fawa'id al-Arifiyyah. His statement: 'And it is valid with the consent of the co-owners...etc.'; He said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its commentary Radd al-Muhtar: It is valid with the consent of the co-owners as passed on division by compulsion, and this is in division by mutual consent, except if among them is a minor or insane, as an interrupted exception, as indicated by his statement after: 'due to its non-bindingness' — an exception from a deleted phrase; i.e., and it is binding... end. 'T'.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling statement about the appointment of a divider (qassam) with a fee, his qualifications, and the prohibition of multiple dividers sharing the fee. The text begins by stating that appointing a divider with a fee is valid (sahih). Abu Hanifa holds that the fee is distributed equally among the co-owners (according to the number of heads), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold it is proportional to each co-owner's share, because the fee is considered a maintenance cost of ownership (mu'nat al-milk). Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the fee is compensation for the act of distinguishing shares (tamyiz), which does not vary with the size of the share; it may be equally difficult for small shares and sometimes inversely proportional, so the basis of distinguishing (asl al-tamyiz) is considered and the fee is divided equally. The divider must be 'adl (just) and 'alim (knowledgeable) about division, and should not be exclusively appointed for one party. The commentator praises the author for not separately requiring trustworthiness (amanah) since justice necessarily entails it. The text then discusses that dividers should not share work and fees (la yashtarik al-qussam) to avoid high fees; the judge should permit each to work independently so they compete for work, lowering fees. Division is valid with the consent of all co-owners, except when a minor is present, then court order is necessary. The commentator elaborates on these points, referencing earlier authorities. The practical consequence is that in Hanafi practice, the fee is divided equally among co-owners unless otherwise agreed.

Key Terms
القسام (al-qassam): The divider of jointly owned property appointed by the judge or by mutual consent of co-owners.
الأجر (al-ajr): The fee paid to the divider for his work of dividing shares among co-owners.
التمييز (al-tamyiz): The act of distinguishing and separating the shares of co-owners in a joint property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position that is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa), the fee of a hired divider is distributed:
equally per head (number of co-owners)
proportionally to each co-owner's share
equally among the dividers
according to the judge's discretion

Key Takeaway
The divider's fee is compensation for the work of distinguishing shares, and in the Hanafi school, it is distributed equally among co-owners, not proportionally to their shares.

Dispute on Dividing Inherited Real Estate Without Proof of Ownership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partition of inherited real estate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on partitioning inherited real estate when heirs only prove possession, and compare the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Inherited real estate requires proof of ownership for partition because the deceased’s ownership persists.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute about partition of inherited real estate. The matn states that if heirs prove possession but not ownership, Abu Hanifa forbids partition while his two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) permit it. We will follow the reasoning of each side and see why the preferred view aligns with Abu Hanifa, emphasizing the distinction between possession and ownership in inheritance cases.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling from the matn
Identify the core ruling: if heirs prove that the property is in their possession but do not prove it belongs to them, what is the ruling on partition?
The matn says 'it is not partitioned' according to Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify the dispute and the scholars involved
Recognize that the dispute is between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Note that the commentary also discusses whether the stricter view is unique to Abu Hanifa or shared by all three.
The 'most correct' view is said to be the position of all three.
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Understand Abu Hanifa's argument: the deceased's ownership persists after death, so partition is a judgment against the deceased, requiring evidence of ownership. Real estate is inherently preserved, so the need for partition (as with movables) does not apply.
He distinguishes purchased property (where seller no longer owns) and movables (where partition aids preservation).
4
Follow the Sahibayn's reasoning
Understand their argument: possession is evidence of ownership, and in the absence of a counter-claimant, partition should proceed. They analogize to purchased property and movables where possession suffices.
The commentary rebuts that possession could be due to deposit, lease, or loan.
5
Determine the tarjih and its basis
Identify the preferred view (mufta bihi): the most correct view is that partition is not allowed without proof of ownership. This is attributed to all three imams in one narration, emphasizing that the purpose of partition (preservation) does not apply to real estate.
The text states: 'the most correct is that it is the saying of all'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة / تعريفها وأحكامها
ولا يقسم إن برهنا أنه معهما حتى يبرهنا أنه لهما
وعندهما : يُقسم كما في الصور الأُخر.
له : أن ملك المورّث باق بعد موته، فالقسمة قضاء على الميت فلا بد من البينة بخلاف صورة الشراء ؛ لأنَّ الملك بعد الشّراءِ غير باق للبائع، وبخلاف غير العقار إذا ادعوا إرثه ؛ لأن القسمة تفيد زيادة الحفظ، والعقار محصن بنفسه، فلا احتياج إلى القسمة، فالمسألة التي لم تذكر في «المتن» فُهِمَ حكمُها من قسمةِ النَّقلي الموروث، وكذا من قسمة العقار المشترى بالطريق الأولى ؛ فلهذا لم يذكر. (ولا إن برهنا ) أنه معهما حتى يبرهنا أنه لهما)، الضمير في أنَّه يرجع إلى العقار، فقيل : هذا قول أبي حنيفة ، والأصح أنه قول الكل ؛ لأنهما إذا ثم قيل : هو قول أبي حنيفة له خاصة ، وقيل : هو قول الكل وهو الأصح ؛ لأنَّ قسمة الحفظ في العقار غير محتاج إليه، وقسمة الملك تفتقر إلى قيامه، ولا ملك بدون البينة فامتنع الجواز.

فإن كان قصد المصنف الله تعيين الروايتين، فليس فيه ما يدل على ذلك، وإلا فتقعُ المسألة مكرّرة، وأجاب المقدسي الله : بحمل ما في «الجامع الصغير» على ما ذكر أنه بأيديهما فقط، وبرهنا عليه فيكون من اختلاف الروايتين ؛ لاختلاف الموضوع، فلا تكرار.
Book of Partition / Its Definition and Rulings. And it shall not be divided if they prove that it is with them until they prove that it belongs to them. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is divided as in the other cases. For him (Abu Hanifa): that the ownership of the deceased remains after his death, so the partition is a judgment upon the deceased, so evidence is necessary, unlike the case of purchase because ownership after purchase does not remain for the seller, and unlike other than real estate if they claim inheritance, because partition provides increased preservation, and real estate is protected by itself, so there is no need for partition. So the matter that was not mentioned in the text (al-Matn), its ruling is understood from the partition of movable inherited property, and likewise from the partition of purchased real estate by way of priority; thus it was not mentioned. (And if they prove) that it is with them until they prove that it belongs to them), the pronoun 'that' refers to the real estate. So it is said: This is the statement of Abu Hanifa, and the most correct is that it is the statement of all; because if... then it is said: it is the statement of Abu Hanifa alone, and it is said: it is the statement of all, and that is the most correct because the partition of preservation in real estate is not needed, and the partition of ownership depends on its establishment, and there is no ownership without evidence, so the permissibility is prevented. [1] His saying: 'And according to the two, it is divided...' i.e., it is divided by their confession, as in the other cases, which are movable property absolutely, and real estate claimed to be purchased, or absolute ownership. Their proof: it is in their hands, and that is evidence of ownership, and there is no opponent for them. And for him (Abu Hanifa): the ownership of the deceased remains until its end. [2] His saying: 'And if they prove...' i.e., it is not divided. Al-Ayni said following al-Zayla'i: This very matter is the previous matter, which is his saying: 'or his absolute ownership', because the intended meaning is that they claim ownership, and they did not mention how it transferred to them, and he did not stipulate the establishment of evidence that it is their ownership, and that is the narration of al-Quduri for him, and he stipulated here, and that is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. If the author intended to specify the two narrations, there is nothing in that to indicate that, otherwise the matter becomes repetitive. And al-Maqdisi answered: by interpreting that which is in al-Jami' al-Saghir as that which is mentioned that it is in their hands only, and they proved it, so it is from the difference of the two narrations due to the difference of the subject, so there is no repetition.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya deals with a key intra-Hanafi dispute on the partition of inherited real estate. The matn states: if heirs prove that the property is in their possession but do not prove it belongs to them, according to Abu Hanifa it is not divided; according to his two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is divided as in other cases. The commentator explains: Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the deceased's ownership persists after death, so partition is effectively a judgment against the deceased, requiring clear evidence of ownership. For purchased property, the seller no longer owns it, so possession suffices. For movables, partition aids preservation, but real estate is inherently protected, so no need. The students argue that possession is itself indicative of ownership, and absent a counter-claim, it should be accepted. The text then discusses variant narrations: some attribute the strict position to Abu Hanifa only, others to all three imams. The most correct view is that it is the position of all because the purpose (preservation) does not apply to real estate. The commentator also addresses a potential duplication with a previous ruling, explaining that the two narrations (al-Quduri and al-Jami' al-Saghir) differ in the level of proof required.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Partition or division of jointly owned property among co-owners, either by way of ownership (قسمة ملك) or preservation (قسمة حفظ).
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, to prove a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct Hanafi view, when can inherited real estate be partitioned?
A) As soon as the heirs possess it.
B) Only after the heirs prove that the deceased owned it and they are the heirs.
C) Only if the property is movable.
D) Anytime, because partition is always beneficial.

Key Takeaway
Inherited real estate requires proof of ownership for partition because the deceased's ownership persists and real estate does not need preservation partition.

Partition with Minor or Absent Heirs: Evidence Requirements
Ruling on partition with proof conditions and appointment of receiver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which inherited real estate may be partitioned when a minor or absent heir exists, and apply the requirement of proving death and number of heirs.

Core Idea
Proof of death and number of heirs suffices to partition inherited property, even with a minor or absent heir, by appointing a receiver.

When heirs wish to divide inherited real estate but some are minors or absent, the Hanafi school provides a clear procedure. This lesson examines the conditions that must be met—proof of death and proof of the number of heirs—and the role of the judge in appointing a receiver for those not present. It also clarifies a subtle point from al-Hidayah about possession of the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is partition (qismah) of inherited real estate. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) when certain conditions are met.
Partition is not obligatory; it is a right of the heirs.
2
State the Two Required Proofs
The heirs must prove (1) the death of the deceased and (2) the number of heirs. Without both, partition cannot proceed.
Proof typically means shari' evidence such as witness testimony or official documents.
3
Explain the Procedure with Minor/Absent Heir
If a minor or absent heir exists, the judge still orders partition but must appoint a trustworthy receiver (wakil) to collect and protect that heir's share.
The receiver acts as a custodian until the heir comes of age or returns.
4
Resolve the Possession Issue
The passage notes a slip in al-Hidayah: it says 'the house is in their hands' (plural) but if a minor/absent heir is present, not all can hold possession. The correct interpretation is that 'their' refers to the two present heirs only.
Possession by the present heirs is sufficient; the minor/absent heir's share is held in trust.
5
Distinguish from Partition of Preservation
Partition of ownership (qismat al-milk) is used here, not partition of preservation (qismat al-hifz), because real estate is inherently preservable. Ownership must be established via evidence.
This distinction prevents unnecessary partitions when ownership is unproven.

And if they prove the death and the number of heirs, and the property is with them, and among them is a minor or an absent one, it is divided and a person is appointed to receive for them. Commentary
This passage explains the procedure when two heirs appear to claim inherited real estate. They must prove the death of the deceased and the number of heirs. If they do, and the property is in their possession, the judge orders division even if there is a minor or absent heir among the heirs. The judge appoints a receiver to collect the shares of the minor or absent heir. The commentator clarifies a point from al-Hidayah: the text says 'the house is in their hands' (plural), but if there is a minor or absent heir, how can it be in all their hands? The answer is that 'their' refers to the two present heirs, not all, because the minor cannot hold possession independently. This is an important clarification for practical application.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Partition of joint property, here meaning division of inherited real estate.
وكيل (wakil): A representative appointed by the judge to collect the share of an absent or minor heir.

A) That they own the property
B) The death of the deceased and the number of heirs
C) That the minor heir has given consent
D) That the property was purchased by the deceased

Key Takeaway
Proof of death and number of heirs allows partition even with minor/absent heirs, with the judge appointing a receiver.

Division upon Request When One Partner Does Not Benefit
Explains conditions for division when one partner's share is too small

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three Hanafi positions on partition (qismah) when one co-owner's share is too small to yield benefit and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Partition is a right, but a request from a non-benefiting partner may be seen as harassment; the relied-upon view allows either partner to compel division.

Imagine you inherit a small share of a property that is too tiny to use. Can you force your co-owner to divide? The Hanafi school has three answers. In this lesson, we explore the passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya that discusses this precise scenario. By understanding the three opinions and the rationale behind the mufta bihi, you will learn how classical Hanafi jurists balanced the right to exclusive ownership against the risk of abuse of legal process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Two co-owners share property. One partner benefits from his share; the other does not because his share is too small.
This is the specific scenario addressed in the matn.
2
State the three opinions
First (al-Khassaf): only the benefiting partner may request. Second (al-Jassas): only the non-benefiting partner may request. Third (al-Hakim): either partner may request.
The matn gives the first opinion; the commentator explains all three.
3
Identify the rationale for each opinion
First: the non-benefiting partner is obstinate (muta'annit). Second: the benefiting partner may be seeking to harm. Third: each co-owner has a right to exclusive ownership; division is a necessary right (haqq lazim).
Understanding the rationale helps in evaluating the opinions.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is who can force division when one partner does not benefit. Under the first opinion, only the benefiting partner; under the second, only the non-benefiting; under the third, either.
This shows the real-world impact of each view.
5
Recognize the tarjih (mufta bihi)
The marginal note 'وعليه الفتوى' indicates that the third opinion (al-Hakim) is the basis for fatwa.
Although the matn states the first opinion, later scholars adopted the third due to its strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن انتفع كُلّ بحصته ، وبطلب ذي الكثير فقط إن لم ينتفع الآخر؛ لقلة حصته، ولا يقسم إلا بطلبهم ( إن انتفعَ كُلّ بحصته ، وبطلب ذي الكثير فقط إن لم ينتفع الآخر؛ لقلة حصته) : أي لا يقسم بطلب ذي القليل؛ لأنَّهُ لا فائدة له ، فهو متعنت في طلب القسمة". وقيل : على العكس؛ لأنَّ صاحب الكثير يطلب ضرر صاحبه، وصاحب القليل يرضى بضرره. وقيل : يقسم بطلب كل واحد. ( ولا يقسم إلا بطلبهم واحد من الشركاء ينتفع بنصيبه ، قسم القاضي بطلب أحدهم ؛ لأنَّ القسمة حق لازم على القاضي فيما يحتمل القسمة عند طلب أحدهم على ما بيناه قبل. ١ ] قوله : بطلب ذي الكثير...الخ ؛ صورته : إن كان ينتفع أحدهم ويستضرُّ به الآخر لقلة نصيبه، فإن طلب صاحب الكثير قسم ، وإن طلب صاحب القليل لم يقسم ؛ لأن الأول ؛ أي صاحب الكثير منتفع به، فاعتبر؛ لأنه طالب ؛ لأنه يخصه بالانتفاع بملكه، ويمنع الغير عن الانتفاع بملكه. وهذا منه طلب الحق والإنصاف، وإن له أن يمنع غيره من الانتفاع بملكه والقاضي نصب نظراً لاتصال الحقوق إلى أهلها، ودفع المظالم، والثاني : إن ذي القليل متعنت في طلبه فلم يعتبر. [٢] قوله : متعنت في طلب القسمة ؛ والقاضي يجيب المتعـنـت بالـرد، وتعــذر الانتفاع بنصيبه لقلة نصيبه، لا لمعنى من جهة صاحب الكثير. «كفاية»(4).
If each benefits from his share, and [division is done] only upon the request of the one with the greater share if the other does not benefit due to the smallness of his share, then division is not done except upon their request. (If each benefits from his share, and only upon the request of the one with the greater share if the other does not benefit due to the smallness of his share): that is, division is not done upon the request of the one with the small share because there is no benefit for him, so he is being obstinate in seeking division. And it was said: the opposite, because the one with the greater share seeks to harm his partner, while the one with the small share accepts his own harm. And it was said: division is done upon the request of each one. (And division is not done except upon their request) if one of the partners benefits from his share, the judge divides upon the request of one of them because division is a necessary right incumbent upon the judge in what admits division upon the request of one of them, as we explained before. [1] His saying: 'upon the request of the one with the greater share...etc.'; its picture: if one of them benefits and the other is harmed due to the smallness of his share, then if the owner of the greater share requests division, it is done, and if the owner of the small share requests, it is not done, because the first, i.e., the owner of the greater share, benefits from it, so he is considered because he is a seeker; he appropriates the benefit of his property and prevents others from benefiting from his property. This is from him a request for right and fairness, and he has the right to prevent others from benefiting from his property, and the judge is appointed to ensure rights reach their owners and to remove injustices. The second: the owner of the small share is obstinate in his request, so it is not considered. [2] His saying: 'obstinate in seeking division'; the judge responds to the obstinate by rejection, and the impossibility of benefiting from his share due to its smallness, not for any reason from the owner of the greater share. (Kifayah (4)).
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the condition of benefiting from one's share in the division of jointly owned property. The matn states the default ruling: division is only executed upon the request of those partners who will actually benefit from their share. If one partner's share is so small that he does not benefit, division is not carried out at his request alone; it requires the request of the partner with the larger share. The commentator then expands by presenting three opinions within the Hanafi school. The first opinion, attributed to al-Khassaf, affirms the matn: the one with the small share is considered obstinate (muta'annit) and his request is not entertained. The second opinion, from al-Jassas, reverses the rule, arguing that the owner of the larger share may be seeking to harm his partner, while the owner of the small share accepts his own harm. The third opinion, chosen by al-Hakim and considered the basis for fatwa, allows division at the request of either partner. The commentator further explains that the underlying rationale is that division is a legal right of each co-owner to enjoy his property, and the judge is obliged to facilitate this when it is possible. If a partner cannot benefit due to the smallness of his share, his request is seen as mere harassment, not a legitimate claim.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Division of joint property among co-owners to assign each an exclusive portion.
متعنت (muta'annit): A litigant who demands something without legitimate benefit, causing hardship to others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first opinion (al-Khassaf), whose request for division is considered valid when one partner benefits and the other does not due to a small share?
A) The partner with the small share only
B) The partner with the large share only
C) Either partner
D) Neither partner

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, while some early texts condition partition on the requesting partner's benefit, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) allows any co-owner to demand division regardless of benefit, though the judge may reject an obstinate request.
