Division of Slaves and Jewels: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on division when both partners are harmed and dispute on dividing slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the compulsory division of slaves and jewels, and explain the reasoning behind each view using the concepts of tafawut fahish and ittihad al-jins.

Core Idea
Slaves and jewels: Abu Hanifa blocks compulsory division; Sahibayn allow it via qiyas on uniform goods.

When two people inherit a valuable slave or a diamond necklace, can one force a division even if the other objects? The Hanafi school has an internal split on this question. This lesson unpacks the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, as presented in a classical commentary on the Quduri text. You will see how the principle of qiyas and the concept of 'exorbitant disparity' (tafawut fahish) shape their conflicting rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested legal issue
Read the matn and the commentary to find the exact question: Can a judge order the division of slaves or jewels at the request of some co-owners even if others do not consent?
The general rule for ordinary goods ('urud) is that same-type items can be divided upon request. The dispute is whether slaves and jewels fall under this rule.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that slaves and jewels are not subject to compulsory division. His proof is the great disparity (tafawut fahish) among humans in intelligence and skills, and among jewels in quality, which makes them akin to different types. Division would defeat the purpose of completing benefit.
He distinguishes slaves from camels, where variation is minimal.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that slaves and jewels can be divided compulsorily, like camels and other goods, because they belong to a single type (jins). Disparity within the same type does not prevent division, as seen in camels and in the division of slaves in war spoils.
Their argument uses qiyas: the 'illa is unity of type.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's rebuttal of the qiyas
Abu Hanifa counters that the 'illa of unity of type is overridden by the extent of disparity. In humans, inner qualities vary so much that the benefit one partner gets from a specific slave may be vastly different from another's, unlike in animals. Thus, the case is excluded from the general rule.
This rebuttal is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) based on the purpose of division.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a dispute arises over a slave, a judge following Abu Hanifa would not force a division unless all owners consent. A judge following the Sahibayn would order division at the request of any co-owner. The mufta bihi in later Hanafi practice is often the view of the Sahibayn for convenience.
The text does not give a clear tarjih, but later fatwa collections lean toward the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القسمة / تعريفها وأحكامها
إن تضرر كل للقلة وقسم عروض اتحد جنسها لا الجنسان والرقيق والجواهر والحمام إلا برضاهم، قالا : يقسم الرقيق والجواهر بطلب البعض، كما يقسم الإبل وسائر العروض، له : أن التفاوت فاحش في الآدمي فصار كالأجناس المختلفة لأنَّ القسمة لتكميل المنفعة، وفي هذا تقويتها، فإن طلبوا والتزموا الضرر عليهم فلهم الأمر ؛ لأنَّ الحق لهم، وهم أعرف لشأنهم. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية». [١] قوله : إن تضرّر كلّ للقلة ؛ بأن كان بيتاً صغيراً بينهما لم يقسمها عند طلب أحدهما ؛ لأنَّ الطالب للقسمة متعنّت للآخر. [٢] قوله : وقسم عروض ... الخ ؛ لأنَّ عند اتحاد الجنس يتحد المقصود، فيحصل التعديل في القسمة، والتكميل في المنفعة. [٣] قوله : لا الجنسان ؛ عطف على ما أقيم مقام الفاعل ؛ أي عروض، أو على الجملة بتقدير الفعل؛ أي لا يقسم الجنسان المختلفان اسماً ومعني، بأن يجعل حصة أحد في جنس واحد، وحصة آخر في جنس آخر ; لفحش التفاوت، فيقسمان قسمة فرد، بأن يقسم كل جنس بانفراده. فلو كان المقسوم إبلاً وغنماً مثلاً، لم يجمع نصيب أحد من الوارثين في الإبل خاصة، ونصيب الآخر منهما في الغنم خاصة، بل يقسم الإبل بينهما، ثم الغنم كذلك، وعلى هذا المكيل والموزون وغير ذلك. فوائد عارفية». [٤]قوله : وقالا : يقسم الرقيق ; لاتحاد الجنس، والتفاوت في الجنس الواحد لا يمنع القسمة، كما في الإبل والغنم، ولهذا يقسم الرقيق في الغنيمة بين الغانمين. كذا في تكملة البحر» [١] قوله : له إنّ التفاوت فاحش ; لتفاوت المعاني الباطنة، كالذهن والكياسة، بخلاف سائر الحيوانات؛ لأنّ الانتفاع بهما لا يختلفُ إلا شيئاً يسيراً، وذلك مفتقر في (۱) تكملة البحر» (۸: ۱۷۲).
Book of Division / Its Definition and Rulings
If each is harmed due to smallness [of share], and division of goods of the same type is permitted, not [of] two different types, and [not of] slaves, jewels, and pigeons except with their consent. The two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: Slaves and jewels are divided upon the request of some [partners], just as camels and other goods are divided. He [Abu Hanifa] argued: Disparity is great in humans, so they become like different types because division is to complete benefit, and in this [slaves/jewels] benefit is strengthened. If they request and accept the harm upon themselves, then they have the right, because the right is theirs, and they know their own affairs better. This is in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyyah. [1] His saying: 'If each is harmed due to smallness' – like a small house between them, it is not divided at the request of one of them because the requester of division is being obstinate towards the other. [2] His saying: 'And division of goods...' – because when the type is the same, the purpose is unified, so equalization in division and completion of benefit is achieved. [3] His saying: 'Not two different types' – it is a conjunction on what is in place of the subject, i.e., goods, or on the sentence with a verb implied, i.e., the two different types are not divided in name and meaning, such that one's share is made of one type and the other's share of another type due to great disparity. Rather, they are divided by individual division, each type separately. For example, if the divided property is camels and sheep, one heir's share is not placed entirely in camels and the other's entirely in sheep; rather, camels are divided between them, then sheep likewise, and similarly for measured and weighed items and others. 'Fawa'id 'Arifiyyah'. [4] His saying: 'And the two said: Slaves are divided' – because of the unity of type, and disparity within one type does not prevent division, as with camels and sheep, and for this reason slaves are divided as spoils among the plunderers. This is in Takmilat al-Bahr. [1] His saying: 'He argued: Disparity is great' – due to the disparity of inner qualities, like intelligence and acumen, unlike other animals, because benefit from them does not differ except slightly, and this requires [explanation] in Takmilat al-Bahr (8:172).
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage covers two sub-rulings on division. First, if both partners would be harmed by division due to the property's small size (e.g., a tiny house), division is not permitted upon request of one, as the requester is being obstinate. Second, the matn states that goods ('urud) of the same type may be divided, but not goods of different types lumped together, nor slaves, jewels, or pigeons unless all partners consent. The commentator then presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that slaves and jewels can be divided upon the request of some partners, just like camels or other goods, because they are of a single type. Abu Hanifa argues against this, stating that the disparity among humans (e.g., intelligence, skill) is so great that they become like different types, thus division without consent leads to injustice. However, if all partners willingly accept the division despite the harm, it is valid. The Sahibayn's analogy with camels is countered by Abu Hanifa's emphasis on the significant inner differences in humans. The ruling on pigeons follows the same logic as slaves and jewels due to their varying quality.

Key Terms
عروض (a'rad / 'urud): Goods other than real estate, like merchandise, animals, etc.
الرقيق (al-raqiq): Slaves; human property.
تفاوت فاحش (tafawut fahish): Exorbitant disparity that defeats the purpose of equal division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the main reason slaves and jewels are not subject to compulsory division?
A) They are not considered property.
B) The disparity within them is so great they become like different types.
C) They are excluded from the definition of goods ('urud).
D) Division only applies to real estate.

Key Takeaway
The division of slaves and jewels sparks an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa requires unanimous consent due to exorbitant disparity, while the Sahibayn allow compulsory division based on unity of type.

Forced Division of Slaves and Jewels: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on forced division of slaves and jewels with detailed proofs and caveats.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on forced division of slaves and jewels, distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from the Sahibayn, and apply the exclusionary rules (genus, gender, subsidiary property) to determine when forced division is permissible.

Core Idea
Slaves and jewels are not forcibly divided because excessive disparity in humans and precious stones makes them akin to different genera.

In the law of property division, the general rule is that goods of the same genus can be divided at the request of one co-owner. But this chapter introduces a crucial exception: slaves, jewels, and pigeons are not subject to compulsory division unless all parties consent. The exception is contested between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The disagreement turns on whether the disparity within the 'genus' of humans (or jewels) is so great that forced division would cause injustice.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule of compulsory division
Identify that goods of the same genus (jins wāḥid) can be divided forcibly at the request of one co-owner, but goods of different genera cannot.
The general rule applies to most chattels like camels, sheep, etc.
2
Identify the exception for slaves, jewels, and pigeons
Note that the matn states these are not divided without consent. This is the starting point of the dispute.
Pigeons are included due to extreme variation in value (e.g., racing pigeons vs. ordinary).
3
Present the Sahibayn's view and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that slaves/jewels of the same genus (all male or all female) can be forcibly divided, analogizing to camels and other goods where within-genus disparity does not prevent division.
They also cite the practice of dividing slaves in war booty as evidence.
4
Present Abu Hanifa's response and distinction
Abu Hanifa counters that the disparity among humans is excessive (fāḥish) in inner qualities, and that male and female humans are different genera, breaking the analogy to animals.
He also notes that jewels vary greatly in value, making forced division unjust.
5
Note the caveats: mixed genders and subsidiary division
The passage clarifies that the dispute only applies when slaves are all male or all female; mixed genders are not divisible by consensus. Additionally, if other goods are present, slaves can be divided as a subsidiary by consensus.
This shows the nuanced application of the rule in practice.

Source Text

Book of Division / Its definition and rulings. If all are harmed due to smallness, and division of goods of the same genus (not of two genera), and slaves, jewels, and pigeons [are not divided] except with their consent if all are harmed due to smallness. And division of goods (3) of the same genus, not of two genera, and slaves, jewels, and pigeons except with their consent. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Slaves and jewels are divided at the request of one, just as camels and other goods are divided. To him (Abu Hanifa): the disparity is excessive in humans, so they become like different genera. Because division is for completing benefit, and in this is strengthening it. If they request and accept the harm upon themselves, then the matter is theirs, because the right is theirs, and they are more knowledgeable of their own affair. So in 'al-Fawaid al-‘Arifiyya'. [1] His statement: 'If all are harmed due to smallness' – such as a small house between two, it is not divided at the request of one, because the one requesting division is being obstinate to the other. [2] His statement: 'And division of goods...' – because when the genus is unified, the purpose is unified, so adjustment in division and completion in benefit is achieved. [3] His statement: 'Not of two genera' – an appendix to what is in the position of the agent, i.e., goods, or upon the sentence with an implied verb, i.e., two different genera in name and meaning are not divided, such that one's share is made in one genus and the other's share in another genus due to excessive disparity. Rather, they are divided by individual division, i.e., each genus is divided separately. For example, if the divided property is camels and sheep, the share of one heir is not confined to camels only and the share of the other to sheep only; rather, the camels are divided between them, then similarly the sheep, and likewise for measured and weighed items and others. 'Fawa'id 'Arifiyya'. [4] His statement: 'And they said: he divides slaves' – due to unity of genus, and disparity within the same genus does not prevent division, as in camels and sheep, and therefore slaves are divided in booty among the spoilers. So in 'Takmilat al-Bahr'. [01] His statement: 'To him: the disparity is excessive' – due to disparity of inner qualities, such as intellect and acumen, unlike other animals, because benefit from them does not differ except slightly, and that requires in [؟] Husn al-Diraya li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqaya, and a shared house, or a small house. [1] And regarding jewels, it has been said: if the genus differs, it is not divided. And shared house, or small house – division, do you not see that male and female among humans are two different genera, and among animals they are one genus? So analogy is not permissible, and division of spoils occurs across genera, so it is not binding. (1) This disagreement is when the slaves are alone, and there is no other item of goods with them, and they are all male or all female. But if they are mixed between males and females, they are not divided by consensus, because males and females among humans are two genera due to difference in purposes. And if there is something else with the slaves that is to be divided, division of the slaves is permissible as a subsidiary to others by consensus, and the judge compels them at the request of one. And many a thing enters as a subsidiary even though it is not permissible to enter as a primary. So in 'Takmilat al-Bahr'.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on division (qisma) presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the compulsory division of slaves and jewels. The base ruling (matn) states that if all co-owners would be harmed by division due to smallness, then division is not forced. It also states that when dividing goods (ʿurūḍ), if they are of the same genus (jins), the judge can force division; if of different genera, he cannot. Slaves, jewels, and pigeons are excepted: they are not divided compulsorily at all, regardless of genus, unless all parties consent. The commentator then reports that Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf (the two companions, referred to as 'qālā') hold that slaves and jewels should be divided at the request of one party, just like camels and other goods of the same genus, because they share the same genus. Their proof is that slaves are all of one genus (human), and disparity within a genus does not prevent division, as seen with camels and sheep. This is also supported by the practice of dividing slaves among soldiers in war booty. Abu Hanifa (referred to as 'lahu') counters that the disparity among humans is excessive (fāḥish) because humans differ greatly in inner qualities like intelligence and acumen. Therefore, slaves become like items of different genera, and forced division is unjust. He distinguishes humans from animals, noting that male and female among humans are considered different genera due to differing purposes, whereas among animals they are one genus. Thus, analogy (qiyās) to other goods fails. The passage then clarifies that this dispute applies only when the slaves are all male or all female and there is no other property mixed with them. If the slaves include both males and females, there is consensus they cannot be forcibly divided because males and females are different genera among humans. However, if slaves are accompanied by other goods that are divisible, the slaves may be divided as a subsidiary to those goods by consensus, and the judge can compel division. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of following the majority or primary item in division, where the subordinate follows the ruling of the primary.

Key Terms
الرقيق (al-raqīq): Slaves, human beings held as property.
الجواهر (al-jawāhir): Jewels, precious stones.
القسمة (al-qisma): Division, partition of jointly owned property into separate shares.
التفاوت الفاحش (al-tafāwut al-fāḥish): Excessive disparity in value or qualities, preventing compulsory division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why are slaves not subject to compulsory division even if they are of the same genus?
A) Because slaves are not property.
B) Because the disparity among humans in intellect and acumen is excessive.
C) Because slaves are always considered different genera.
D) Because the Sahibayn's view is stronger.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school records a fundamental disagreement on forced division of slaves and jewels, rooted in the extent to which disparity within a genus prevents compulsory partition.

Forced Division of Jointly Owned Property: Conditions and Restrictions
Rulings on compulsory division of various jointly owned items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of jointly owned property can be forcibly divided and which cannot, and state the Hanafi conditions for compulsory division.

Core Idea
Forced division is only allowed when it does not cause excessive harm or destroy the property's utility.

The Hanafi school carefully balances co-owners' rights with preserving property value. Not every jointly owned item can be divided by force; the law protects against destruction of value or harm to all. This passage from Sharh al-Wiqaya details specific cases — from houses and jewels to sewn garments and wells — illustrating the principle that compulsory division must be possible without excessive loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching condition
The key condition for compulsory division is that division must not cause excessive harm (ḍarar) to all co-owners. If division would ruin the item or significantly reduce its value, it is not forced.
2
Classify the property type
Determine whether the property is immovable (house, well) or movable, and whether it is of a single genus or mixed genera. Same-genus items are more likely to be divisible.
Mixed genera (e.g., male and female slaves) are treated as different genera and not forcibly divided.
3
Apply the harm test to each example
For each item mentioned, check if division would cause harm: e.g., sewn garment is ruined by cutting; pigeon house, well, mill lose their function; unseparated property cannot be divided before separation.
4
Distinguish forced vs. voluntary division
All items that cannot be forcibly divided can still be divided by mutual consent. The judge only compels division when conditions are met.
5
Recognize scholarly differences
Note the varying opinions on jewels and other items. Some scholars allow division of small jewels with minor disparity; others require same genus. This intra-Hanafi variation affects practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وفي «التجريد»: لو أوصى لهما بصوف على ظهر غنم أو لبن في ضرع، أو بما في بطون الغنم لا يقسم قبل الجزّ والحلب والولادة، وفي الخانية»: إذا كان بين رجلين ثوب مخيط لا يقسم القاضي بينهم ، ولو غير مخيط فاقتسماه طولاً أو عرضاً جازت القسمة، وأما الحمام وكذا البئر والرّحى فلما ذُكِرَ من إلحاق الضرر بالكل، ولو اقتسما

So he said in 'Takmilat al-Bahr': As for jewels, because their ignorance (unknown quality) is excessive; do you not see that other than the specific item is not suitable as compensation for something that is not property, like marriage and khulʿ? And it has been said: large ones are not divided due to excessive disparity, and small ones are divided due to little disparity. And it has been said: if their genus differs, they are not divided; if they are of the same genus, they are divided like other genera. And clarification of the subject is in the texts and commentaries: it is stated in 'al-Atābiyya': A kettle and a basin made of brass are treated like things of different genera, so they are not divided compulsorily. Likewise, garments made of cotton and linen when they differ in workmanship, like a cloak, robe, and shirt. And in 'Mukhtaṣar Khāharzādah': A saddle, a mattress, and a Qur'an are not divided. And in 'al-Tajrīd': If he bequeaths to two persons wool on the back of sheep, or milk in an udder, or what is in the bellies of sheep, it is not divided before shearing, milking, and birth. And in 'al-Khāniyya': If two men have a sewn garment, the judge does not divide it between them; if it is unsewn, and they divide it lengthwise or widthwise, the division is valid. As for the pigeon house, similarly the well and the mill, due to the mentioned harm to all. And if they divide...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of compulsory division in Hanafi fiqh, focusing on special cases. It first reiterates the ruling on shared houses: a small house (dār wadīʿa) cannot be forcibly divided because it would harm all co-owners. The text then presents different opinions on jewels (jawāhir). Some say that if the genus differs, they are not divided. Others distinguish between large jewels (not divided due to excessive disparity) and small ones (divided due to little disparity). Still others hold that jewels follow the general rule: if of the same genus, they are divided, otherwise not. The commentator cites 'Takmilat al-Bahr' explaining that jewels are problematic because their quality is highly uncertain; thus, they are not suitable as exchange for non-property items like marriage or khulʿ. Further rulings: items like brass kettles and basins are treated as different genera and not divided compulsorily. Clothes made of mixed materials (cotton and linen) with different workmanship are also treated as different genera. Specific items that cannot be divided at all: a saddle, a mattress, and a copy of the Qur'an (likely because division would destroy their utility). Unseparated property (wool on sheep, milk in udder, fetuses) cannot be divided before separation. A sewn garment cannot be divided because division would ruin it, but an unsewn garment can be divided by splitting lengthwise or widthwise. Finally, a pigeon house (ḥamām), well (biʾr), and mill (raḥā) cause harm to all if divided, so they are not subject to compulsory division. The passage ends mid-sentence, discussing permissive division by agreement.

Key Terms
الدور المشتركة (al-dūr al-mushtaraka): Shared houses; if small, they cannot be forcibly divided due to harm to all.
الحمام (al-ḥamām): Pigeon house; also used metaphorically for similar structures like wells and mills that cannot be divided without harm.
الجواهر (al-jawāhir): Precious jewels; their division depends on the extent of disparity in quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a sewn garment owned jointly by two men?
A) The judge must divide it equally.
B) The judge does not divide it because division would ruin it.
C) It can be divided if one party requests.
D) It is treated like an unsewn garment.

Key Takeaway
Compulsory division in Hanafi fiqh is only allowed when it does not cause excessive harm, destroy utility, or involve excessive disparity; many items cannot be forcibly divided.

Dividing Co-Owned Houses: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partition method for houses in same city

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on dividing co-owned houses in the same city and identify the practical consequence (thamarah) of each position.

Core Idea
Proximity changes the rule: Abu Hanifa splits each house; the Sahibayn combine them to avoid tiny shares.

When two or more people co-own multiple houses in the same city, how should the division be carried out? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, offer different answers. This lesson unpacks their reasoning, based on a passage from the Hanafi commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The passage concerns the division (qisma) of houses and shops that are co-owned. Determine the exact point of disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
The disagreement only arises when houses are in the same city (misr wahid); for distant cities, all agree on separate division.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that houses are different types (ajnas) because their value depends on location, neighbors, and proximity to the mosque. This makes equalization impossible unless each house is divided separately.
He treats each house as a distinct unit for partition.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
The two disciples argue that houses in the same city share a common name and form, so they can be divided collectively. Dividing each separately may harm a partner with a small share.
They prioritize the benefit of avoiding fragmented shares.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, each house must be split proportionally among partners, so each partner gets a fraction of every house. Under the Sahibayn, entire houses can be assigned to different partners if values match, avoiding divided ownership of each house.
Example: Two partners own two identical houses in the same city. Abu Hanifa gives each partner half of each house; Sahibayn allow giving one whole house to each partner.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو دار وحانوت قسم كُلِّ وحدها) : أي إذا كانت الدور قريبة بأن كانت كلها في مصر واحد، قسم كل وحدَها عند أبي حنيفة ، وقالا : يقسم بعضُها في بعض، وإن كانت الدور بعيدة : أي في مصرين، فقولهما كقول أبي حنيفة له.

[١] قوله : عند أبي حنيفة الله ; له : إنّ الدور أجناس مختلفة ; لاختلاف المقصودِ باعتبار المحال والجيران والقرب من المسجد، فكان اختلافاً فاحشاً، فلا يمكن التعديل في القسمة، فلا يجوز جمع نصيب كلِّ واحدٍ في دار إلا بالتراضي، والساحة كالدور؛ لأنه بين البيت والدار، فأخذ حظه من كلّ واحدٍ منهما كذا. في تكملة البحر»).

[٢] قوله : وقالا : يقسم بعضها في بعض ; إذا كانت في مصر واحد، وكانت القسمة أصلح لهم ; لأنهم جنس واحد ، نظراً إلى اتحاد الاسم والصورة، وأصلُ السكني جنسان; نظراً إلى اختلافِ الأغراض، وتفاوت السكني، وإذا قسم كلّ دارٍ على حدة ربّما يتضرّر لقلة نصيبه . كذا في تكملة البحر (۲)
(Or a house and a shop: each is divided separately.) Meaning: if the houses are close, i.e., all in one city, each is divided separately according to Abu Hanifa, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: some of them are divided with others. If the houses are distant, i.e., in two cities, their statement is like Abu Hanifa's statement. [1] His statement: According to Abu Hanifa, his proof: indeed houses are different types due to the difference in purpose considering locations, neighbors, and proximity to the mosque, so the difference is great, thus equalization in division is not possible, so it is not permissible to combine the share of each one in a house except with mutual consent. And the courtyard is like houses because it is between the house and the building/place, so he takes his portion from each of them. From Takmilat al-Bahr. [2] His statement: And they two said: some are divided with others if they are in one city and the division is more beneficial for them because they are one type, considering the unity of name and form, and the origin of residence is two types considering the difference of purposes and variation of residence, and if each house is divided separately, perhaps one would be harmed due to the smallness of his share. From Takmilat al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the division of houses and shops when co-owned. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that each property must be divided separately even if they are in the same city, because houses differ in value and utility depending on location, neighbors, proximity to mosque etc. Thus, combining them would lead to inequity unless the partners agree. The two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, hold that if the houses are in the same city, they can be divided collectively because they share the same general purpose and name; dividing each separately might result in small, unusable shares. However, if the houses are in different cities, there is no disagreement and each is divided separately. This is from Takmilat al-Bahr. The commentary elaborates the reasoning of each.

Key Terms
دار (dar): A house or dwelling, considered as a type of immovable property subject to partition.
حانوت (hanut): A shop or commercial property, similar to a house in partition rules.
قسمة (qisma): Division or partition of jointly owned property to allocate shares to each partner.
مصر (misr): A city or large town; determines whether properties are considered close or distant for partition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, when may houses in the same city be divided collectively?
A) Only when partners agree.
B) Always, because they are one type.
C) When the division is more beneficial for the partners.
D) Never; they must always be divided separately.

Key Takeaway
Houses in the same city are divided separately per Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn allow collective division to avoid harm from small shares.

Ruling on Division of Baths, Wells, and Canals
Permissibility and method of dividing shared water sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on dividing shared water sources and distinguish between the two narrations regarding the judge's authority.

Core Idea
Division of a shared water source is permissible if partners consent, but a judge may not order it if it causes loss of benefit.

This passage from Hanafi fiqh addresses the division of common water sources such as baths, wells, and canals. The key issue is under what conditions division is permitted, particularly when sought through a court order. The text presents two conflicting narrations from the Hanafi school, reflecting a difference of opinion on the judge's authority. Understanding this ruling requires careful distinction between the parties' agreement and judicial enforcement, as well as the nature of the property being divided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is dividing a shared water source (bath, well, canal). The default ruling is that if partners themselves agree, it is permissible provided each can derive some benefit.
This is the baseline; court involvement changes the analysis.
2
Distinguish the two riwayat on judicial division
When partners seek a judge's order, there are two narrations: one prohibits the judge from dividing (due to loss of benefit), the other permits (if partners consented). Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: whether the judge can enforce division.
The second narration is linked to the phrase 'except with their consent' in the text.
3
Apply the ruling to water sources with and without land
For canals, wells, or rivers without attached land, division is not allowed even by partners (according to one source). If there is land with irrigation rights, the land is divided and the water right remains shared. If each partner can source water elsewhere, then division of the water source itself is allowed.
The presence of land is a critical factor; without it, the utility is inseparable.
4
Note the authority of the cited sources
The text references al-Asl (zahir al-riwayah) and al-Nawadir (nawadir). Al-Asl's ruling (divide wells and rivers for different lands) carries stronger authority. Al-Nawadir's permission to divide a well by ropes is a less authoritative variant.
Understanding source hierarchy helps weigh conflicting rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمام أو البئر بأنفسهم جاز، ولكلّ واحدٍ منهم نوعُ منفعة، بأن يتخذ نصيبه من بيتاً، وإن طلبا جميعاً القسمة من القاضي هل يقسم، فيه روايتان : في رواية لا يقسم ; لأنها تضمنت تفويت منفعة، وليس للقاضي ذلك ; لأنه يكون سفها يمكنه. وفي رواية : يقسم ; لأنهم رضوا بذلك، وإليه أشار المصنف له بقوله : إلا برضائهم ; لأنه فيه نوع منفعة. كذا في «المحيط»، وفي «التاتارخانية» : وإذا كانت قناة أو بئر أو نهر ، وليس معه أرض فأرادَ بعض الشركاء القسمة، فإنّها لا تقسم، وإذا كانت أرض لها شرب قسم الأرض وترك الشرب، والقناة والبئر كالشركة. وفي «الخلاصة»: ولكل منهما شربه ، وإن كان يقدر كلُّ واحدٍ منهما أن يجعل لأرضه شرباً من موضع آخر قسم ذلك كله فيما بينهم، وفي «الأصل»: لو كانت أنهاراً وآباراً لأرض مختلفة قسم الآبار والعيون والأراضي، وفي «النوادر»: ولو قسم البئر بالحبال جاز ; لأنَّ التفاوت فيها قليل.
The bath or the well: if they themselves (the partners) divide it, it is permissible, and each one of them has a type of benefit, such as making his share a room. And if both request division from the judge, is it divided? In this, there are two narrations: In one narration, it is not divided because it entails loss of benefit, and the judge does not have that (authority) because it would be foolishness that he can (prevent?). In another narration, it is divided because they consented to that, and the author pointed to it by his statement: except with their consent, because there is a type of benefit in it. Thus in al-Muhit. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If there is a canal, well, or river with no land, and one of the partners wants division, it is not divided. If there is land with irrigation rights, the land is divided and the irrigation right is left; the canal and well are like the partnership. In al-Khulasa: Each of them has his irrigation right, and if each of them can provide irrigation for his land from another place, all of that is divided among them. In al-Asl: If there were rivers and wells for different lands, the wells, springs, and lands are divided. In al-Nawadir: If the well is divided by ropes, it is permissible because the disparity in it is slight.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage addresses the division of shared water sources such as baths, wells, canals, and rivers. The general principle is that if the partners themselves agree to divide a bath or well, it is permissible as long as each can derive some benefit, e.g., converting his share into a room. However, if they seek court intervention, there are two opinions: one prohibits the judge from ordering division because it would waste the utility, while the other permits it if the partners consented. For canals, wells, or rivers without accompanying land, division is not allowed because they are indivisible utilities. If they serve a specific land, the land is divided but the water right remains shared. The text cites several sources: al-Muhit, al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Khulasa, al-Asl, and al-Nawadir, showing variations in rulings based on circumstances. Notably, dividing a well using ropes (a method measuring shares by length of rope) is deemed permissible in al-Nawadir because the discrepancy in water volume is negligible.

Key Terms
حمام (hammam): A public bathhouse; considered a type of property that may be divided.
بئر (bi'r): A well; shared water source subject to division rules.
قناة (qanat): An underground water channel or qanat; similar to well.
شرب (shurb): Irrigation right or water share

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to one riwaya in the passage, why does the judge not divide a bath?
A) Because baths cannot be divided physically.
B) Because it entails loss of benefit and the judge lacks authority.
C) Because the partners always agree.
D) Because the bath is a public utility.

Key Takeaway
Division of water sources in Hanafi law is permissible with partners' consent, but judicial division is limited to cases where benefit is preserved.

Duties of the Qasim (Divider) and Procedure for Drawing Lots
Procedure for the divider: describe, survey, value, and assign shares with path and water right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended duties of the qasim in partitioning co-owned property and apply the procedure for drawing lots to allocate shares equitably.

Core Idea
The divider must describe, measure, value, and assign shares with access rights; drawing lots ensures fairness and removes bias.

When co-owners wish to divide property, they often appoint a divider (qasim). This lesson explains the recommended steps the divider should follow to ensure an equitable partition: from writing down shares to conducting a lottery. The passage also demonstrates, with an example, how to allocate lots when shares are unequal, emphasizing that the lottery is a tool for transparency and harmony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended duties of the qasim
List the actions the divider should perform: describe (write down shares), equalize (adjust for fairness), measure (survey the land), value any buildings, and separate each share with its path and water right.
These are sunnah (recommended), not mandatory, but following them prevents disputes.
2
Understand the lottery (qur'a) procedure
The divider prepares numbered lots equal to the number of shares (based on the smallest fraction). Names are drawn; the first drawn gets the first lot, etc. The allocation of physical portions follows the drawn sequence.
Example: For shares 1/6, 1/2, 1/3, divide into six lots. The smallest share determines the unit size.
3
Analyze the example with a son and daughter
If heirs are a son and daughter, the land is divided into three thirds. The daughter's name goes into the lottery; if drawn first, she takes one third, and the son takes two thirds. This illustrates how the lottery is adapted for unequal Quranic shares.
The lottery is a recommended means to 'please hearts' and avoid suspicion of bias.
4
Evaluate the role of building valuation
Buildings are divided separately. The divider values them so that if a building falls into a share, the receiving party knows its value for potential compensation or adjustment.
This ensures that no one gains an unfair advantage from the building's location.
5
Distinguish sunnah from required elements
While describing, surveying, and assigning path/water rights are sunnah, the physical demarcation (ark) is necessary for valid partition. Omission of sunan does not invalidate but may cause future disputes.
The passage says 'statement of the best practice' (afdal), confirming these are recommended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصور القاسم ما يقسمُ ويعدله ويذرعُه ويقومُ بناءه ويفرز كل قسم بطريقه وشربه

[١]قوله : ويصور...الخ ; أي إذا شرع القاسم في القسمة ينبغي أن يصوّر مــا يقسمه ; أي يكتب على كاغده : إنّ فلاناً نصيبه كذا ، وفلاناً كذا ; ليمكنه حفظه إذا أراد رفع ذلك الكاغد إلى القاضي ; ليتولى الإقراعَ بينهم بنفسه. كذا في «العناية»(۱). [٢] قوله : ويصوّر...الخ ; قال الإمام حميد الدين : صــورته : أرض بــين جماعة ; لأحدهم سدسها ، وللآخر نصفها ، وللآخر ثلثها، يجعلها ستة أسهم، ويلقب الجزء الأول بالسهم الأوّل، والذي يليه بالثاني، وعلى هذا، ويكتب أساميهم، ويجعلها قرعة، فمن خرج اسمه أوّلاً فله السهم الأوّل، فإن كان ذلك السهم بأن كان صاحب السدس فله الجزء الأوّل، وإن كان صاحب الثلث فله الجزء الأول، والذي يليه، وإن كان صاحب النصف وله الجزء الأول، واللذان يليانه.
فالحاصل : إنه يخرج القرعة، فمن خرج اسمه أوّلاً فله السهم الأول، ومن خرج اسمه ثانياً فله السهم الثاني، وهذا الطريق إنّما يستقيمُ إذا كانت الأنصباء متساوية، والأصل أن ينظر إلى أقل الأنصباء حتى إذا كانَ الأقل ثلثاً جعلها أثلاثاً، وإن سدسها جعلها أسداساً ; ليمكن القسمة.
صورة المسألة : إذا كان الوارث ابناً وبنتاً، فيكتب على القرعة اسمها وسمّى الثلث المسمّى بالأوّل مع ما يليه ويتعين الثلث الآخر للبنت، ولو خرج اسم البنتِ أولاً تأخذ البنت الثلث المسمّى بالأوّل، ويتعين الثلثان الآخران للابن، وإنما شرعت القرعة لتطيب القلوب، وإزاحة تهمة الميل هذا. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية». [٣] قوله : يقـوم بناءه....الخ ; لحاجته إليه بالآخر، إذ البناء يقسم على حدة، فربما يقع في نصيب أحدهم شيءٌ منه، فيكون عالماً بقيمتها قاله الجلبي (٢) [٤]قوله : ويفرز كل قسم بطريقة وشربه ... الخ ; بيان الأفضل، فإن لم يفعل أو
And the divider describes what he divides, equalizes it, measures it, values its building, and separates each share with its path and irrigation right. [1] His statement: 'And describes...' meaning when the divider commences division, he should describe what he divides, i.e., write on his paper: 'So-and-so's share is such, and so-and-so's share is such,' so that he can preserve it when he wishes to submit that paper to the judge so that he (the judge) may personally supervise the drawing of lots among them. From al-Inaya. [2] His statement: 'And describes...' Imam Hamid al-Din said: Its form: Land among a group; one has a sixth, another half, another a third. He makes it six shares, labeling the first portion as the first lot, the next as second, and so on, and writes their names, and makes it a lottery. Whose name comes out first gets the first lot; if that lot corresponds to the one with the sixth, he gets the first portion; if the one with the third, he gets the first and the next; if the one with the half, he gets the first and the two following. In summary: He draws lots. Whose name is drawn first gets the first lot, whose name is drawn second gets the second lot. This method works when the shares are equal; the principle is to look at the smallest share so that if the smallest is a third, he makes it into thirds; if a sixth, into sixths, to enable division. Example: If the heirs are a son and a daughter, he writes her name on the lot, and the third labeled 'first' with what follows it is determined, and the other third goes to the daughter? Actually: If the girl's name comes out first, she takes the third labeled 'first', and the other two-thirds go to the son. Indeed, the lottery was legislated to please hearts and remove suspicion of bias. From al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyya. [3] His statement: 'values its building...' because of need in the end, since the building is divided separately, so perhaps something of it falls into someone's share, so he would know its value. Al-Jalabi said. [4] His statement: 'and separates each share with its path and irrigation right...' is a statement of the best practice. If he does not do so or...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage outlines the procedural duties of the qasim (the divider appointed to partition co-owned property). First, he must describe the property in writing, specifying each party's share, so that he can present it to the judge for the official lottery (qur'a). The commentator from al-Inaya emphasizes that this written record ensures accuracy. Second, Imam Hamid al-Din provides a detailed example of how to divide land among partners with unequal shares (1/6, 1/2, 1/3) by converting the whole into six equal lots and assigning lots based on the smallest share. Lot drawing is performed based on names, and the allocation of physical portions follows the sequence of drawn lots. The method assumes equal shares if the shares are equal; otherwise, the land is divided into units equal to the denominator of the smallest fraction. An example with a son and daughter (heirs) illustrates how lots are used to assign thirds and a reminder that the lottery is a procedural means to ensure fairness and avoid accusations of bias. The divider must also value any buildings on the property separately so that if a building lies in someone's share, its value is known for equitable adjustment. Finally, the passage notes that separating each share with its path (access road) and irrigation right is the best practice, implying it is recommended but not mandatory.

Key Terms
قاسم (qasim): The person appointed to divide co-owned property.
قرعة (qur'a): A lottery or drawing of lots to allocate shares randomly.
سهم (sahm): A lot or share; also the numbered unit in the lottery.
تعديل (ta'dil): Equalization or adjusting shares to make them equitable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 3
B) 6
C) 12
D) Based on the number of heirs

Key Takeaway
The qasim follows a recommended procedure of describing, measuring, valuing, and assigning shares with access rights; the lottery is a sunnah tool to ensure fairness and remove suspicion.

The Divider's Duties: Depiction, Leveling, Measuring, Valuing, and Separating
Procedural ruling on divider's duties with commentary and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five actions a divider must perform before dividing property and explain the purpose of each, including the role of lot-drawing (qur'a).

Core Idea
The divider must depict, level, measure, value buildings, and separate shares—each step ensures fairness and prevents disputes.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah outlines the recommended procedure for a divider (qasim) when partitioning jointly owned property. The matn lists five actions, and the commentary unpacks each with practical examples. You will learn not only the steps but also the underlying wisdom: meticulous documentation and lot-drawing are tools to ensure impartiality and satisfy the co-owners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five actions
Locate the five verbs in the first line of the matn: yusawwir (depicts), yaddil (levels), yadra'u (measures), yuqawwimu (values), yufrizu (separates). Write them down with their meanings.
These are the core duties of the qasim.
2
Understand the purpose of taswir (depiction)
Read the commentary on 'yuṣawwir'. It explains that the divider writes each share on paper so that the judge can later supervise the lot-drawing. The depiction is both a record and a preparation for qur'a.
Documentation prevents disputes and enables judicial oversight.
3
Analyze the lot-drawing example
Work through the example with three owners holding 1/6, 1/2, and 1/3. The divider makes 6 shares, labels them 1–6, writes heirs' names on separate slips. The first draw gets share 1, second gets share 2, etc., but the actual allocation groups shares proportionally.
This method works only if shares are expressed as a common denominator.
4
Apply to the son-daughter example
In the case of a son and daughter (inheritance shares typically 2:1), the divider must adjust. The text shows a method: write the daughter's name on one lot; assign shares accordingly. Notice the principle: the smallest share unit determines the base number.
Lot-drawing is not random; it implements predetermined shares.
5
Explain the remaining duties
Ta'dil (leveling) ensures portions are equal in value, not just area. Tazri' (measuring) gives exact dimensions. Taqwim (valuing buildings) is needed because buildings are indivisible; they are allocated to one party with compensation. Ifraz (separating) must include pathways and water rights.
These steps ensure the division is equitable and complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصور القاسم ما يقسم ويعدله ويذرعُه ويقومُ بناءه ويفرز كل قسم بطريقه وشربه

قوله : ويصور...الخ؛ أي إذا شرع القاسم في القسمة ينبغي أن يصوّر ما يقسمه؛ أي يكتب على كاغده: إنّ فلاناً نصيبه كذا، وفلاناً كذا؛ ليمكنه حفظه إذا أراد رفع ذلك الكاغد إلى القاضي؛ ليتولى الإقراعَ بينهم بنفسه. كذا في «العناية».

قوله : ويصوّر...الخ؛ قال الإمام حميد الدين : صورته : أرض بين جماعة؛ لأحدهم سدسها، وللآخر نصفها، وللآخر ثلثها، يجعلها ستة أسهم، ويلقب الجزء الأول بالسهم الأوّل، والذي يليه بالثاني، وعلى هذا، ويكتب أساميهم، ويجعلها قرعة، فمن خرج اسمه أوّلاً فله السهم الأوّل، فإن كان ذلك السهم بأن كان صاحب السدس فله الجزء الأوّل، وإن كان صاحب الثلث فله الجزء الأول، والذي يليه، وإن كان صاحب النصف وله الجزء الأول، واللذان يليانه.

فالحاصل : إنه يخرج القرعة، فمن خرج اسمه أوّلاً فله السهم الأول، ومن خرج اسمه ثانياً فله السهم الثاني، وهذا الطريق إنّما يستقيمُ إذا كانت الأنصباء متساوية، والأصل أن ينظر إلى أقل الأنصباء حتى إذا كانَ الأقل ثلثاً جعلها أثلاثاً، وإن سدسها جعلها أسداساً؛ ليمكن القسمة.

صورة المسألة : إذا كان الوارث ابناً وبنتاً، فيكتب على القرعة اسمها وسمّى الثلث المسمّى بالأوّل مع ما يليه ويتعين الثلث الآخر للبنت، ولو خرج اسم البنتِ أولاً تأخذ البنت الثلث المسمّى بالأوّل، ويتعين الثلثان الآخران للابن، وإنما شرعت القرعة لتطيب القلوب، وإزاحة تهمة الميل هذا. كذا في «الفوائد العارفية».

قوله : يقـوم بناءه....الخ؛ لحاجته إليه بالآخر، إذ البناء يقسم على حدة، فربما يقع في نصيب أحدهم شيءٌ منه، فيكون عالماً بقيمتها قاله الجلبي

قوله : ويفرز كل قسم بطريقة وشربه ... الخ؛ بيان الأفضل، فإن لم يفعل أو
And the divider depicts what he is dividing, levels it, measures it, values its building, and separates each portion with its path and drinking right.

His saying: 'And he depicts...' etc.: i.e., when the divider commences the division, he should depict what he is dividing; i.e., write on his paper: So-and-so's share is such, and So-and-so's such; so that he can preserve it if he wishes to raise that paper to the judge, so that he (the judge) may undertake the drawing of lots among them himself. As in al-'Inayah.

His saying: 'And he depicts...' etc.: Imam Hamid al-Din said: Its example: Land among a group; one has a sixth, another half, another third. He makes it six shares, and labels the first part as the first share, the next as second, and so on. He writes their names and makes them a lot. Whosesoever name emerges first gets the first share. If that share is the one with the sixth-holder, he gets the first part; if the third-holder, he gets the first and second parts; if the half-holder, he gets the first and the two following parts.

So the summary: He draws the lot. Whosesoever name emerges first gets the first share, whosesoever name emerges second gets the second share. This method is only correct if the portions are equal, and the principle is to look at the smallest portion, such that if the smallest is a third, he makes it thirds; if a sixth, sixths; so that division is possible.

Example of the issue: If the heir is a son and a daughter, he writes on the lot her name and calls the third labeled as first with what follows it, and the other third is determined for the daughter. If the daughter's name emerges first, she takes the third labeled as first, and the other two thirds are determined for the son. The lot-drawing was legislated to make hearts content and remove suspicion of bias. As in al-Fawa'id al-'Arifiyyah.

His saying: 'and values its building' etc.: because of his need for it in the end, since the building is divided separately, so perhaps something of it falls into the share of one of them, so that he may know its value. Al-Jalabi said it.

His saying: 'and separates each portion with its path and drinking right' etc.: Explanation of the best practice. If he does not do it or
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Sharh al-Wiqayah, explaining the duties of the divider (qasim) when partitioning jointly owned property. The matn commands the qasim to perform five actions: depict (yusawwir), level (yaddil), measure (yadra'u), value (yuqawwimu), and separate (yufrizu). The commentary then elaborates on each. Depicting means writing down the shares on a paper so that the judge can supervise the lot-drawing. The example involving three shares (1/6, 1/2, 1/3) is given to show how to create a lot with six shares labeled first, second, etc., and assign them based on the drawer. The method assumes equal shares but adjusts by using the smallest unit as base (e.g., thirds or sixths). A second example involves a son and daughter: the lot determines who gets first choice, with the shares adjusted accordingly. The commentary emphasizes that lot-drawing (qur'a) is meant to ensure fairness and remove bias. The valuation of buildings is necessary because buildings are divided separately from land. The separation of portions includes specifying pathways and drinking rights. The phrase 'فإن لم يفعل أو' is cut off, likely indicating that the best practice is to separate even if not specified. This passage is a procedural guide for Hanafi judges and dividers, with no explicit evidence cited, as it is a practical ruling based on custom and avoidance of dispute.

Key Terms
القاسم (al-qasim): The person appointed to divide jointly owned property among co-owners.
التصوير (al-taswir): Writing down the shares and their owners on paper for the judge's reference.
التعديل (al-ta'dil): Equalizing the shares so that each portion is equivalent in value and utility.
التذريع (al-tazri'): Measuring the property in cubits (dhirā') to determine exact dimensions.
التقويم (al-taqwim): Valuing the buildings and improvements separately from the land.
الإفراز (al-ifraz): Physically separating the portions and allocating each to its owner.
القرعة (al-qur'a): Drawing lots to assign shares impartially.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi divider must perform five documented steps culminating in lot-drawing to ensure a fair and indisputable partition.

beginner
6 min
Procedure for Labeling Shares and Drawing Lots
Procedural ruling with commentary expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the Hanafi method of labeling portions and drawing lots for property division, and explain why this procedure is recommended.

Core Idea
Lot-drawing with sequential labeling ensures impartial and orderly distribution of property shares.

Dividing jointly owned property can lead to disputes if not done fairly. The Hanafi school prescribes a detailed method of labeling shares as first, second, third, etc., and using lots to assign them. This procedure is recommended (الأفضل) to prevent contention. In this lesson, we examine the commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, which expands on the basic matn to show the practical steps: drawing the property on a paper, measuring with a cubit grid, and sequentially assigning shares based on the lot order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal ruling
The matn states: labels shares as first/second/third, writes names, draws lots, and assigns accordingly. The legal status of this procedure is recommended (sunnah muakkadah), not obligatory for validity.
2
Understand the preparatory steps
The commentary explains that the divider draws the house on a paper, levels the shares, measures cubits with a grid, and values the building. This ensures an accurate representation for the judge and impartial division.
3
Trace the lot assignment sequence
The divider chooses a starting side, labels portions in order (first, second, third). After drawing lots, the first drawer gets the starting portion, the second gets the adjacent, and so on. This works for both equal and unequal shares.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
The method ensures contiguity (تتصل) and impartiality (القرعة removes bias). The order of portions is fixed before the draw, so the result is fair and prevents disputes.
5
Apply the ruling to a simple case
Suppose three partners own a long strip of land. Label westernmost as first, middle as second, eastern as third. After drawing lots, the first drawer gets the western third, second gets middle, third gets eastern. This is the Hanafi method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلقب الأقسام بالأول والثاني والثالث، ويكتب أسماءهم ويقرع، والأول لمن خرج اسمه أولاً، والثاني لمن خرج ثانياً: أي يُصَوِّرُ الدَّارَ المقسومة على قرطاس؛ ليرفع إلى القاضي، ويعدلها : أي يسويها على سهام القسمة، ويذرعُها ويُصَوِّرُ الدرعان على ذلك القرطاس بقلم الجدول، فيكون كل ذراع في ذراع بشكل لبنة ، ويُقَدِّرُ البيوت والصَّفْةَ ، وغيرهما بتلك الدرعان، ويقوم البناءً ويبتدئ القسمة من أي طرف شاء، فإن جَعَلَ الجانب الغربي أولاً يجعل ما يليه ثانياً، ثمّ ما يليه ثالثاً وهكذا، ويكتب أسماء أصحاب السهام إما على القرعة أو غيرها، فمن خرج اسمُهُ أولاً يعطى نصيبه من الجانب الغربي جملة من العرصة والبناء إلى أن يتم نصيبُهُ ، ثُمَّ مَن خَرَجَ اسمه ثانيا يعطى نصيبه متصلاً بالأول، وهكذا إلى أن يتم سواء كانت الأنصباء متساوية أو متفاوتة.
And he labels the portions as first, second, and third, and writes their names and draws lots, and the first is for whosesoever name emerges first, and the second for whosesoever emerges second: i.e., he draws the house being divided on a paper, to be raised to the judge, and levels it: i.e., makes it equal according to the shares of division, and measures it and draws the measurements on that paper with a column-like pen, such that every cubit by cubit is in the shape of a brick. And he estimates the rooms and the portico and others by those measurements, and values the building, and begins the division from whichever side he wishes. If he makes the western side first, he makes what is next to it second, then what is next to it third, and so on. And he writes the names of the owners of the shares either on the lot or otherwise. Whosesoever name emerges first is given his share from the western side, both courtyard and building, until his share is completed. Then whosesoever name emerges second is given his share adjacent to the first, and so on until completion, whether the shares are equal or unequal.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that elaborates on the procedure for labeling portions and drawing lots. The matn states that the divider labels the portions as first, second, third, etc., writes the names of the owners, and draws lots. The first drawer gets the first portion, the second gets the second, and so on. The commentary provides a detailed example: the divider draws the entire house on a paper, levels the shares, measures each in cubits (depicting the measurements on the paper with a grid), values the building, and then begins assigning share by starting from a chosen side (e.g., western). He labels the westernmost portion as first, the next as second, etc. After writing names on the lots, the first drawer receives the western portion (both land and building) until his share is filled, then the second drawer receives the next adjacent portion, and so on. This method works for both equal and unequal shares. The key principle is that the assignment follows the order of the lot results, maintaining contiguity. The purpose is to ensure impartiality and orderly distribution.

Key Terms
القرعة (al-qur'a): A physical lot (e.g., a ball of clay with a name) used to randomly assign portions.
القرطاس (al-qirtas): A paper on which the divider draws the property and its measurements.
الدرعان (al-dhirā'): Cubit measurements drawn as a grid on the paper for precise dimension representation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method of labeling portions sequentially and drawing lots ensures impartial and orderly distribution of property, whether shares are equal or unequal.
